BEFORE THE MIRROR
Freckles and How to Cure Them. —It is always
those with the finest and fairiest skins who
freckle most easily, but this knowledge is poor
comfort when the face is disfigured with these
ugly little blemishes. To, prevent the forma-
tion of freckles a wash should be applied to
the face which will form a shield from the
rays of the sun. A simple and inexpensive one
can be made up at home by dissolving an
ounce of cleminite in four tablespoonfuls of
hot water. Shake the bottle, and when cold
apply to the face and allow to dry on the
skin. If this is done every day, renewing the
application whenever the face is washed, and
a little mercolised wax rubbed into the skin
every night, freckles will not make their
appearance, no matter how hot the sun. To
remove freckles which have already formed,
apply the wax every night, rub it well into
the skin of the face and neck and leave it on
till the morning. Then wash off, using Pilenta
soap and warm water.
To Reduce a Double Chin.—To reduce a double
chin, give hard, firm massage every night with
mercolised wax, stroking the flesh firmly back-
wards from the chin towards the lower por-
tion of the neck. Do this every night, and in
the morning bathe with cold water and rub
into the skin a little parsidium jelly. _ This
treatment will strengthen the flabby tissues
and restore the contour of the face. Occasion-
ally a double chin is due to stooping over
books or work, but generally it is the result
of putting on weight. When the latter is the
ease a few clynol berries, eaten regularly every
day, will greatly assist in reducing the super-
By “JEANNETTE.”
These are, undoubtedly, days of exceptional nervous strain for one and
all of us Anxiety, with its beauty-destroying influence, is casting its shadow
over the lives of many of us women, and while perhaps a few —the more
stoical among us—can avoid worrying over past or prospective events, we
mav all with a little care and forethought, succeed m preventing the result
of worrv from being noticeable in our appearance. To these fortunate
few this little chat will, however, be as interesting and instructive as to
those who stand in real need of help in toilet matters, and may be the
means of bringing to their notice some hitherto unknown method of retain-
ing or regaining that beauty of face and figure which is the birthright of
every woman. Some of the ingredients mentioned below are, at present, not
generally known to the public, but any good chemist will usually be found to
have a small quantity in stock.
I .nous flesh. A glass of hot water should be
Ipped after each meal-
[flic Hair —To Increase the Growth. —To arrest
m undue falling of the hair and increase the
growth, scalp-massage and the application of
. good tonic is absolutely necessary. Part the
lair in the centre, "and. starting at the fore-
lead, massage for at least ten minutes. Then
ipply a tonic. A simple and inexpensive one
•an be made up at home by mixing one ounce
>f boranium (which can be bought at the
diemist’s) with a quarter of a pint of bay
•um. Add the boranium to the bay rum, allow
o stand for half an hour, then strain and add
sufficient water to make half a pint. Da%
imongst the roots of the hair with a soft
sponge.
•Superfluous Hair.—Dark-complexioned women
ire far more frequently troubled with _ a
growth of superfluous hair than those with
fair skins, and at the first sign of these objec-
tionable hairs steps should be taken to remove
;hem, or they will develop into a strong growth
which will take a very long time to destroy.
A little powdered pheminol applied to this
growth will remove the unsightly blemish,
destroying it permanently if the hairs be but
few. Two or more applications may be neces-
sary, if the growth be unusually strong, but
about one ounce of pheminol should be suffici-
ent for the most stubborn case. Pheminol. can
be obtained at the chemist’s, and a little
powdered alum should, be obtained at. the
same time for dusting the skin before applying
the pheminol.
ANSWERS TO CORRESPONDENTS.
TO DARKEN THE EYEBROWS (L.M.).' —
There is no dye which I could recommend as
“safe,” but if you rub a little mennaline into
the eyebrows and at the roots of the eyelashes
regularly every night it will darken them
naturally, and will also increase the growth
of the eyelashes.
TO REDUCE WEIGHT (SADIE).—Yes, your
weight and measurements are certainly more
than they should be for your height, and I
quite agree that being so stout makes you look
old. I am pleased to be able to tell you of a
quick, easy and pleasant way to reduce both.
Get a small quantity of clynol berries from
the chemist’s and take one after each meal,
three times a day. Weigh yourself after one
month’s treatment, and you will be delighted
with the result.
TO KEEP THE PIAIR PAIR (DORIS).—If
you shampoo your hair regularly with stallax
granules it will prevent it from turning dark.
One teaspoon of stallax is sufficient for a
shampoo, and an original packet will make
from 25 to 30 shampoos. Stallax keeps
indefinitely. E 4
TO IMPROVE A DULL, MUDDY COM-
PLEXION (D.B).—Your complexion is dull
and muddy-looking because it needs thorough
cleansing and renewing of the outer cuticle.
To do this get a tin of mereolised wax, and
with the tips of the fingers rub a little gently
into the skin, going over the entire face and
neck. Leave the wax on all night and in the
morning wash it off with a good soap (you
will find Pilenta excellent). In a month s time
you will notice a most surprising improve-
ment. (2) Liquid per&ol will prevent the
excessive perspiration of which you complain.
(3) Nothing can be done.
TO ARREST GREYNESS (FLO).—You are
indeed far too young to go grey yet. Get
about two ounces of concentrate of tamma-
lite and mix it with three ounces of bay rum.
Dab this on the hair and amongst the roots.
It will soon make matters all right.
TO WHITEN A BROWN NECK (W;K.).—To
whiten your neck use .iettaline. Get a tube of
this from the chemist’s and rub a little well
into the skin of the neck; leave it on till next
morning. Do this regularly every night -for
three or four weeks and you will find that the
skin will become beautifully clear and white.

The Red Page
THE UNWRITTEN BOOKS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
By faith I see them, neatly ranged,
Each in its decorated wrapper.
They’ve stood just so, untouched, unchanged,
Some portly tomes and. others dapper,
For many years, and as to-day;
With authors’ egotism bitten,
I’ve gazed on their devices gay,
Ant) wondered at the fine display
Of splendid works I’ve not yet written.
Here stands my volume tall and slim
Of lover verses, “Tears of Acid,”
And here the novel fat and trim
That was to limn the painful, placid
Besotting life of little trade,
A book with bubbling springs of wit in,
A volume blitho of sketches made,
And one of essays strong and staid—
All noble books I have not written.
The books I have not written are
The only ones I’m proud of owning;
Their verses have a virtue far
Above the rhymes’ innocuous droning
You’ll find in printed books of mine,
The wretched things I ne’er admit in
Where polished blackwood forms a shrine.
And, marshalled smartly line on line,
Are cased the books I have not written.
These masterpieces have no flaw
Of paper, type or' illustration ;
Their get-up honors every law
That Morris gave an eager nation.
Content I’ll rest, if when I lie
Some dust-strewn, old, forgotten pit in
Posterity will judge me by
These works expressing all that’s I,
The noble books I have not written!
VICTORIA.
E. DYSON.
Invention and Discovery
For several years past American maga-
zines have been sprinkled with what editors
over there are pleased to call “free verse.”
Thoughts, sometimes good, sometimes com-
monplace, are set out in longer, or shorter
gasps, without rhyme and practically with-
out rhythm. Usually the diction is that of
prose, but here and there is a line or phrase
that surprises and lingers in the memory or
stirs a real emotion. Never does the alleged
poem as a whole capture the imagination or
hold the fancy, for the good and sufficient
reason that its method defies the genius of
the English language and that the author
has entirely mistaken the nature and scope
of verse. Each language has its natural
and individual rhythm—an undersong in its
everyday speech which shapes its true song
into definite form. The head-rhyme, as it is
called, of the Hebrew, the alliterative chant
of the Saxon and the scansion by quantity
of the Latin were all developments of the
character of the language used. Consequently
invention in verse is practically impossible,
while discovery in verse is narrowed only by
the adventurous literary voyages of the past.
The English language is a world in itself.
Capes and islands and continents of song
have been explored, and there may yet be
uncharted lands; but you cannot make a
new island or continent however you may
subdivide or re-arrange the old.
The so-called “free verse” bears no relation
to the undersong of English speech. No
stretch of imagination will. allow us to think
of the natural singer breaking into emotional
expression in this way. Some of the things
printed might stand for the natural music of
barking dogs, for the pulse of the motor-car,
even for the beat of hammers or the run of
the circular-saw; but the human heart and
mind do not express themselves as dogs and
hammers and saws. There is a sweetness of
rhvthm and a musical cadence which has
already found its accepted forms and which
ia all human and all in conformity with the
genius of the language used.
There is nothing arbitrary about our verse
forms. They have grown up naturally. Ex-
plorations of all kinds have been made, and
the thing which was valueless and failed to
win its way into the heart and mind has
been thrown aside. Take the story of Eng-
lish blank verse. We have only one blank
verse line, consisting of ten syllables, alter-
nately accented and unaccented. All kinds of
subtle minor variations are possible owing
to the differences in stress and the diversi-
ties in the length of vowel sounds; and a
lialf-elided syllable here and there may give
strength and freshness to the line. Howard
discovered that blank-verse line in the time
of Henry VIII. No literary law- fixed it as
an arbitrary form, nor did any sort of con-
vention stop succeeding poets from trying-
other roads ; but Howard’s lino still stands.
There is no other blank verse in the language
with the same music and flexibility.
Look into the regular experiments made in
English verse without rhyme and see how
true this is. Longfellow tried in “Evange-
line” and again in “Hiawatha.” The result
was infinite monotony. Tennyson in his
“Boadicea” lifted a noisy chant which
achieved force without much grace and
would have been utterly intolerable if
it had been carried further. In his lines on
Milton the same author scored a triumph in
close, non-rhyming imitation of classic forms;
but the effort was very short and could not
have been sustained. Compare these things
with the elastic wonder of the metre in
which “Paradise Lost,” “Alastor” and “Hy-
perion” were written and it is at once evi-
dent that Howard’s blank verse is something
in keeping with the whole genius of English
speech, and something which, for that very
reason, can never be superseded except by
some yet undiscovered verse form which lias
a still surer relation to the rhythm of our
speech and thought.
Something of the same sort is true of all
our best-tested and most firmly established
forms. They stay because they have been
found the best; and to the singer with some-
thing to say this is not a handicap but a
very great advantage.
Of delicate irregularity within the bounds
of regularity there is a lot that might be
said. Confusion between quantitative scan-
sion and scansion by accent has led to a stiff-
ness which Is own brother to monotony. The
number of beats in the line of a song is more
important than the actual number of sylla-
bles; and on the gliding movement with halt-
elisions down, to weak ending's the most
delicate and fairy-like of all thought-music
may be made. This is matter for the soul,
tlie assertion of the principle of keeping
verse true to its aim does not touch the
question of discord-in-harmony. To accept
the view of American editors that very bad
prose displayed in a particular way by the
printer may be worthy verse would be equiva-
lent to believing that song is dead and that
human thought and feeling no longer move
to the rhythm of the worlds and stars.
D. M. W.
The Very Greatest Bolshevik
Leo Tolstoy, by Aylmer Maude, is an
oft-told story but it is told exceedingly well,
and the tale of the Great Bolshevik is
appropriate just now. Tolstoy lived to over
80 years with increasing whiskers. He was
a powerful novelist of a miserable type; a
story-writer and journalist; a strenuous
educationist; a large-hearted philanthropist
till his creed got him down and brutalised
him; and a most disastrous crank. Inci-
dentally, he was an enormous student.
Russia, his own country, had lived for
a brief space—brief even compared with the
length of the absurd scrap known as written
history—under a bad Government. Tol-
stoy, being utterly devoid of the con-
structive faculty, propounded no scheme of
better government. Rushing to conclusions
at top speed he formulated the doctrine of
no law, no defence, no resistance, no pro-
perty, and complete abasement. The good
were to submit and the wicked were to run
the show. He got, in time, to a point at
which he declared that a ruffian who was
beating a child to death should not be inter-
fered with, because interference implied re-
sistance, force, and therefore some sort of
law. In time lie drew the line even at
legislation for shorter hours, better wages,
improved factory conditions, the establish-
ment of hospitals, care for infant life, etc.,
because it was legislation, and thus
represented law and force. So at last he
became an impossible sort of animal, all
introspection and whiskers.
He never earned his living by manual
toil, but with proud and haughty humble-
ness he occasionally made flagrant boots to
prove the dignity of labor. Thereby he
wasted much time and damaged . a
friend’s feet. In a moment of excite-
ment he gave up meat, salt, tobacco and
tidy clothes, and adopted Henry George’s
doctrines. The burden of George was that
the Government (which Tolstoy said
shouldn’t exist) ought to raise the revenue
(which Tolstoy said shouldn’t exist) by
taxing private land (which Tolstoy said
should-ex*’st even less than the other two).
And he regarded the 12 apostles who
taught instead of working as the real
exemplars, not noticing that if 1,200.000,000
apostles followed in their footsteps the other
1100,000,000 folk would have to work like
the very devil to feed them.
The Great Bolshevik founded a number
of labor colonies to be worked on his
principles, so that the world might see how
to do it. They all burst up in disgust, but
the bull-headed prophet, being incapable of
learning by experience, went on prophesying
just the same. One of his theories w T as that
everybody should give away his property,
which of course there would be no one to
receive, but he died With his worldly goods
in decent repair.
Tolstoy passed out too soon. If lie had
lived eight years longer he would have seen
his policy of negation in full blast and have
smelled the burning. For he preached ruins
and forgot to say what should be built with
the ashes. (Methuen and Co., London.)
A Gallipoli Diary
Major Graham Gillam, D. 5.0., a British
officer in the feeding and munitions branch,
is the writer, and he gives a plain, unvar-
nished account of a pottering campaign
whereof he saw the beginning, the middle
and the end. Being in the supply branch -he
had a permanent address, at first behind a
biscuit-box and later in a dugout, and a
home in which to store his papers. That made
diary-keeping possible. The drawback of a
diary is that it is like a Chinese picture with
no perspective in it. The event which looks
10ft. high when it happens may prove in
the light of later events to be only 6in.
high, or vice versa; but no adjustment can
be made if the volume is to be honest. Thus
if Cortez had been a diarist he might have
recorded that on the day of his historic land-
ing he was bitten by the most fearsome
beast in America, and learned afterwards
that a mosquito was hardly even the begin-
ning of his troubles. On the other hand, a
diary is a photograph and not a fake. It
shows what was done under circumstances
as they were or seemed to be at the moment,
not what should have been done in the light
of what was learned about these circum-
stances afterwards—possibly from docu-
ments found in the enemy’s baggage. Major
Gillam writes as a man who had a full view
of the tiny battle-ground and pervaded the
whole of it with his mule-teams. He sat
on' his biscuit-tin on the shore and watched
his fleet and army at the same moment, like
Xerxes, and he saw ships torpedoed and
other happenings. Strategy was spread out
before him. His story from the first chap-
ter to the last is that the expedition, like
the one that surrendered
was hopelessly undermanned. The position
was bad at first, and as Turkish soldiery and
Ilun submarines poured in it became worse.
A big force, he holds, might have rushed the
position in a day or two. for a single lull
made all the difference. Then it could have
commanded the Dardanelles; bombed the
Turko-I-luns off the Asiatic shore; made an
open road for the fleet to Constantinople,
where the Sultan was already trembling in
his boots: brought the plausible Ferdinand
of Bulgaria in on our side instead of against
us. seeing that the plausible one only wanted
to be on the winning side; and extended a
hand which might have saved Roumania and
prevented the collapse of Russia. The Major
considers the management of the campaign
as good as any hardworking commissariat
officer, with headquarters behind a biscuit-

THE MAN ON THE LAND has OFTEN to
LEAVE the FARM WORK he is doing to RE-
PLENISH the HOUSEHOLD WATER supply. The
ACTUAL operation of PUMPING daily from a
WELL to the HOUSE TANK does not take up a
GREAT DEAL of time, but in the AGGREGATE it
represents HUNDREDS of WASTED HOURS
YEARLY. He should install a CHALLENGE
WINDMILL to do the pumping for him. The TIME
SAVED would be REFLECTED in the MONETARY
RETURN from his farm. The UP-TO-DATE MAN
uses a CHALLENGE WINDMILL and CONTINU-
ALLY SOUNDS its PRAISES.
The CHALLENGE CONSTRUCTION is the
EMBODIMENT of SUPERIOR MECHANICAL
ELEMENTS. BROUGHT TOGETHER for the first
time in ONE UNEQUALLED COMBINATION;
and PRODUCING A WINDMILL of EXCEP-
TIONAL MERIT.
Briefly stated, these elements consist of an
INTERNAL GEAR, CENTRE ONE-PIECE
CRANK. ROCKER ARM, SPLIT BOXES, with
detachable, reversible BABBITT BEARINGS,
interchangeable with graphite, SIDE VANE and
MULTIPLYING SPRING GOVERNOR.
The internal type of gear is recognised by
mechanical engineers as superior to an external gear
in at least two respects. First, twice as many cogs

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At Sunset.
Lord of the azure, flame-arrayed,
A god’s work done,
Ending the life of the day he made
Down sinks the sun.
Peopling the roseate coast of cloud
With dome and spire,
Mansions of wonder stand gleaming and
proud
By capes of fire.
Rising, he summoned the leaves from their
dreams
Of Night, dew-laved ;
Burning all day on the seas and streams
He slew and he saved ;
Now like the crimson casque of Mars,
A glory strange,
Seeking his rest amid the stars
Beyond the range.
Mystic and mighty the wide sea swims
To the darkling east —
Flows, till the sweet of its purple dims
In the Night’s grey mist;
These, since the first, have mocked the sight
Of mortal eyes—
Glory that closes in mist and night,
And beauty that dies;
Perishing pageants, made to die,
And lights that fail;
Even the glowing flowers of the sky,
Like earth’s shall pale.
But, Night forsaking, the sun wheels back
Awaking the sea.
Scattering roses in his track
And lifting the tree.
So, from the endless chaos of strife,
Defeating the gloom,
Drooping superb from the Tree of Life,
New stars shall bloom
1.... but a wind blows down from above,
Two names on its breath—
Peace, more deep than the sea, and Love
That is stronger than Death.
N.s.w.
P. E. QUINN.
The Sleeping Beauty.
She lies upon a gilded couch,
Her round, white arms beneath her head,
And shadowy slaves about her crouch
Mute and immobile as the dead.
Soft azure silks her limbs enfold,
And shimmering gauzes fine as mist,
And milky veils with broidery scrolled
Veil her thick tresses’ heavy twist.
Some glittering dream the day beguiles,
Some sweet thought through the dream-
ing slips,
For joy too delicate for smiles
Haunts the curved flower of her lips;
And shrived she seems from stress and dole,
As one who sees in magic trance
Through the dark forests of the soul
The silver-footed fairies dance.
No voice may break the calm profound
, Nor echo pierce to where she lies
To stir the vision that has bound
The languid glimmer of her eyes:
Nothing, till Eventide shall press
Those lips and break the tranced swoon
That holds in thrall this fair princess—
The golden summer afternoon.
VICTORIA.
SYD. C.
After Four Years.
I wish the blamed thing would end,
An’ let me get back to me job.
I’m sick of ’op overs, an’ ’Uns,
An’ five-nines, an’ trenches, an’ rum;
Of duckwalks, an’ sandbags, an’ mud,
An’ the luridly crimson blank line.
I’m sick o’ rest camps, an’ billets,
An’ madamosells an’ vang blong,
Of cafay nore, fried eggs an’ chips,
An’ lousy, damp straw for me bunk.
I got no use for brasso, or bianco,
Or for drill, or blood-pink rout-marches.
I can’t stand the sight of a leave pass,
Or a flat in little old London ;
Or the resterong down there in Soho,
An’ Susanne who said that she loved me.
I jest want the damned thing to end,
An’ let me get back to me job.
FRANCE.
WILLIAM JAMES DIGGER.
After the Battle.
What had the bloody hands of war for these
Whose bones lie rotting, putrid in the
earth,
Feeding the maggot under grass and trees,
Who should have fed life’s endless line 'of
birth ?
W hat have they had for all their sweating
fears,
Blood poured as water from a sewer vent.
Their anguish and their broken hopes, their
tears,
Their young lives scattered ere their
youth was spent?
Companioned of the things that know no
name,
The rat , the louse, the maggot and the
worm, . - ....
They had the passion of a living flame,
The wind of tempest and the might of
storm:
And had companionship of men, and saw
In one declension everlasting law.
N.S.W.
MARY GILMORE.

TERMS TO SUBSCRIBERS.
(In Advance, Only.)
For £ 1 6s. 6d. per annum you can have The Bulletin
sent regularly to you every week for a year, to any
ADDRESS IN AUSTRALASIA, post paid.
Cheques, drafts, etc., which should be crossed, and
all Business Communications to be addressed to the
Manager.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be
refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practic-
able, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” column
all contributions submitted, whether in the form of
Political, Social or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales
or Sketches (those dealing with Australian subjects,
and not exceeding two columns in length , or , say,
SOOO words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters or Newspaper Clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But
no MS. will be returned or presei'ved unless these
conditions are observed. When MS. is forwarded
without stamps for return it will be understood that
the copy is to be destroyed if not acceptable. Per-
sonal applications for return of MS. cannot receive
attention. The Bulletin desires, further, _ those
sending MS. to distinctly Understand that it is not
responsible for the preservation or transmission of MS.
To Amateurs in Black and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any picture
which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, JANUARY 23, 1939.
THE GREAT INSANITY.
In the last few days several Federal and
State politicians and a number of Trades
Hall officials have stated, with the stolid
calm of the well-fed ox, that Bolshevism
is being “organised” in Australia. It is
like chatting placidly about organising an
epidemic of cholera. Australia has been
as remote from Bolshevism, so far, as
it has been from war; it has not
come in contact with the bitterest sufferers
from either horror. It is able, therefore,
to view them in an oddly detached, im-
personal fashion. But people in Europe
have lived cheek-by-jowl with both, and
they cannot talk so calmly about either.
* * * *
What is this Bolshevism which is being
“organised” here? It is no new manifesta-
tion. The world has always known it in
one form or another, and always it has re-
presented a reaction against conditions
which were not only intolerable, but
also incapable of being altered ex-
cept by force. France became Bolshevik
under the Bourbons for the same reason
that Russia did under the Romanoffs.
There was no other way. But what reform
does Bolshevism offer a country like Aus-
tralia that cannot be achieved without the
shedding of a drop of blood or the destruc-
tion of one atom of the community’s wealth?
If Australia is tired of the British con-
nection it only needs to say so: it will not
have to fight to get free of it. If it is
weary of imported Governors, the whole
band can be speedily sent back to England,
in a constitutional manner, and local poli-
ticians or aldermen or even Trades Hall
officials substituted for them. If Australia
wants disarmament it can have it on the
same lines as it achieved the C.M.F. and
the Navy. The six-hour day and profit-
sharing would have been instituted long ago,
in many industries here, if the union rules
and the big union bosses had been agreeable.
\n even shorter working day and a re-
adjustment of the wage standard giving em-
ployees the whole profits of the concerns in
which thev work are practicable politically,
even if they are economically impractical.
It would only be necessary under our form
of o-overnment for a majority of the electors
to 'decide that the experiment was worth
trying What lunacy for a community to
organise death and devastation to get some-
thing which is to be had for the asking!
* * * *
Bolshevism invariably runs on the same
lines. In Australia —after a distracted herd
of Governors had fled or been destroyed, and
after the various Parliament Houses had
been closed and put in a state of defence—
the Government would pass into the hands of
someone with a name like Schmidt or
Maekiewitz —a person with a genius for
grievance and recriminatory speech, but with
no practical experience in directing so much
as a fowl-run. Bolshevism always starts in
the great cities —the places where there is
the biggest amount of portable and drink-
able property. In the early stages of every
previous Bolshevist outbreak —in Paris in
the beginning and middle of last century, and
recently in Petrograd and Berlin —the lead-
ers have done well for themselves. They
have inhabited palaces, bathed in vintage
wines and collected and secreted money and
valuables. It would be no different in (say)
Sydney. Schmidt and friends would put
ud at Government House or the Union Club
and get to work on the business of uplifting
humanity. The uplifting always takes the
same form—the erection of barricades and the
establishment of shooting posts. The
luckier Comrades —that is, those reformers
who were handy with revolvers and bombs,
quick at dodging Lewis-gun bursts, and
whose activities happened to be in the neigh-
borhood of the banks and jewellers’ shops—
would then begin to reap their reward. It
was noticed in Paris during the Commune
that the Comrades tended always to gravitate
towards the Rue de la Paix: in fact, long
after it had been cleaned out they continued
to pour in from the Rue de Rivoli on one
side and the Avenue de I’Opera on the other.
The next stage is one of disillusionment. The
discovery is made that very little property is
worth anything in the absence of settled gov-
ernment. Wardrobes, carpets, tables, arm-
chairs, stoves, mantelpieces, dinner services,
sofas—they are all useful, but they are not
easy things to personally carry through agi-
tated streets. Looting is only a payable
proposition really in war-time, and even then
it is limited to a small class. The Crown
Prince got away with a lot of stuff, for
example, because he had a supply of G.S.
waggons and lorries to carry the goods, and
an army to guard and deliver ‘ them. All
through history the majority of Revolution-
aries—Communists, Bolshevists, or whatever
they have happened to call themselves —have
had the same experience. They have burst
into premises at great risk, and emerged
either with nothing of value or with some-
thing that they have not been able to carry
for 50 yards. The Russians made the dis-
covery a year ago when they surged along the
Nevski Prospekt and into the Grand Baza.ar;
the French found it out in 1798 and again in
1871. The French burned their Treasury
Building, the Town Hall, the Docks and the
Palais Royal, by way of protest; the Rus-
sians did the same with their public build-
ings and they are still at it. The Berliners
have begun. Indiscriminate incendiarism is
one of the appanages of Bolshevism.
* * * *
Still, Bolshevism in the business quarters
of cities has at first the charm of thrill and
novelty. The fighting rages from street to
street. Epic deeds are done. Reputations
are made and ruined. The joy of battle
acts, as always, as an antidote to the sur-
rounding ruin and carnage. But away in
the suburbs and the country are the less spec-
tacular sufferers —perhaps 75 per cent, of
the community. They are mostly people who
at first took no interest in the new move-
ment; and who, later, told themselves that,
anyhow, nothing wmild induce them to join
in the fighting. They quickly find their
error. Bolshevism having been successfully
launched, and Schmidt having established
himself more or less, the suburbanites dis-
cover that the first effect of the coming re-
generation of mankind, so far as they are
concerned, is the abolition of their jobs. They
remain at home. The trams and trains that
once brought them to the city have other uses ;
they are sand-bagged and piled up at street
corners. The power-houses are full of
machine-guns. The gasworks are out of
action and the water-mains have been blown
up. For the first time the outlying citizen
is made to realise the part that transport
plays in modern life. It might be possible
to deliver necessaries like bread, meat and
milk in armored cars or tanks, but there are
none available, and, anyhow, the organisa-
tion is not there. Starvation begins. It
is at this stage that the suburbanite seizes
the nearest lethal weapon, and, leaving
famine and pestilence in his home, plunges
into civil war on whichever side his fancy
directs. Schmidt is assassinated and his
place is taken by Johnson. The struggle
to uplift humanity continues. In 1871
35,000 Parisians—men, women and children
—were shot in the streets, and as many more
died of preventable sickness. No official
estimate has been made of the slaughter in
Russia in the past 12 months, but it is
known to run into hundreds of thousands.
The fully-roused Australian is as warlike as
any man. He could be confidently relied
on, in the event of Bolshevism being “organ-
ised’l here, to establish a casualty list that
would impress humanity. Meanwhile the
following—from a recent cable—is the sort
of thing that would be happening:—
The revolt of the peasantry continues, though it
is ruthlessly suppressed. In despair the peasants
are destroying the railroads, thus stopping food
transport. The situation in Northern and Central
Russia threatens general starvation.
Justice in the law courts does not exist for
the most part. Youngsters of 18 have replaced
dismissed magistrates. People are frequently shot
by their guards while being taken from one prison
to another. The population is starving upon an
ounce to four ounces of black bread and a little
fish daily.
The situation in the provinces is worse than
in Petrograd and Moscow.
* * * *
A time arrives in all these struggles to
regenerate mankind when people begin to
stop and ask if it is worth while. It is
then that Lenin or Liebkneciit or John-
son or whoever is in charge at the moment is
advised to grab his bag and run. It is a
sign that the jag is over, that civilisation
is about to get back to the social conditions
which. it has taken 5000 years to evolve.
The citizens hanker more and more for the
old peaceful life —for streets that are not
swept by cannon and rifle-fire, for shops
that have not to be guarded like fortresses,
for the punishment of murderers, robbers
and ravishers; for real justice as opposed to
the sort that is dispensed by Whitechapel
Jews and boys of 18; and, above all, for
food, fuel, water and some sort of organisa-
tion to deal with disease. A desperate
struggle begins to regain the old landmarks.
Through shame, misery and sickness the
goal is reached. It always has been and it
always must be so. In Australia, as the
community started attending to its wounds,
buryinv its dead, and repairing its property,
there would be the additionally bitter reflec-
tion that any permanent reform that had
emerged from it all could have been got
without killing .anybody, without starving
anybody, without rendering anybody home-
less.
WATT’S “POLICY” AND ITS “DIRECT
RESULT.”
During the past few months, or few years,
something new has crawled into Australian
politics. For instance, announcements like
this appear quite casually in the press:
Mr. Watt stated to-day that, as the direct result
of the policy of the Government in the encourage-
ment of new industries, he expected shortly to be
able to announce the establishment of tin-plate
manufacture in the Commonwealth... .Though Mr.
Watt would make no statement as .to who would
be the manufacturers or where the industry would
be started, it is understood that the Broken Hill
Proprietary Co., Limited....
So it is suggested, as a virtue, that, as
the direct result of some sort of Govern-
ment action or negotiation, a rich co.
is to start the manufacture of tin-plates,
doubtless on a profitable basis, while smaller
and less influential people are not. There
are quite a multitude of industries, such
as iron and tin and shipbuilding and
certain branches of machinery making, to
mention only a few large specimens, that
the wretched free list seems to make im-
possible in this high-wage and short-hour
country for people w T ho aren’t in com-
munication with the Cabinet, and the mass
of the people ought to know more than
they do about the nature of that com-
munication. What is the process of getting
at the Ministerial ear, and where is the
ear, and do certain parties go to the ear
or does the ear go to them? When the
Government policy of encouraging, say, the
tin-plate industry is going to be put in
motion it is a pity that the nature of
the motion isn’t advertised for the benefit
of all and sundry, instead of there being
a hole-and-corner confabulation with some
firm the very name of which is too sacred
to be officially revealed, though its identity
seems to be an open secret. It is certainly
the business of the Government to en-
courage new industries, but not to
encourage any one rich firm’s industry in
particular. This “injurious differentia-
tion,” as Bruce Smith used to say in
his best days, is apt to arouse angry com-
ment. Some recent transactions with soli-
tary firms even roused an Auditor-General
to angry comment, though there is. no
milder man on earth than an Auditor-
General.
* * * *
A stout Protectionist tariff is open, hon-
est, unbiased encouragement to new indus-
tries. Everybody who cares to go into a
certain line of business gets the same meas-
ure of support. The new firm or company
or co-operative society or solitary industrial
adventurer who isn’t started yet, and so can’t
approach any official personage, is offered the
same terms as the old, full-grown concern
which is worth millions and operates in
four States. The Watt policy, on the other
hand, only offers a chance to the big, rich con-
cern already in existence, for it is impos-
sible to ring up the unborn on the telephone.
In other words, it is necessarily a process
of greasing the most affluent hog, and it tends
to make a few big affairs bigger and give
them the aspect of a monopoly. The Pro-
tectionist tariff encourages competition. It
doesn’t lead to industrial men wearing out
the stairs of Government offices on their
way to see the Ministerial ear about
the establishment of a tin-plate or steel-
plate factory. The man to whom every step
on every official stair is more familiar than
a brother is no more advantaged than the
one who was never on an official stair in
his life. Proximity to the place where official
consideration is located ceases to count.
The mute, inglorious Milton of toil, 700
miles away, has the same chance as the one
next door. There is no need for any darkly-
reticent portfolio-holder to practise conceal-
ment about the name of the man who pro-
poses to open a job and employ Australian
union labor at union wages. It isn’t a sort
of hidden crime like Pearce’s departure for
England, or a secret treaty such as Bis-
marck used to make. Nor is the establish-
ment of the job a lordly gift handed down
to the common herd as an evidence that
Wattsisname, the indefatigable states-
man, or Thingumbob, the unsleeping poli-
tician, has been perspiring brains in the
public interest and deserves a monument.
If in the Government’s thirst for per-
sonal interference the making of books ever
becomes a matter of State, like the compil-
ing of tin-plates, the effect of the Watt
poficy will be more apparent but not more
ridiculous. When it is decided that a book
is wanted the job will naturally be assigned
to someone who has made books and so is
already in the business, he being a sort of
Broken Hill Proprietary Author. Limited,
and known to the men in office. So no new
writer will start unless he lives close enough
to some Minister to state his case, and a
remarkable monopoly will set in.
* * * *
This country is, or ought to be. in some
haste about new industries. It wants
thousands of them, most of the thousands
being affairs that are small in themselves
though ■ Ti >antic in the aggregate. It wants
tags for bootlaces, bungs, frames for leather
bags, buttons, buckles, needles, pins, slates,
umbrellas, blotting paper, paper bags, door
knobs and knockers, and countless articles
that Watt doesn’t know of and which he
wouldn’t have time to attend to even if he
lived to the age of Noaii at the Flood, and
which his elephantine friend, the Broken
Hill Proprietary Co., would scoff at. The
rage of that institution, if requested to make
buttons and paper bags and blotting paper,
can be imagined. Yet the lack of a multi-
tude of small industries not only drains the
country of money, and creates an unemployed
class which has to be supported out of loan
moneys, but it cripples big industries for
which the small jobs should supply raw
material. The occasional founding with
great sweat and mystery of one rich-men’s
factory—if it really founds, which doesn’t
always happen —as a direct result of the
policy of the Government is poor compensa-
tion "for the loss of multitudes of factories
which should belong to men who are not rich.
The real policy of the Government seems to
be to discourage all industries except those
that can be personally meddled in and
muddled in by Ministers. Their direct in-
terference is supposed, in very isolated cases,
to counterbalance the lack of a tariff; but
the people can’t live on isolated cases, all
planted at one small corner of a great con-
tinent and within telephone-speech of the
flappy thing that the official wears on the
side of his head.
THE GILDED AGE.
The deceased year was a boom period for
the property-jobbing interest in Sydney. It
was so also as regards Melbourne, but Syd-
ney, as the biggest city and the capital of
the State with the biggest population, led
the way. Judging by the reports of eight
or nine big firms, and allowing for the mul-
titude of small firms and for private sales,
it appears that £6,000,000 worth of city
and suburban property changed hands. This
was a large increase on the figures for 1917,
while they, in turn, were double those of
1916. No land was created during the year,
the creation of land being a lost industry,
except for small reclamation jobs in those
parts of the earth where volcanic action
has simmered down. And the value of the
new buildings created was only equal to about
half the useless trading done. It wasn’t a
time when much new property came into
existence; in fact, the building industry was
miserably flat. But it was no end of a
time for passing the old property from
hand to hand, which is a simple, brainless
occupation, teaching nothing and leading to
nothing. Prices were unusually good; the
fact that new structures were scarce put'
a fancy value on old shacks. The financing
of these big transactions wasn’t very diffi-
cult. Part of the purchase money was paid
in Government paper money, good only in
Australia, and the rest by signing p.n.’s
and mortgages on imported stationery,
which made a sort of new currency of a
sort, also good only in Australia. The com-
mission that Bacon, the dealer, made by
transferring Ham’s old tenement and bit of
dust to Pokk, and the money which finan-
cial institutions and solicitors made by dis-
counting Pokk’s promises to pay or by draw-
ing up conveyances and mortgages between
him and Bacon aud Ham, amounted to a
sort of national prosperity in itself.
* * * *
This is the Gilded Age in Australia. The
country is full of currency of one sort and
another, and of new securities that are so
handy that they pass from Smith to Jones
almost as currency. There is ordinary
paper money of which nearly £60,000,000
has now been issued. In proportion to
population Australia got far. ahead of
Britain for its issue of paper money. Wheat
certificates are another form of artificial
affluence, the amount of which is uncertain.
They are based on the inability of the Gov-
ernfnent to get the wheat crops to market,
and have the deadlock as a sort of special
security. Something like £5,000,000 worth
of War Savings Certificates are almost as
handy as the practically-extinct sovereign.
The War Loans, which, being mostly sub-
scribed in paper money that was only good
in Australia, had to be mostly spent or
hoarded in Australia, added something like
£150,000,000 to the paper exuberance of
the country. (The cost of the armies at
the Front, being a liability that couldn’t
be met with local paper, was met in part
by accommodation to the amount of about
£90,000.000 from the British Government.)
Money for local purposes was never so abun-
dant, or half so abundant, as during the
Gilded Age of the war. In fact, it was
the first time Australia possessed a huge cur-
rency which had to be used for local pur-
poses, because it was of no value for other
purposes.
* * * *
There arose a natural tendency to do some-
thing with all this money which couldn’t get
away. The corrupting influences of idle
weaith, even if it is only printed or rag
wealth, are proverbial. It is the kind of
thing that leads the sons of millionaire
fathers to fast women and slow horses and
marked cards and cocaine orgies and shat-
tered constitutions and premature burials.
The clumsy incapables of the Australian
Government supplied the riches by manufac-
turing, during the brief term of the war, so
much stuff of one kind and another that the
banks had three times as much “cash’' re-
serves in 1918 as in 1901. The
“cash” had accumulated on their bands
and the heap threatened to topple over. The
Government also sunplied the idleness. B.v
its War-time Profits Tax, its refusal to supply
any extra Protection to counterbalance the
constant increase of wages arising from its
paper boom, and by a multitude of war pre-
cautions, all of them prohibiting something,
it was brilliantly successful in preventing
the establishment of new industries. Aus-
tralia was craving for hundreds of things
.that it couldn’t get, but if anybody was
found attempting to sunpl.v them he was
smitten with a club. Building was placed
under severe restrictions, at the same time
that there were bitter complaints about
house-famine. Ship construction, measured
by the tonnage completed, not by th<u
which was being put together in the
imagination, was little more than one-
hundredth part in 1916 of what it
had been in 1902. A new shipbuilding en-
terprise at Newcastle was warned to stop.
Hotel bars were closed early to restrict the


local manufacture of grog. 1 This infer-
ence is drawn from the fact that the tariff
was readjusted in favor of imported grog, so
that it might not suffer by early closing.)
There was no slave market where superfluous
paper money should be spent on the purchase
of girls; no Monte Carlo, no legalised cock-
fighting, dog-fighting or two-up, and less than
the usual amount of horse-racing. Every
effort was made to keep down ordinary work
or ordinary dissipation, except the land-
gambling variety. In 1918 the pile of avail-
able lOU riches was at the highest, and the
chances of using it in useful industry were
at the lowest; so people devoted themselves
with unusual energy to buying and selling
dust and chances of unearned increment.
Failing that, they might have gone to bed
and stayed there in disgust.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Wanted—Some Strong Men.
Unless the Government wants a series of
sordid troopship rows it will pay particular
attention to the type of “C.O. troops”
selected for duty in the coming months. In
the past the most prevalent classes, both out
and home, have been: —
(--) “Non-starters”—elderly and otherwise.
(2) Ex-Imperial and Home Service Army officers.
(3) A.I.F. “duds.”
The “non-starter” was in a false position
while the war was on; now that it is over
(if it is over) and lie can never see active
service, his last justification as a commander
of expeditionaries is removed. A number of
ex-imperial or Home Service officers are
also understood to be now available —-more
or less young men who, for whatever reason,
have take.n no part as combatants in this
war. It is no reflection on them personally
to remark that at this stage of the proceed-
ings their services are undesirable. The
men do not understand them, and, as a rule,
they cannot understand the men. Almost
equally undesirable is the A.I.F. field-officer
who has managed to get and hold his rank
without acquiring the liking or respect of
those under him. His besetting trouble is
usually weakness —or a weakness. He makes
a deplorable C.O. troops. The average offi-
cer who is angling for this sort of job at the
present time is under the delusion that it
is the soft one it used to be; and the authori-
ties have always looked on troopship com-
mands as duties that can be safely given
to any fool or weakling. Both views are
wrong. The submarine menace may be over,
but no expeditionary is as easy to bring back
as he was to take away. The C.O. of the
future will have to do a lot more than hold
a daily inspection and spend most of the
rest of his time in the best cabin on the ship
if he is to prevent friction. As regards the
other side of the question, it is notorious that
the Government lost hundreds of thousands
of pounds, directly and indirectly, by putting
weak C.O.’s on transports. The typical
ship’s master is not a weak man —far from
it. Part of his job is to do the best he can
for the company that employs him. Here is
the way one of them did it on one troopship.
There were 1500 men aboard and the con-
tract provided that they should get 2oz.
cheese and 2oz. jam per man per day. In-
stead, the cheese was supplied one day and
the jam the next. The contract provided
for 3oz. sugar per man per day; actually
the men got 2oz. Meat for tea was in the
contract: the men got no meat at this meal.
Each day not less than 80 men, who should
have been drilling, were secured by the
skipper for “necessary ship’s duties.” For
some time from 20 to 30 of them were em-
ployed painting the ship ! The explanation
was that the captain of the ship, a masterful
Scot, had the C.O. well bluffed. A first
reouisite on all these voyages is that the
C.O. should be at least as strong a man as
the shipmaster. It is equally essential that
he should be the master, and, incidentally,
the friend, of the men. Australians are
the most docile troops in the world—so long
as they ‘are under a C.O. whom they know
and respect. There are plenty of first-rate
men available on this side, and there are
hundreds in France and England. It would
pay the Government handsomely to detail
so many picked field-officers to do nothing
but command troops on transports till the
demobilisation of the A.I.F. is finished. It
is an expert’s job if ever there was one.
Mr. President.
President Woodrow Wilson of the United
States belongs to a political class which
includes, or has included, such widely
diverse specimens as Stead of the Review
of Reviews (also of the spook Julia) ;
Dr. Arthur, M.L.A., of Sydney; St. Louis
the first ( not the second) ; Mrs. Tanquekay :
Pecksniff as he would have been if he
had been what Tom Pinch thought he was:
Gladstone; and the Sermon on the Mount.
This is the antithesis of the class which
included Henry VIII., Henry Parkes and
Henry of Navarre. Wilson belongs to
the Early Perpendicular or Long aind
Narrow school of architecture. He suggests
a marble tower —firm, erect, spotless,
and with no stomach worth speaking of:
that is to say, the kind of stomach that
implies joviality, not the mere bag you
put victuals in. He is precise in his speech
and his collar and square-toed in his boots.
His conscience is the biggest article in his
luggage. When be defines a thing he uses
very many words so as to leave nothing
out, and in his anxiety to be fair and not
violent he uses them very indefinitely; con-
sequently his famous sketch of the terms
of peace ran to 14 Articles, and has been a
blessing to the Hun and a nuisance to the
Allies. The Articles mean almost anything,
but the general tendency is that we
shouldn't be hard on a defeated enemy, or
hit a boa-constrictor when he is down, keep-
ing in mind that he may be a married boa-
constrictor with a large family. The Presi-
dent was an unsuccessful lawyer even in
a quiet backwash sort of city. This was
not because he lacked parts—he is, indeed,
nil of them—but because the successful
lawyer must be aggressive and one-sided
and not a man of peace. But he was an
ideal Professor of Jurisprudence, which is
very different from being a Professor in a
Military Academy, as Focii used to be.
Among other things Wilson is an elder,
a teetotaller, a philanthropist, an opponent
of bigamy and capital punishment, an early
riser, takes cold baths, and has a leaning
towards vegetarianism. He was never out
of his own country until his present trip
to Europe, and during that trip he avoided
the poker-school in the smoking-room.
Naturally he has never seen war, and his
ideas of it were based on the rather gentle-
manly strife of the U.S. civil disturbance
in which there were no atrocities worth
speaking of, save those which Wirz, the
German Confederate, who was in charge of
Andersonville Prison, and the Huns he
gathered round him, inflicted on the Federal
prisoners of war. There was no indemnity
when the row was over. Wirz was the
only man executed; Jefferson Davis, the
boss rebel, was merely disqualified as an
elector, and everybody shook hands, more
or less. With that idea before him, and
not knowing how it feels to be a citizen of
a small, weak country living under the
shadow of militarism, Wilson never got the
Allied point of view. In his desire to be
absolutely just he is apt to lean towards
the plausible person who spoke last, so
the Huns tried hard to secure him as a
guest that they might breathe in his ear.
If they had got him they would probably
have dressed up two parsons as Hinden-
burg and Ludendorff to show him how
mild ( those individuals really are. He
hasn t one redeeming vice and his respect-
ability beggars description.
The Man Who Has the Misfortune to Be
Pearce.
Having made up to climb into the
limelight in London, Senator Pearce laid
low and remarked nothing until the time
was ripe for his arrival in the Big Smoke -
not in the stress of work, but in the days of
jubilee. Prior to the war the good-natured,
well-meaning tradesman had throughout his
ministry enjoyed the patronage of the brass-
hats of the Defence Department. Then came
the thunder of the drum, and, utterly at sea, he
left himself more than ever in the hands of
his Department. It would be an idle hope
that in such a turmoil as the world has
passed through any man born of woman
would not make mistakes. They were in-
evitable. Rut even mistakes may be par-
doned. They may indeed be magnificent.
The mistakes of Australia’s 'Defence Depart-
ment, for which Senator Pearce was respon-
sible, never rose above the drably calamitous.
Yet, in spite of all that, the man who has
the misfortune to be Pearce has booked
his passage to Europe to “take charge,” as
he pleads, “of demobilisation and repatria-
tion arrangements.” Everybody knows that
General Mo NASH is doing that job, and
everybody knows, too, that he will go on
doing it even after Pearce has reached
England. The outcry on all sides against
the Defence Minister’s going to England in
such circumstances demanded one of two
things: abandonment of the programme or
its justification that the outcry is unjustified.
Instead, he merely puts forward to the Re-
turned Soldiers’ League a wretched ad miseri-
cordiam appeal, something in this shape:—
(1) During the war he had in the course of his
duty to his country to do many things which had
earned him enmity and dislike from those affected.
(2) It had been his duty to intern over 4000
enemy aliens in order to protect Australia and pre-
vent interference with the soldiers of the Empire.
(3) He also had to deal with disloyal elements in
the community, and by checking their nefarious
actions he had earned the lasting enmity of that
section.
(4) During the war he had deemed it his duty
to put his country and Empire first and foremost,
and when the political party with which he was asso-
ciated found that it could not prevent him doing
so, that party had expelled him.
(5) There were in Australia ultra-conservatives
who could not forget that he was a National Labor
man, who also refused to put their country before
their party, and who throughout the war, through
their press organs and elsewhere, had done their
best to harass him.
(6) It would be bitter irony if all these were suc-
cessful in using the soldiers, who had fought for
the Empire and for this country, as their weapon to
accomplish their ends.
These pitiable “points,” because they have no
bearing on the subject in hand, need no
answer. If there was anything to be said in
defence of Pearce’s trip, Acting Prime
Minister Watt would have spoken. But
Watt, Acting Prime Minister, is as silent as
the day after to-morrow. Wherefore Defence
Minister Pearce leaves Australia for the
places of the mighty with a dead cat thrown
after him for good luck.
r ♦ —•
Webster’s “Muddle” Department and
the Censorship.
“W.P.”: On Friday, November 8, three pri-
vate telegrams were lodged at Sydney (two
at the G.P.O. and one at Central Railway)
for country offices, and accepted without
comment. Two of them contained words to
the effect that special newspaper editions
reported that the armistice had been signed,
and fighting had ceased. Two weeks later,
the telegrams not having arrived at their
destinations, an inquiry was forwarded to
the Department, and a reply received, say-
ing the non-delivery was due to circum-
stances over which that Department had
no control. On December 24 the following
circular arrived (the form suggested that
many similar to this had been sent out) :
With reference to your communication of Bth
inst., complaining of non-delivery of a telegram
dated Bth ult., addressed , , I have to inti-
mate that the non-delivery was due to circum-
stances over which this Department had no control.
In this connection, I may state that proclamation
by the Governor-General, published in the Common-
wealth of Australia Gazette of 3rd August, 1914,
provides that cablegrams, etc., are accepted at the
sender s risk, and subject to censorship by the
Commonwealth authorities; that is, they may be
stopped, delayed, or otherwise dealt with in all
respects at the discretion of those authorities, and
without further notice to the senders. The pro-
clamation provides, further, that no claims in re-
spect of them, whether for the reimbursement of
the sums paid for transmission or otherwise, will
be considered by the Government of the Common-
wealth in any circumstances whatever. It is re-
gretted your application for a refund of the charges
cannot, therefore, be acceded to at present.
If the news was not to be made public, why
was it allowed to go through the press, and
come out in special editions? The with-
holding of the other message is more astound-
ing still. This was the text: “Sydney gone
mad, stores closed, tremendous excitement,
great celebrations.” One can analyse that
thoroughly, and he will find no word to
cause alarm and nothing referring to hos-
tilities. Was the withholding, then, a ruse
of the Postal Department. to prevent over-
loading the lines? If this is the case
it amounts to taking money under false
pretences. Certainly, had the senders been
notified at the counter, most of the messages
would have been withdrawn. One feature
very well worth noting is that the Depart-
ment, having pigeonholed a message, is not
under any obligation to notify the sender;
and as any message may evidently be sup-
pressed it is obvious that great loss may be
occasioned to one or both parties, especially
as the sender will naturally conclude that
the addressee has received the message and
will act accordingly. If the commercial com-
munity doesn t kick against the maintenance
of this regulation, having evidence of the
manner in which it is made to operate, then
the commercial community deserves to suffer.
Cocky and His Markets.
A report has been presented to the Lawson
Government of Victoria by agricultural-}
superintendent A. E. V. Richardson, who
has lately been examining the rural-research
work conducted by the State authorities of
California and other parts of North America.
One of the matters mentioned in the report
is that of the help given by California,
through its district advisers, to the “market-
ing and buying organisations” of the rural
-producers. To what extent this assistance
is given Richardson doesn’t precisely
state, but he will find plenty of scope in
Victoria for applying whatever information
he has collected on this vital matter. It is
a simple fact that, in Victoria, as in the
other States, the farmer who is encouraged
to grow surplus crops is left almost entirely
to his own devices to market them. In
some cases, such as that of perishable goods,
everything seems to be against the producer
who lives ahy distance out of the city.
The roads are often bad. The railway
authorities don’t seem to care whether they
take the man’s goods or not. They offer
no adequate facilities for carrying the stuff
in an economical manner; and consignments
are tossed out when they reach the city into
the hands mostly of private dealers, who
frequently put their own prices on the goods
and thus double or treble their legitimate
commission. Now that agricultural-superin-
tendent Richardson has “done” the States,
Oman, the Minister, might put him on to
fmd out exactly what the commission agent
and the railways are doing to the Victorian
farmer who sends his produce to the city
and has to depend on produce-handlers for
fair treatment. And then he might write
another report drawing comparisons as
odious as the differences demanded.
A The Military Police.
In Sydney, recently, an A.I.F. corporal and
Military Medallist had a misunderstanding
with a G.M. policeman. The corporal was
knocked out with a baton, and last week he
appeared before a district court-martial.
The Bulletin has no sympathy with the
“flash,” swashbuckling variety of expedition-
ary; this class of man is a nuisance to civil-
ians and a curse to the A.I.F. But the re-
currence of these cases suggests that the re-
turned men, who behaved remarkably well
abroad are not altogether to blame. Neither
probably are the G.M. policemen. On all
the evidence the fault lies with the system.
The trouble arises from the fact that a large
proportion of the G.M.P. have never been
to the Front. Many of them tried to get
there, no doubt, and are as good men as
those who went. But there is no logic or
reason in these matters. The expeditionary
remembers what he and his friends suffered,
and he feels resentment at being handled, not
to mention batoned, by men who—for how-
ever good a reason —were living in the pleas-
antest country in the world while he was in
Flanders or Palestine. The community has
always accepted the principle that distaste-
ful laws cannot be enforced. It is much the
same here. There is only one type of man
who possesses the necessary moral authority
to deal Avith an expeditionary where military
offences are concerned, and that is another
expeditionary. And the more distinguished
service the latter has to his credit the greater
influence for peace and quiet and law and
order generally he will wield. This was
discovered at A.I.F. Headquarters in Lon-
don about a year ago, with the result that
a number of men who had done practically
nothing but Provost Corps work were sent
over to France, and their places taken by-
fighting soldiers in need of a rest. The effect
on discipline was excellent. There is another
reason why a seasoned expeditionary makes
a good policeman—of expeditionaries. lie
knows their language. He knows the argu-
ments to apply. One returned n.c.o. who
knows his job, or for that matter one level-
headed A.I.F. private, could do better work
by a neatly-timed appeal to an obstreperous
warrior’s pride in his battalion or brigade
than half a dozen home-service ipen with
batons. It is a question of human nature,
and on that all who handle men base their
general conduct if they are wise. It was
announced last week that a military police
force is now to be sent to Colombo to check
possible rows there. If it is on the lines of
the G.M.P. force in Australia its only effect
will be to cause them. The Government is,
of course, in a difficult position. In the
G.M.P., as in other branches of the service,
it has a large number of officers and men
who have done good work here in the last
four years; and it would be a hardship in
some cases if they were to be abruptly dis-
pensed with. As against this there is the
credit of the A.I.F. and the comfort of the
civilian population, both of which are ad-
versely affected by the retention of the pre-
sent men. None but the best of military
police should be good enough for a country
in the throes of demobilisation; and the
Government will only find the best amongst
the pick of the returned men.

When the Revolution Came to
Switzerland.
From Corporal Digger, at Berne;—
Syedah! This is good! lam out of Hunland—
six months in three prison “hospitals” and
seventeen months in assorted Hun gaols! Now I’m
booked for home. That’s best of all.
These people, though kind, are simple-hearted,
and funny—damned funny. You see, they’ve
been out of the war because their Federal
Council was ready for it, and, though they
looked across at Belgium and shivered and
were scared of the Huns, still nothing happened.
They adore military displays and pageants and
processions—you should see the pride even the
train conductor takes in his red bag and starting
horn! So when the armistice was signed they
felt they’d missed the ’bus. France, next door, was
shrieking with delight, and Switzerland had
nothing!
And that’s where Bill the Bolshevik came in.
There have been 36 of him at Zurich a,nd Basle
hot-airing to the unions this last 12 months
about Militarism and the Federal Council being
curses to the country. The Swiss hard-working person
vaguely inclined to agree with him because it
promised some excitement, but he remembered
about . the missus and the kids and went and
did his three months sentry-go on the frontier
each year, and up near Basle he watched the Huns
and the French flinging sudden death at each
other and wore a gas-helmet when the wind blew
his way, and was glad and sad by turns. He
and his home were safe, but he was out of all
the excitement. He wouldn’t strike, though. To
down tools would mean that the Huns would come
in “to restore order,’’ and he had noted Fritz’s
methods in Ivan’s country.
But now Jacques over the way was rejoicing,
and, as the Swiss climbed the fence to have a
look, the Bolshie put the hard word on him again
—and he stopped work. All over the Federal rail-
ways the wheels stood still. The trams stayed .
in the sheds, the carters fed their horses and
yarned, the timber-getters greased their axes and
came into town, - the shops closed, and the popula-
tion everywhere set out .to enjoy themselves. They
dmnt mean any harm really, only they were tired
The Great Adventure.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
“There will be new formations, new enterprises,
new possibilities, new and closer combinations and
alliances, new adventures for Gon and for man. This
is not going to be a statesman’s job, or a Parlia-
ment’s job, or a parson’s job; it is the people’s
job all through.— The Archbishop of Canterbury.
Had God grown old, and did He cease to
keep
His watch below the starlight and the sun?
Was there some breach of Fate that Hell
could leap
Out from her central fires and sulphurous
deep
And make Hate dance to music of a gun?
Did the four great archangels drop their
shields,
And tremble wing to wing in sudden fear,
While the vast Reaper on the stricken fields,
Harvest on harvest, counted his pale yields
Of garnered youth that cost the world so
dear?
I know not if the shattered battlement
Of Truth rained broken pearl into the void,
And jasper flakes, from the foundations rent,
By storming Falsehood, to the far suns sent
Rumor of Heaven defeated and destroyed.
I know not what a silver clamor fell
Along the shining trenches far and high
Where sworded angel ranks were seen to
swell
And the clear captain-word of Gabkiel
Rallied the white defenders of the sky.
But this I know: the heavens were dark with
doom,
And Truth strove mightily when hope was
fled;
And tortured earth below became one tomb
Of youth and joy; and in a little room
Of every street a woman wept her dead.
This, too, I know: through darkness and
despair
There came a sudden glory on'the night;
The very rose of death bloomed white and
fair;
Triumphant bugles shrilled along the air
And faith stood victor-crowned in all men’s
sight.
Perchance at that great moment bolts of fire
Flamed thunder-linked, with keen, white
lightnings shod;
And Heaven’s artillery with loud desire
Swept from the Holiest over wall and spire
To scourge black ruin from the gates of
God.
Perchance, with beat of w’ing and clash of
shield
. And shouting of innumerable hosts,
Vast angel armies strove in open field
And saw the ranks of darkness break and
yield
Along the cloud-capes of their golden
coasts.
Perchance into the horror of the deep,
. O’erflamed with tumult of supernal woe,
A cataract of vengeance, leap on leap.
Poured Hellward, roaring, from the crystal
steep,
Weighted with broken legions of the foe.
I cannot tell; but this the winds have told,
Blowing across the grasses of our graves:
The world is wide, the ways are manifold ;
And all the stars of night that seemed so
cold
Are warm to burning with a faith that
saves.
Speed, sneed the ship of high adventure fast!
God has uncharted lands of life to find;
The pennon of the truth flies from His mast.
Together,. through new seas, out of the past
We sail into the wider ways of Mind.
Heaven wins. Earth cheers. The ranks of
Hell are broken.
On! On to find God places in the sun !
Each to his post. The last great word is
spoken.
The wind of victory for splendid token,
The voyage of the ages i s begun !
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.

of the deadly monotony of the daily grind,
and wanted an excuse for a spell and to join in
the tin-kettling across the road.
But the Federal Council was taking no risks.
It stuck up proclamations imploring the people
to “maintain order in this national crisis,” and
the people gathered round the placards and were
thrilled and thought what hard-doers they were.
Then the Council mobilised some of the strikers,
sent them into the towns, and told them to keep
order. For three days I lived in a comic opera.
Cavalry and machine-guns clattered up and down
the old cobblestones. The infantry marched around
with bayonets fixed, piled arms in the city squares,
and attitudinised before an admiring public.
Patrols were all round us. The women came out
and fed the soldiers with cakes and buckets of
hot chocolate. Everyone was delighted. Each day
the soldiers did marches to freedom, looking mighty
proud, and the people applauded. Each night
the unions came out and proceshed with union
banners and red flags, and the soldiers cheered.
Whiskered agitators climbed the übiquitous foun-
tains and made excited speeches in Swiss-German
to which no one listened. There was one “re-
grettable incident.” The soldiers were pressing
the crowd back when one of them accidentally
discharged his rifle, and an elderly frock-coated
citizen, who was doing his best to keep out of
trouble, got it. He was highly indignant, and
i 3 now engaged in suing the Federal Council.
Early on the third day the Council got fed
up and made a coup. It arrested the 36 Bolshies,
put. them into motor-lorries, took them to the
Hun frontier and chased them across. The Hun
sentries recognised their old cobbers from Berlin
and let them in. The Unionists, feeling some-
what bored, promptly met, sang their national
anthem umteen times, and declared all strikes off.
The Swiss had had their war and their peace
celebration all in one 1
Next morning the municipal sweepers got busy
and by midday all was normal again.

An individual who begins to be rather con-
spicuous is the loafing soldier who wants
a drink. So far a mere handful by com-
parison have returned from the war, and
those few got the first chaise at the avail-
able billets. How many soldiers will want
a drink when the multitude returns to
scramble for the fag-end of the billets is a
large and thirsty subject. Already there is
a wild-cat debate about the rights of re-
turned men to wharf-lumping jobs, though
wharf-lumping is as uninspiring work as
anyone could imagine; but even the profit-
less dispute about wharf-lumping doesn’t
keep all the men off the street corners. So
this semi-Freetrade Australia, which dare
not find work for its people even at making
their own clothes, lest it should interfere
with its wonderful brass god the importer,
has started to raise criminals and wasters
out of the aftermath of the war —a pretty
large crowd that is already forgetting the
ways of industry for lack of anything to
be industrious about. The incurable tee-
totaller says the remedy lies in closing the
pubs. It doesn’t seem much of a remedy.
A man loafing in the street with a full
knowledge of the degradation of his work-
less state is more dangerous than a man
loafing in a pub with only half a knowledge.
P.S. —This is especially the case when it
rains, for even a wet hen gets angry.
In his character of martyr to duty the
wandering Minister of Defence is something
more than a joke. He is a scream. In his
published ’list of sworn enemies and dis-
likers the vainglorious bungler has the
audacity to cite 4000 interned enemy aliens
plus their relatives and friends. Pearce
the free-and-independent personal interner
of 4000 enemy aliens! Why, public opinion
simply had to compel the gathering-iq of
foes in our midst; xvherefore, broadly speak-
ing, the man who had the last word in
matters of military precaution earned the
disrespect of well-informed loyalists, rather
than the dislike of disloyalists. There never
was any rash courting of alien enmity on
the Minister’s part. A number of Germans
and German sympathisers may have ceased
to glorify his name as much as they had
done before the war; but the chances are
that far more were thankful for their liberty.

The splendid capacity of State Parliaments
for making asses of themselves is exemplified
in the Vic. Fruit-grading Act. which the
growers assured the legislators would be in-
effective and merely a nuisance. Under it
the grower has to paste on all his cases a
label setting forth his name and address and
the grade of the fruit—A, B. C or D. The
buyer—the fruiterer or greengrocer—-unani-
mously ignores this label. He mostly buys
from some half-dozen men whom he
knows, and he judges the grade of the fruit
by personal inspection. Anyhow, the grower
invariably marks his fruit “Grade D,” to
avoid the chance of prosecution by an in-
spector who may not know a peach from a
nectarine, or an apple from an anricot
(writer has known that happen). But—
this is where the damfoolishness of the Act
comes in—-the up-country grower, who also
labels his produce “Grade D,” is at the
mercy of the city agent, who may sell the
stuff at a good price on the personal inspec-
tion of the buyer, yet may send back a re-
turn at the market price of a fourth-class
product. Of course, the fruit-agent, being
like the butter-agent, a strictly honorable
man, would not dream of doing any such
thing; still there is a powerful temptation.
Anyhow, the Act has done no good and has
merely given the grower a lot of unnecessary
work.
Tennyson Smith, recently imported lec-
turer for the prohibition propagandists, is
out to see that the trade in intoxicating
liquors ceases to be respectable. It is his
candid admission, and is valuable as an in-
dication of the exact position taken up by
himself and those who have hired him to
put a little foreign zeal into a cause which
without perpetual clamor grows very languid.
The trade needs td be made not less but more
respectable. The hidden evil is the worst;
and Tennyson Smith and his friends have
so far failed to prove that there is neces-
sarily any evil at all about the business if
thorough respectability is insisted upon by
the public. A thoroughly respectable trade
would produce no drunks at all; the part of
our present trade which operates out of
sight during hours when prohibition is sup-
posed to be in force produces a lot.
“Solomon Malaprop” has a few words; —
I read (12/12/’18) that proposals regarding new
industries “shall be promptly and sympathetically
considered.” Also, that “the Federal Cabinet. .. .is
anxious to hear from manufacturers and others....
who are contemplating the opening up of new in-
dustries in the Commonwealth, and it invites definite
and fully-detailed proposals.” “Manufacturers and
others” who have work to do haven’t time or
patience to submit their proposals to Hughes, the
talky barrister of few briefs; and Cook, the
local preacher. They don’t want to give
full particulars of their businesses to a
leaky crowd of dilatory theorists not one of whom
has much mechanical training in any case. Life is
too short to wait for a cheque which is six months
late with its own balance-sheet. Why, in the name
of all that is wonderful, should the doing of honest
work with Australian labor at Australian union
wages and conditions not be allowed unless these
impossibles are approached for their godlike per-
mission, after which they will, presumably, leave the
manufacturer to . take all the risks, and annex half
or three-quarters of the profits under their special
war-tax, and let in the goods of British rivals, free as
usual? The manufacturer would be more than satis-
fied if he were put on something like the same- basis
as his rivals in Japan and Canada and the U.S., and
didn’t have quite so much of his profits embezzled on
the plea that he is a Peace-War-Armistice industry.
He doesn’t want the Cabinet’s dashed blessing, nor,
having no wireless-engineering works to sell, does he
feel like furnishing “fully detailed proposals.” The
documents would probably be lost, and would next
turn up as the basis for an argument in an I.W.W.
paper, and no one would know how they got there.
