“Ulsterman” in response:—■
Being well aware that “Henry Horsecodlar” is
endowed with a fine command of forceful language,
I was not at all surprised that he described me as
“a rotten historian.” After this relief to his feel-
ings he admitted half of my statement and then
followed with a series of dogmatic assertions which
left the impression that his opinion of historians —•
“rotten” or otherwise —should not be taken seriously.
He 'states that the trouble with Ulstermen is that
“they won’t be Irish” ; but I venture to think that,
on reflection, he will consider that they were quite
Irish in 1798, when they allied themselves with the
people of Leinster and Munster in rebelling against
the British Government. On the other hand, he
will probably think that they have been quite un-
Irish in their refusal to forget certain actions
which were done at that time by their southern
allies. Probably “Henry Horsecollar” will admit
that Ulstermen again became Irish in 1868, when
they sent 10 representatives to Parliament to help
to pass the Land Act of 1870, the Irish Church Act
and the Ballot Act. After Ireland had become the
most favored part of the United Kingdom, Ulstermen
and all other reasonable people throughout that
country were content to let matters rest; but not
so the impulsive and the ignorant, who were easily
misled by political agitators. In 1798, when there
were genuine grievances, there were genuine Irish
patriots; but since then the character of the self-
styled patriots has gradually declined till they have
at last reached the Trotsky and Lenin stage. At
present, of the two “Irish patriots” who are most
prominent in the limelight, one is a half-bred
Spaniard and the other a semi-lunatic lady who is
a scion of an aristocratic English family, and is the
wife of a Polish “artist.” I have no doubt that
both of these Irish patriots would heartily applaud
“Henry Horsecollar’s” cheap sneer at Ulstermen
as “aliens.” It is quite natural that, as leaders of
the Irish Bolsheviks, they should have a deep dislike
for the people of the only part of Ireland which, dur-
ing the recent war, was not a disgrace to civilisation;
but at this side of the world we expect something
better from a writer such as “Henry llorsecoli.ar.”
Pearce, Minister for Excuses and Pre-
tences. has once more fallen over his feet.
For some weeks Melbourne has been taking
a mild interest in the situation at the
Queenscliff siege-artillery camp. A number
of the young men there reckon that,, having
enlisted with the sole idea of going on ser-
vice abroad, they should now be demobilised.
Pearce recently made the public statement,
to justify his refusal, that the lads “practi-
cally enlisted to take the places of men who
were on active service” —a really shocking
thing to say if it were incorrect. _ Now
Alfred Joubert has published the certificate
given to his son, who is one of the siege-
artillerists in question. The document
specifies that the young man had “passed
the medical examination for the R.A.G.A.
for reinforcements, 36th heavy artillery
group, for active service abroad:’ The
swearing-in happened on January 14, 1918,
so young Joubert, instead of going as he
desired to the bloody fields on which other
members of his family had already dis-
tinguished themselves, was detained for a
twelve-month at the Heads; and then he
was told, by the inadequate and shifty per-
son who invited him to join up because men
were badly wanted at the Front, that he
stayed in Australia from his own choice.
xVs soon as this country is declared free
from pneumonic ’flu’ Commonwealth and
State Health officials had better try to come
to an agreement about inoculation. The
process has been tremendously boomed,
especially in Sydney; but it is quite obvious
that State and Commonwealth authorities
are not equally enthusiastic; and in the
U.S.A., where they have had umpteen times
more experience of the disease than we
have had, they practise inoculation hardly at
all. An influenza specific which is claimed
to unexpectedly cure rheumatism, epilepsy,
varicose veins, deafness, etc., etc., sounds
too much like cheap-jackery to be passed
without calm and serious inquiry.
A glaring example of profiteering was dis-
closed by Prendergast, M.L.A., in asking
for the extension of a little woollen-mill in
‘Melbourne, lately started by the Repatria-
tion Department. Prendergast’s contention
is that hundreds of men should be employed
instead of only five, seeing that the cloth
is of excellent quality and costs, he alleges,
only 7s. 6d. a yard, whereas it is being sold
to a city firm at 15s. If Prendergast’s fig-
ures are right the Department is shockingly
guilty of neglecting a chance to push trade.
The mill could, on Prendergast’s showing,
sell excellent stuff at a reasonable price;
but it demands double the cost of production
for a very limited output, and on those terms
the output would deserve to remain limited
for evermpre. The business of the Govern-
ment should be to encourage local manufac-
ture in the interests of the consumer as
well as of the worker, not to run a mill in
order to sell a handful of goods at 100 per
cent, profit.
Australia is being allowed to hear a little
of what happened in the middle of last
year out towards Amiens. It was dreadful,
certainly: the only difference between
officers and men of some portions of the
Fifth Army was that the officers ran the
faster. They both beat the Boche by miles.
But every time any; Australian sees or
hears a reference to that performance he
ought to visualise a few other things. He
ought to picture the British Fleet keeping
a way open across the seas, and escorting
no less than 80 p.c. of all the American
troops that landed in France; the British
mercantile marine, feeding not only Eng-
land, but largely France, Belgium and Italy
also, and carrying over 70 p.c. of the
American troops; the British coal-mines,
supplying not only home requirements, but
those of France and Italy too ; the British
munition-works, supplying four-fifths of the
Allies’ needs, American included. It wasn’t
marvellous that in the British army—the
largest of any army in France—there were
some poor weeds. The wonder was that
Britain could have any army of consequence
there at all.
“Wealsman”: The newest Senator,
Mulcahy, of Tasmania, owes his selec-
tion for the job to a “Loyalty League”
anti-Catholic circular letter distributed
among Parliamentarians on the morn-
ing of the voting. The circular set
forth that the Speck’s Senatorial team con-
tained only one Protestant, and urged that,
in view of the persistent and increasing push-
fulness of the R.C. authorities in their en-
deavor to secure political advantages, etc.,
the position was dangerous and ought to be
checked. As Mulcahy’s chief opnonent for
the Senate vacancy was M.H.A.,
who is also Launceston president of the
Loyalty League, the circular looked like an
attempt to boost Marshall on the sectarian
issue. At any rate, it was so regarded by
the 11 Labor members of Parliament, who
so resented the intrusion of the serpent that
they unexpectedly and unanimously cast
their No. 2 votes for Mulcahy. The figures
at the penultimate count were Marshall 20,
Mulcahy 15, Hurst 12. As the Labor party
had previously announced that its members
had decided to nominate only one candidate
for the vacancy, viz.. Lieut. Hurst (the ex-
M.IJ.A. who fought on Gallipoli and collected
a commission and a smashed arm in France),
and had left all hands free as to their second
choice, it seemed highly probable that Labor’s
No. 2 votes would be about equally divided,
in which case the Senate seat would go to
Marshall. But the final count showed that,
of the 12 votes cast for Hurst (one Govern-
ment supporter, probably Whitsitt, voted for
the Labor soldier), li had alternatively
voted Mulcahy and only one Marshall,
An apologist for Pearce and family’s visit
to England has been found in Melbourne. He
urges that the thing of first importance is
that demobilisation shall not proceed faster
than the men can be “absorbed” in Austra-
lia, and says that Mr. Pearce is to see to this.
In other words, and according ;o this ex-
planation, the Minister’s job is not to assist
demobilisation, but to be a brake upon it.
The Digger on the other side, longing for
home, will welcome Mr. Pearce !
Thirteen Westralian shearers put up a
good joke recently. They were under con-
tract to the Boolathana station and, oh
Jan. 13, decided by ballot (8 to 5) that the
jumbucks awaiting the shears were too wet
to deal with. The station prosecuted them.
At the hearing evidence was given that not
only had no rain fallen since December 19,
but" the sheep were to be shorn in a speci-
ally-protected shed. Solomon gave judg-
ment for the barbers, on the ground that by
the terms of contract a ballot by the men
was the only authority for deciding the
question of wetness. Then costs amounting
to £3 15s. 6d. per shearer were solemnly
given against the station-owners!
“Soe”: “Soller” (B. 9/l/’l9) was wrong
when he said that 3s. 6d. is the charge made
by the N. S. Wales Government for a death
certificate. When correct particulars ate
given a certified copy of an entry of birth,
death or marriage (or an extract therefrom)
costs only 2s. If particulars are incorrect
a search is necessary, for w T hich an extra
charge of ss. is made for each name searched
under. Records for five to 10 years are
usually searched through, but in lots of
cases applicants get 60 years (i.e., from 1856
on) for the same money. If they want the
search made in the records of ah earlier year
than 1855 there’s another ss. payable, and
for that modest sum they can have the re-
cords probed as far back as the wicked old
days of 17S8.
Another tragedy comes from South Aus.
Isaac Gully, living in a wild part near
Nuriootpa, was shot dead by his daughter,
a girl about 20, and the coroner’s jury
called it self-defence, “with no blame at-
tached.” The man was in the habit, it
seems, of threatening to “wipe out” all his
family, and when he took down the cart-
ridges and reached for a gun she used the
revolver that had been bought for her by
a brother in the city. As the brother said
candidly to a newspaper man, “It was
either father or the others. The sister got
in first.” After the verdict the matter
seemed to be at an end; but the Law
Department has decided that it is serious
enough to deserve some further inquiry.
Lefroy, Premier of Westralia, got a better
gift than his knighthood early in the new
year. It was a large-sized tin containing a
tongue. The new knight thought at first
someone was attempting a joke; but it
turned out to be the first product of the
State Freezing and Canning Works. at
Wyndham. According to the W.A. Minister
for Works, business will be in full swing by
May next.
Died in Sydney last week, “Tom” Knox,
of Dalgety’s. He started his commercial
life in a bank; then became a stock and
station agent, dropped that to manage a
M.L. branch of the firm w T hich was ulti-
mately to become Dalgety’s, Ltd., and finally
settled in Sydney. He put in half a century
making money for himself and others, and
at the end of it there wasn’t one reasonable
person to say that he was other than a
straight and kindly boss and a man. He did
a little peace-soldiering long ago, and, lat-
terly, interested himself in Polar exploration.
But his lifelong hobby was business. That
and Respectability.
Cable, 21/1/’l9: —
It is reported that ex-Emperor Karl, of Austria, is
still seriously ill. His entourage denies that his
condition is due to alcoholism.
Neither is his friend Wilhelm any longer on
the Spree.
While old Captain Hancock was at Moonta
he was the nearest approach Australia
had to an old-time patriarch. He wasn’t
merely boss of the one group of big mines by
which the town lived. He was the religious
boss, and often the domestic boss as well;
and he kept Moonta a little bit of Cornwall
—the little bit of Cornwall that Oswald
Pryor pictures sometimes. It used to be an
old gag that the first words the little
Methodist-to-be learned to say were “God
bless feyther and mawther and Cap’n Han-
cock.” You have to know these things to
understand how it happens that Wallaroo
and Moonta have in 50 years had only one
strike that was worth calling a strike.

The Itigo Opera Co. made a promising
opening in “Cavalleria” and “Pagliacci” at
the Princess’s (Melb.) on Saturday. Of
course there were the defects that are seem-
ingly inseparable from first-night produc-
tions, but considering that the. operas were
given in mixed English and Italian and the
moderate orchestra had to behave like a
much larger band, the achievement was
pretty satisfactory. One of the brightest
features was the mostly-Australian chorus,
which sang with decision and brought the
reinforcement of its fresh young voices to
one or two sad and wilting occasions.
These occasions were due to the bloodless
quality of a couple of the Italians, who,
after a lifetime’s experience, were incapable
of appreciating the tumultuous sexuality of
each little play. The worst offender by far
was Filippini as Alfio in “Cavalleria.” A
small, mild citizen, with the air of regarding
a harsh word as a terrible adventure, Filip-
pini is the typical vocalist who refines rude
feelings for the pleasure of young ladies at
suburban social evenings. His profession
of sturdy disdain for his rival Turiddu would
have been disqualified by his lack of physical
resources, even if he had not ruined the
whole impression by standing stolidly with
his feet turned out in a manner which makes
him the Ned Duggan of the operatic stage.
But where Filippini inevitably failed through
his petty personality, Cacialli, who was
weakest in “Pagliacci,” had merely reached
the crisis of the orthodox Italian who has
discovered that the malignancy of a character
like Tonio cannot be translated into a couple
of handwaves and a gleam of teeth. Caci-
alli’s Tonio wanted the crooked wickedness
which the former exponent, Scamuzzi, never
failed to drive home. His singing, though
nicely phrased, had rather a portentous suet-
pudding effect. Lois, the old Gonsalez per-
former, tenored the heady passions of
Turiddu with an abundance of gesture which
seemed unwarranted by the feebleness of the
enemy (Filippini). Leah Myers’s soprano,
in the part of Santuzza, occasionally emerged
somewhat bruised by the higher flights of the
score, but she made the tragedy apparent on
the histrionic side, whereas Ethel Hems, as
Lola, hardly gave a clue to the giddiness
that beguiles the erratic fancy of Turiddu.
Balboni’s heartbroken clown in “Pagliacci”
was easily the hero of the evening. His

On Tuesday and last night Anita Roma
and Pattie Russell were the heroines of
“Cavalleria” and “Pagliacci,” and will sing
again in the same parts to-morrow. On
Saturday the bill will be “Rigoletto.”
The weather in Melbourne has been won-
drous kind to the pantos this month, and
their prosperity abateth not. At the King’s
the performance continues to be carried on
under the name of “Jack and Jill,” and by
w’ay of apology for founding any sort of
panto on a bald and unconvincing nursery
rhyme J. and J. are required to walk up a
hill for the purpose of sliding down it in a
conspicuously harmless manner. A wild
caricature of a plot requires the water sup-
ply on the top of the hill to be drawn, in a
dry and rusty bucket, from a Magic Well.
Lupino and Bert Bailey sit near the windlass
and converse on divers subjects, and every-
body grows rich beyond the dreams of avarice,
owing to the kind attentions of a Good Fairy
who provides the panto with a Punch and
Judy show at matinges only. Espinosa has
now added his personal dancing efforts to
his artistic ballet effects.
At Her Majesty’s the panto excuses its
title with the fact that an unaccount-
able fairy supplies a young person called
Goody with Two Shoes. It is eccentric
behavior on the Fairy’s part, considering
that her protege’s mother is a terrible man-
she with a bibulous nose, and no amount of
magic power can ever make the family thor-
oughly respectable. But these unwise things
are always being done at Christmas time—on
the stage. The scarecrow Dame invariably
becomes mother-in-law to the principal boy,
if she wasn’t plain mother in the first in-
stance. This time it is the Duke’s Rightful
Heir (May de Sousa) who weds the sole
product of the appalling Dame. Her senti-
mental songfulnefes makes one feel sorry for
Mav’s matrimonial future. When the sutfer-
audible orchestra permits the words of the
principal boy’s ballads to be heard she is
found to be singing with as much feeling as
sweetness.

The fine, rich voice of Peter Dawson, who
was lately in khaki, will be heard again at
Melb. Auditorium on Saturday and Monday
next. The accomplished Peter is a host in
himself, but there will be half a dozen vocal
and instrumental artists to help him
through.

The latest communique from the Courtiers
at St. Kilda (Vic.) reports an increase in
the exuberance of Percy Clifton, who this
week is a Bairnsfather hero and is also com-
ing out worst in a sketch about Beer. Clif-
ton and Zoli almost exhaust the humorous
resources of the Courtiers, for, though there
is a light comedian, Oswald Browne, he has
more the style of an elated milk-ho. Some
of the ladies possess fair voices, and one of
them, Maggie Foster, fiddles very tastily;
and there is a tenor, Leslie Jephcott, who
owns a rather thin tootle.

Elsie Ferguson is reigning for another
week at Hoyt’s Lyceum (Melb.) in “The
Lie.” At the same house Claire Whitney
is exhibiting “The Ruling Passion” with
all the brilliancy of which her dressmaker
is capable. At De Luxe Wallace Reid gets
a full helping of applause in the intense
war special, “The Firefly of France.” A
whole menagerie of spies is let loose in the
outbreak, and there are things that happen
to Wallace beside which a fall off a high
building is a snooze in church. Sessue
Hayakawa and Florence Vidor are teaching
the art of Jap lovemaking in a series of
ceremonious lessons. The film is called
“The Bravest Way” and describes the
heaviest heartaches in an elegant fashion.
At the Britannia the latest sensation is the
midget May McAllister in “The Killjoy.”
The Melba borrowed the captivating Mabel
Normand in “Mickey” for another three
days, but will finish this week with “The
Power and the Glory” wherein June Elvidge
has a five-reel spin.

There was a revival of “Katinka” at Syd-
ney Criterion on Saturday night. The cast
is a seasoned one, on the whole; but the
players make up in zest what they lack in
youthful spontaneity. Florence Young,
Claude Bantock, Olive Godwin, John
Ford, Reginald Roberts and Leslie Plolland
are in it, and they do all the old familiar
stunts that have gratified playgoers for gen-
erations past. Gladys Moncrieff uses her
clear soprano with finished effectiveness, but
she makes no visible effort to act. Phil
Smith is diverting on strictly normal lines.
For the purposes of the plot he is a wealthy
Yank; in reality lie is just Phil Smith. The
eight chorus-lads who swing their sticks,
and wave their hats, and hop absurdly round
in unison, are in evidence throughout the
show, and this paper tires of the whole
futile tribe. “The Pink Lady” goes on next
Saturday.
Sydney’s principal picture-theatres adver-
tise special inducements to customers to
leave their homes unattended this week and
chance the Burglars’ Union, which is un-
usually busy just now. Leading stars on
the first-release films include Norma Tal-
madge (at the Crystal Palace and Strand)
Sessue Hayawaka and Wallace Reid (Hoyt’s
and the Haymarket), Roy Stewart and Gladys
Leslie (Lyceum) and Belle Bennett (Globe).
At the Globe “Australia’s Own,” mostly
filmed in New Guinea, is adding interest to
the screen with Nellie Romer as lead, and
at the town Hall the lesson of the week is
To Hell with the Kaiser”—as if Hell was
a place where any body could be dumped !
The drama mostly gives Adelaide a miss
in the iimmer months; not that the city is
torrid for many evenings at a time, but it
is always liable to be. The Royal, which
now fills in its odd weeks with pictures,
3S . going again with Allen Doone, and the
Tivoli promises to re-open at once. The
Camille three, as funny as they are clever,
continue to be the brightest thing in the
Majestic bill, and the Hamptons, Elmars,

ole does the same for the English Tommy.

Colies. having finished the Hun, does
the halls:—
Little Tich seems the most likely successor to
Dan Leno. His long- shoes that w e knew in Australia
have been discarded; and his stunts nowadays are
mostly clever burlesques of every-day types. That
old stager Wilkie Bard comes a very close second.
His are mostly well-acted one-man comic sketches,
but he is without the vivacity of Tich. Harry Weldon
is a red-hot favorite at present, mainly on account
of a peculiar clumsy, rolling gait, like that of a
restless ruminating elephant, and a lisping of his
s’s—which is copied from one end of London to
the other. Billy Merson is a novel example of a
scientifically constructed comedian entirely lacking
in spontaneous humor. I wasn’t surprised to read
that he was also an active member of a big engineer-
ing firm. Another distinctive turn is that of Albert
Whelan —late Waxman, of Melbourne —whose lean
Digger dial, Australian twang, and a calm, cold,
unsurpassable casualness on the stage typifies the
“grim resourceful,” etc. Australian. Whelan de-
pends almost solely on his own dry personality ;
but, like many English comedians, he’s been doing
the same turn for two years or more, and it’s be-
ginning to show signs of wear. Neil Kenyon is
going strong with humorous, well-written character-
sketches. Will Evans and George Graves are about
the best exponents of conventional low-comedy stuff.
Evans does roug'h-female impersonations, while
Graves always scores as a master of humor of the
panto.-“baron” kind. Our long friend Carlton lives
well on his tall bald head and his waggish conjur-
ing and burlesque-hypnotising stunts.
On the female side, everlasting Vesta Tilley puts
up a wonderful show as a male impersonator. I
daren’t make a guess at her age, but her activity
on the stage is amazing. Her Tommy sketches are
splendid. She has several imitators, but is easily
in the lead. Babs and Beatty, two young Jewesses,
are firm favorites. The humorous lady of the firm
isn’t afraid to sacrifice her natural attractiveness 1
to the highly ludicrous. Marie Lloyd is still giving
em the “rough stuff” with superb artistry. In
the same line, but more on the daintily-naughty
side, is Maidie Scott. Lorna and Toots Pounds are
still precocious exponents of the noisy soubrette
business. Their advertised Australian origin and
certain rough imitations of popular English artists
have won them great popularity. Margaret Cooper
holds a good place as a consistent entertainer af
the piano.
Peake, of S. Aus., has his troubles, too.
Recently the wheat muddle in the State
became worse than usual, and Treasurer
Butler, the Minister in charge, was told
that he had too much work to do altogether,
and for the good of his health ought to give
up the management of the scheme, which
he did after some demur. Yet within a
week or two there was a re-shuffle of port-
folios and the allegedly over-worked Minister
was given Agriculture on top of his other
jobs.

Gone oyer the range, Ernest Sando Emer-
son ( ‘Milky White”), verse-, story- and
sketch-writer and nature student. For The
Bulletin and other Australian publica-
tions he did much graceful work, particu-
larly in verse. He was 49 when Death shut
the inkwell at Brisbane.
“D.F.” : The loftiest boat in Australia is
one which has just been taken up to Lake
Babylon, also called the Nameless Lake, on
tlm high tableland near Deloraine (Tas.).
which is not far short of 4000 feet above
sea-level and some hundreds of feet above
the Great Lake (3250 feet). The Hydro-
Electric Department is putting a big power-
boat, 40 feet by 12 feet, with a draught of 3
teet and a capacity of 15 tons, on the Great
Lake to carry material across to the point
on the western side where the cutting of the
canal to bring the waters of the Upper Ouse
into the lake is to begin.
Sometimes, thank Heaven, the man who
sends out of his own country for something
his own country could supply as well gets
his deserts. In London, a while before the
war, a publisher sent some popular tunes
to Dresden to be printed—“ Melodious Melo-
dies” he called it. There was something
there from “Florodora.” The Hun printer
made it “Floro-dora.” The publisher sent
back the proofs, writing “join” over the
hyphen. When the book was printed, there
it was: “ ‘Tell Me, Pretty Maiden,’ from
I loro-join-Dora.”

Dec., 1918.. £53,951 10 p.c. = £24,000 £29,951 £253,515
That last line tells of a sort of glorious
resurrection. The Q.N. crashed magnifi-
cently in ’93, and after the wreckage had been
overhauled a lot of capital and reserves was
written off to cover losses, while three-
quarters of the customers’ deposits were
converted into interminable stock, the re-
maining ss. in th(> £ being struck off, though
it was arranged that shareholders were to
get no dividend until the whole of this ss.
was made good out of profits. Besides this
indebtedness there was also £1,833,327 of
Government deposits to be repaid. Alto-
gether in 1897 shareholders were burdened
with 31 millions of debts which had to be
met before they could get a taste; and
about half of this total had to be paid out
of profits only.
The clean-up was swifter than general-
manager Ralston anticipated in his nicely
planned repayment scheme. The final
instalment to the Queensland Government
was met six years ahead of the scheduled
time, while the indebtedness to the other
depositors was lifted very rapidly during
recent _years. In 11 years to Dec., 1908,
only 15 p.c. of the debt was repaid ; in the
next five years the bank managed a further
30 p.c.; and during the 41 years to August
last the remaining 55 p.c. was got out of the
way. Meantime the bank’s position has . been
steadily strengthened : in the 10 years to Dec.
31 last £179,515 has been added to visible
reserves after providing for last half’s divi-
dend. And there is not the slightest doubt
that the directors took advantage of the
unusual position to build up internal reserves.
Shareholders could not get anything until
all the old troubles had been unloaded, no
matter how large the profits might have been ;
and since the management was running well
ahead of its time-table, there was very little
advantage in showing big profits while such
a fine opportunity offered to lay down solid
foundations for the future.
How remarkably the Q.N. has developed
is shown by this comparison:—

The interminable stock (representing 75 p.c.
of the old deposits) is practically pref. capi-
tal—cheap, too, for it carries only 3£ p.c.
p.a., and in these dear-money times it must
be earning nice profits for the bank. Every
1 p.c. the £3.091,669 brings in above 34 p.c.
means £15,458 added to each half’s profit.
Capital is .£480.000 and visible reserves
£253,515. Including the inscribed stock, the
bank’s funds total £3,825,184, while all lia-
bilities to the public amount to £8.661,985.
So for every £1 of its own funds (reckoning
the interminable stock as capital) the bank
holds less than £2 6s. of the public’s money ;
which is a wonderfully strong position. Of the
£8,661,985 of liabilities no less than 56 p.c. is
held liquid. To meet the £3,801,713 of lia-
bilities not thus represented, there is the
£7,638,897 in advances and premises, and
these items would have to depreciate by over
one-half before creditors were denied their
full 20s. in the £.
As to the future, nobody knows quite
what is going to happen. The splashing of
loans has not ceased yet, and the period of
depression which most bankers anticipated
has not been sighted. Bananaland has been
favored w'ith good seasons during the past
couple of years, and has done well out of
its produce. But the prospects are. not
wholly promising. The copper outlook is
not bright; the sugar industry is unable to
accommodate itself to the new labor condi-
tions ; and the Bolshevik of the Northern
State threatens trouble. Still, things will
have to be very bad before Q.N. divs. run
dry again. The margin between visible
profits and the 10 p.c. is very wide. By
and bye it may become uncomfortably wide
and capital will have to be increased. A
div. in excess of 10 p.c. is likely to be
misunderstood by people who don’t know
the sad history of the past sixteen years.

The profits for 1917 shown in the first
table are swollen by £50,000 surplus over
book values on the realisation of assets.
Not only are profits better, but the abnormal
conditions have strengthened the co.’s
finances. Assets, apart from properties and
advances, in 1915 had a book-value of
£1,004,319: they now amount to £1,267,901.
Meantime liabilities have been reduced by
£53.571. And that does not tell the full
extent of the movement, for the conglom-
erate of “loans on land, stations, stock and
other securities, and properties” now also
includes some fixed deposits. On the other
hand the book value of properties jand
advances has shrunk from £2,467,210 in
1915 to £2,130,852 at June 30 last. This
reduction has, no doubt, clipped something
off profits; but that has been more than
made good by increased commission earn-
ings ; and, since nobody knew how the war
was going to work out, the directors prob-
ably viewed the strengthened liquid position
with much satisfaction.
There's a big element of gambling in
this, business. The co. puts its money on
Jupiter Pluvius, who can be guaranteed to
win more often than he fails. And the
co. is such a solid punter that when its
luck. is out it can always make a recovery
within a few seasons. Prior to the ’nineties
the A.M.L. and F. had paid almost a con-
tinuous 20 p.c. div., but after the big
drought and the subsequent smash it found
itself with many square miles of western
N. S. Wales thrown on its hands. Though
some heavy losses were made, the co, was
not very badly hit in the long run ; and, as
mentioned above, the realisation of old
properties resulted in a surplus of £50,000
over the book value, and the chances are
that the surplus was much greater, some-
thing being put up the sleeve to meet any
new troubles which should happen along.
But drought was not the co.’s only bugbear;
the Labor party, which threatened to smakh
up the big estates, gave the gilded share-
holders cold feet, and they . rushed to
the Argentine with some of their money.
Probably they’re a little sorry now; for.
after all, the Labor Party’s graduated land-
tax created a market for some of this co.’s
properties, most of which were leaseholds.
And the Argentine has proved not quite
the. country it was thought to be. The
chairman told shareholders last year that
“in Argentina there is a great diminution
in the number of sheep, not from causes
w T hich periodically cause a reduction in Aus-
tralia, which is speedily redressed, but from
various causes likely to be permanent.”
Capital is £520,000 and the eo. works on
£1.911,090 of debentures, almost wholly
4 p.c. money, which in these high-interest
times gives a good margin of profit.

Formed just over 10 years ago to amal-
gamate the. Baker and Rouse and Australian
Kodak interests, this co. has had a wonder-
fully successful career. The earlier profits
were helped somewhat by the sale of assets —
shares in the Eastman Kodak Co. of the
U.S.A. —but recent figures have not been
swollen in this way. Since the war started
the co. has been on a fine wicket, partly
because its Kodak connection guaranteed an
ample supply of raw materials for the local
factory. Further, when Uncle Sam got busy
on war supplies the American Kodak people
passed their Asian and Maoriland business
temporarily into the Australian co.’s hands.
The big increase in profits during the past
few years has been due almost wholly to the
greatly increased output of the factory; and
the directors give home manufacture this
testimonial: —
The policy followed during the war, of increasing
prices only when absolutely necessary, has been con-
tinued ; so that plates, papers, films and cameras are
still being sold here at considerably lower prices than
are ruling in England, <
Another view of the co.’s progress can
be got from this comparison :
1909. 1917. 1918.

The co. started with a paid capital of
£150,000, and shareholders have put up
nothing further since, though capital has
been increased by the transfer of £30,000
from reserves. In the 94 years covered by
the figures, liabilities have increased by
£48,871. Properties and buildings alone now
stand in the books at £48,537 more, after
the directors have clipped 2 p.c. off the
value of the buildings each year—an unusual
but wise proceeding. Meantime £148,500 has
been added to stocks and £20,902 to other
more or less liquid assets, while plant, though
extensive additions have been made, now
stands at £9825 less than formerly owing to
the very liberal allowances made for de-
preciation. Shareholders have done exceed-
ingly well. On their original capital they
have received in the 10 years divs. totalling'
over 172 p.c., and even then there has been
left in the business an amount some
thousands in excess of the £150.000 capital
they started with. Nowadays there are net
assets of a book value of almost £1 17s.
to represent each £1 share (including, of
course, the bonus shares) ; and excepting
possibly stock (which, no doubt, has been
accumulated at war prices), they are a
particularly solid lot of assets. There are
very few balance-sheets which indicate so
clearly as Kodak’s what the directors are
doing in the matter of depreciation. Further,
not a penny is represented by goodwill,
trade-marks or processes, though at the
moment they are undoubtedly worth a lot.
The co. lately acquired 120 acres at Kew
(Vie.) where a model factory suburb is to
be laid out.

The two features of that last line are: (1)
the record profit; (2) the fact that the
whole of the capital is now represented by
tangible assets, with a margin to spare of
£3022. Ten years ago, when the co. issued
its first report, £7500 of the paid-up capital
of £18,125 was represented by “rights” and
£2680 by preliminary exes. As the business
expanded a further £8671 was absorbed in
handling debenture issues and conversions.
The co. was formed under arrangement with
the Balmain municipality, whereby in re-
turn for the destruction of garbage it was
given the right to supply light and power;
and the co.’s special Act permitted it to
extend its mains. Other municipalities have
signed up with the Corporation, on terms
“not less favorable than those offered bv the
Sydney City Council Electric Light.’' De-
partment for similar services.” These muni-
cipalities have the right to take over the
whole outfit on a valuation basis at stated
periods; and last year there was a move-
ment on the part of several councils to
acquire the business. A couple* of the
municipalities, however, were hard-up, and
the others apparently decided that even if
they could find the money they probably
could not provide any more efficient service,
while the risk (especially with an uncertain
labor outlook) was too great. But the fact
that the local governing bodies have the
power to take over the plant, and also that
the co. must not charge more than the City
Council (which isn’t out for dividends)
would be prepared to do the business for,
makes it very unlikely that this private
concern will ever grow very fat. Nevertheless
the net profit for the past half-year shows
a return on capital of over 13 p.c. p.a..
and considering the fact that a lot of capital
is at present in War Loan and deposits,
awaiting the time when further supplies
can be made, the chances are the co. will
show still better results later on. If the
profits increase to any extent the directors
will probably do shareholders a good turn
if they voluntarily cut their rates to
customers a trifle. It may save a lot of
trouble later on.
The co.’s balance-sheet is quite healthy.
Paid capital is £253,113 and there are
debentures (mostly bearing 51 p.c.) totalling
£54,400. All other liabilities amount to
only £7OOB. On the other side £4014 has
been put into land and £298,147 into plant
and machinery, against which there is a
depreciation reserve of £46,117 —an allow-
ance more liberal than American life-tables
for this industry prescribe. The co. has
£11.311 in stocks and £59,604 in cash, War
Loan and sundry creditors.

and there are many temporary advantages
in a return to the old system. On the other
side there is the direct profit to be derived
trem the sale of refined metal, the develop-
ment and demand for science and brains,
the establishment of large, permanent
industries instead of evanescent ones,
and the realisation of as much benefit as

Gordon Lyon’s Adelong Creek (N.S.W.)
staggers heavily in the wake of its lusty
son, the Estates, which is working higher
up stream. Certainly the Creek’s two
plants are not over-big, but that doesn’t
explain why the yardage keeps so low. The
trick in dredging is to plug through stuff.
Otherwise costs mount up and profits don’t
collect in the riffles. A year back No. 1
plant showed • that in six months it- could
get through 338,900 cub. yds. at an
average cost of 2.93 d. per cub. yd., while
No. 2 handled 266,600 cub. yds. for 4.2 d.
per yd. For the half-year to November
last No. 1 plant shot its bolt with 239,800
cub yds. costing 4.48 d. per yd., while No. 2
bucketed 272,700 cub. yds.' for 3.71 d. per
yd. The result was a net profit of only
£3OO for the six months. The falling off
in profits recently has been startling: Nov.
’l7, £4813; May, ’lB, ' £1887; Nov., ’lB,
£3OO. The obvious cause is the decline in
the value of the grotind, as No. 1 plant ran
down to s id. wash and No. 2 couldn’t rake
up more than 3.83 d. dirt. Just now, how-
ever, better yields are showing up from
both dredges, and as the co. still lias some
cash in the till a few divs. may be possible.
* * * ‘ *
A couple of weeks ago the bears got on to
Gundagai Long Tunnel (N.S.W.), and 10s.
shares, which had been up to 14s. 6d.,
bumped to 4s. Bd. L. F. Harper, of the
Mines Department, had a look over the
show, and his report only added to the
gloom. Further, H. W. Cosgrove, under- i
ground manager for five months until he re- i
contly dropped out (put it that way if you :
like), rushes in to say (1) that values in
the winze below 300 ft. were under 2dwt.;
(2) the 100 ft. driven on the bottom level
won’t go a ’weight; and that (3) “there was
not, on January 1, a face of ore with a re-
coverable value of sdwt. per ton over a
length of 20ft. and a stoping width of 4ft.”
Harper weighs in with the opinion:—
Should the contact zone material, as a whole, have
even low gold values, an enormous tonnage is avail-
able, and the average yields should he kept up by a
judicious blending of the rich ore, but the outlook
for this ia not favorable at the present time.
Samples from the contact zone at the sur-
face gave “uniformly poor results,” and
another taken “right across the face”, at No.
3 letel, just under the winze from No. 2, in
which a rich make of ore was proved, showed
only a trace of gold. Further: —
That the ore bodies are going down was proved in
the winze above referred to. The size of the rich pipe
vet remains to be proved, but it certainly does not
persist through to No. 3 1eve1....N0 evidence could
be obtained which would afford definite information
as to the position of new makes. Apparently the
future of the mine depends upon the size and fre-
quency of occurrence of these rich makes.
All this provides sad reading for sharehold-
ers who recall the report of two years ago by
manager Geo. Hepburn. He came from the
Boddington (W.A.), and “guaranteed” that
the 300 ft. level from his proposed new shaft
would “open up enormous bodies of payable
ore,” and that “shareholders may with
reasonable confidence expect regular divs. for
years to come.” Still, Harper’s “revela-
tions” do not come as a surprise; for values
at the Long Tunnel have always been
erratic. The gold is largely found alongside
reefs, in big formations, ranging from 10ft.
to 20ft. and upwards. Pittman, years ago,
pointed out that most of the rich patches
were to be found in the neighborhood of the
crosscourses intersecting the lode at nearly
right angles. Some of these patches were
wonderfully rich: 3001 b. dirt gave 3500 z.;
another 3001 b., 500 oz.; And 2cwt., 900 oz.
Optimists who know the country anticipated
another bonanza in the vicinity of the winze
between 300 ft. and 380 ft., but if it really
does exist it will probably be 20ft. or
so further south, and the picks are working
to find out.
The Long Tunnel is suffering from the
usual complaint—the battery is ahead of the
mine. There are only two working faces:
one at 300 ft., the, other in the south face at
380 ft. John Allen, who sampled the show
some years ago for an English syndicate
which offered the previous owner, George
Rice, £30,000 (and was refused), has been
called in to have a look. His report will
be ready at the end of this week. The bat.
tery is also to nave a word —the clean-up is
announced for the end of the month. Mean-
time the bears are having the time of their
lives.
Butler's Tin, Torringtun (N.S.W.), has
been gasping for water. So despite a record
market the working account for the half-
year to November 30 is credited with only
£705, compared with £4401 for the previous
term, and £1390 for the November half,
1917. The result was a working loss of
£3431. The mill has now been idle almost
seven months; but there have been other
troubles besides the drought. When T.
Hooper took over the management in Aug-
ust last he found “it would be impossible
to carry on the stoping, even supposing
water was available for the mill, without
filling up the stopes with mullock.” This
work has now been done as far as practic-
able. The N. S. Wales Mines Department
came to the aid of the co. six months ago
and subsidised 100 ft. of driving south-west
in No. 2 level and the sinking of a winze
below the No. 3 level. The 100 ft. of driving
has disclosed nothing of any great value,
the lode averaging 34ft., “showing tin all
the way, but nothing very rich.” Hooper
recommends getting to work to determine the
size of the lodes in the stopes of the three
levels. However, he is ready to set the mill
running three shifts throughout this year
as soon as rain comes. Meantime the finan-
cial position is becoming less cheerful. The
50,000 shares were recently increased from
20s. to 305.. and calls have brought in £2692,
while £S32 was outstanding at November 30.
The co. owed £6262, including £2305 still
owing to vendors, and had only £Bl9 in
more or less liquid assets, apart from plant.
Still, its past record and .Tames Lewis’s re-
cent report both confirm the possibilities of
this complex show.
* * * *
Bendigo Amalgamated’s final kick for 1918
was 34970 z„ including plates, from 6531
tons. Carlisle, Constellation, the Nelson and
the Northern w T ere the' best mining props.
The co. needs to handle twice or three times
the foregoing tonnage ; then, with the same
average yield, a bit of cash would flow back
to contributors. In Dyifson’s opinion bet-
ter stone is opening out at 2100 ft. on the
Victory, back in the Carlisle .western ground
off the south stopes, and as fair prospects
show in the western make of spurs cut into
off the Unity shaft at 1255 ft., the chances
are that both the Carlisle and the Unity will
be able to supply a lot of crush dirt during
the current year. In the Constellation the
stone from the west leg of the South Moon
saddle is making up for the poor stuff in the
Pyke reef, on which work has had to be
stopped. Then, too, chortling is going on
over the better stone in the Derby line at
1225 ft. from the Nelson shaft. This is off a
strong easterly fault, and stone worth up
to 50s. is being trucked. No one is
able to size up this development yet, for
the working is far below anything done on
the Derby line north and south of the shaft.
However, the miner is being hustled into
the south workings.
* * * *
Mine-owners in the West are bitterly com-
plaining about the further increased price of
explosives—all imported. Those whose con-
tracts have run out have had to pay since
the beginning of the year an additional
21s. 6d. per case. With the armistice it was
thought that a more plentiful supply of
explosives would be available and prices
would come down instead of going up.
Some struggling claim-holders are consider-
ing the matter of asking the Government
to come to their assistance. There would,
however, be no cause to growl if the mine-
owner had adopted The Bitlletin’vS sugges-
tion and established a co-operative body to
purchase stores on his behalf. Now the in-
dustry is almost down and out the proposal
is to be considered at the forthcoming
Melbourne conference.
* * * *
The New Wallsend Collieries (W.A.) pro-
ject is a far-away pasture that looks dis-
tinctly green. The block is over 2800 acres
in size and covers the area in which the old
Wallsend seam, cut off by a fault, was lost.
W. D. Bedlington at last traced a 12ft.
seam by putting down hand-bores, and it
was to find capital to work this that a West-
ralian crowd came east a while ago. Terms
don’t seem outrageous, for all the water
squeezed is 8000 paids and £2OOO cash, leav-
ing 22,000 £1 shares to be placed. To find
machinery the co. is to allot 10,000 ordi-
nary shares to the Western Machinery Co.,
Ltd., but the terms have to be looked up in
a Perth solicitor’s office instead of being set
out in the prospectus.
Ida H. (W.A,), which is little heard of
nowadays, has issued a report in London
covering three years to June 30 last. A
receiver was appointed in Oct., 1916, and a
year later the Australian creditors were paid
off, largely with the proceeds of the sale of
57,000 Nigerian tin shares. The accounts
aow show a debit balance of £11,950. The
,show is at present being tributed on a 10 p.c.
royalty basis, and the manager reports that
this arrangement is profitable to the co.
Burma Corporation, the silver-lead concern
in which a lot of Lake View and Oroya capi-
tal is sunk, shows receipts amounting to
£796,200 for last year, an increase of £271.300
on the previous 12 months. Despite diffi-
culties in working occasioned by the war, the
ratio of expenditure was reduced from 63.71
to 60.24 p.c., the net surplus running out at
£158,500. The co., however, has so far not
distributed profits and will probably not
do so for some time to come, as big plant
expansion is contemplated. Ore reserves are
estimated at 4,138,000 tons, assaying 24.60 z.
silver, 27.2 p.c. lead, 18.9 p.c. zinc and 0.7
p.c. copper.
Mararoa (W.A.) is busy straightening
up tlie workings and adding to the plant
on its new show at Cue. Considerable
savings are expected to be effected as a
result of alterations and additions, and
when everything is in full swing develop-
ment will go ahead. About the first Work
of importance to be undertaken will be the
further opening up of the rich shoot in
the lower levels, from which the late owners
got most of their gold. The northern end,
where good values were formerly obtained,
is also to receive attention.

North Mt. Farrell (Tas.), having been
taken over by the Byron Moore and Day
(Melbourne) crowd, has issued the last
accounts from the old Speck headquarters at
Devonport. Although ore worth £12,706 was
got out during the half-year to Oct., P. and
L. account benefited by only £B7O. The co.
had to hand over £2485 to the Federal
Treasurer for 1915-’l6 war profits, and
£558 additional was due when the books
were closed. However, the co. still had ore
valued at £11,218 to be settled for. That,
with other liquid assets, gave £14,243
against debts of £11,579, so there isn’t much
of a margin to play with.
* * * *
“Traveller”: Tripping down the east
coast of Tasmania one learns why the cash
shovelled into coal-mining was never re-
covered. From St. Helens to the Schoutens
not a single harbor exists wherein a decent-
sized boat can shelter. The one possible
point, is at Seymour. There, 50 years back,
two jetties tried to serve old-time believers
in the Speck’s, coal seams, but Neptune bat-
tered one to pieces and tolerates only a few
piles in the other. Daltnayne has tried its
luck four miles further north, but the sea-
end of its jetty is now hobbled down by a
big mooring chain. This leaves barrister
Morley’s new co. still with the shipping
problem to handle. Down Bicheno way
McWilliams, M.H.R., is awaiting calm
weather and a steamer to test a bulk lot of
coal dug out from near the Douglas River.
Then, 30 miles south, some of old Bill Lyne’s
folk have driven under Mt. Paul on a 10ft.
seam ; but, bar Dalmayne, sufficient work has
not been done anywhere to disprove old
statistician Johnson’s declaration that the
Speck coal measures came into the world
2000 years too late to fight against the older
fuels of the Greta and Newcastle seams.

“Bim” offers his prescription:—
Standard gold is worth £3 17s. IOJd. per oz. at the
Mint, and fetches up to £6anoz. in India, China and
Japan. There is an embargo on the export of gold
from Australia, and the Chamber of Mines thinks
if it were removed our gold-producers would be
able to take advantage of the higher price which Is
paid outside. And the Chamber means to ask for
a Government subsidy on gold production. Both
well and good’ and still quite unlikely to 're-open
the low-giade mines of Australia, where nr'llions of
tons of poor dirt carrying gold are waiting cheap
modern methods of' treatment. A subsidy of ss.
or 10s. an oz. would be ineffective and trifling. The
Mint should buy local gold at the price it will
fetch in Calcutta or Tokio, and it would then pay
to re-open gold-mines all over Australia. The
bookish critic will point out the ill effect of such
action on the value of the sovereign and the Bank
of England note. That ought not to concern Aus-
tralians, because Britain is big enough to look after
the welfare of her pound sterling, and does not need
our aid, although she will badly want our gold before
long. The easiest way to settle this difficult ques-
tion would be to restore silver to its old tie with
gold on the basis of a ratio of 15 to I—-15oz. of
silver to equal one oz. of gold—in the settlement of
all debts, domestic and international. If it had not
been for Bismarck destroying silver as money by
putting Germany on a gold standard in 1873, the
world would still have been using the gold-silver
standard. There is not enough gold in the world
to act as a sort of continental shelf to support
the mighty mass of paper recently' reared upon it.
For 5673 years silver was a monetary standard along
with gold, and it would hurt neither individuals nor
nations to return to the double standard. It would
help Australia as a debtor nation, which, however, is
another story.
There have been times when gold, instead of
being very cheap and buying very little, was
very dear and bought a great deal. There
have been times, for instance, when, instead
of getting only 121 b. or 151 b. of wool for his
sovereign, the gold-mine owner could com-
mand _4olb. This paper doesn’t remember
the gold-mine owners offering to do anything
to ease that situation. The present situation
is admittedly very rough on the gold-miner.
But every situation is likely to be rough
on somebody; and the simple truth is that,
compared with others —base metal miners,
for instance —the gold-mine owner has been
extraordinarily fortunate. Perhaps that is
why he feels so sore now.

■ are native-born Australians. Holman (Ma

* Captain Hancock, just dead in Adelaide
at 82, was for a generation S. Australia’s
best-known mining man. He came from
Cornwall, of course, had a small job at the
start, and finished up with many years’
service as general manager of amalgamated
Wallaroo and Moonta. If there had been
no other brains in the world the “king of
Munta” might not have been much of a
potentate. His strong point was his ability
to assemble the little bits of knowledge of two
or three or 50 men, and put the result to
good use. Probably his biggest service to
the copper-mines was the introduction of
the system of driving levels every 15 fath-
oms instead of every 10, as prescribed by
the old Cornish plans. He did something
valuable £or Broken Hill, too, for though he
never managed a mine there he and Green-
way were the first men to try on a commer-
cial scale the present system of concentrat-
ing sulphide ores—or the crude thing that
developed into the present vast industry.
Edward Mitchell, who died the other day
in Melbourne, aged 98, was a member of
the Burke and Wills expedition in 1864.
But William Cant of Westralia is our
oldest surviving explorer. He accompanied
the Austin party in its exploration of the
Murchison in 1854. Mr. Cant lives in
the Geraldton district and is reputed to be
in his 100th year.
Bigjohn Forrest’s widow is returning to
Westralia ; and all that remains of Bigjohn
himself will return shortly afterwards for a
public funeral.
Edward Mulcahy, the new Tasmanian
Senator—he takes Long’s place—is a Limer-
ick man, born in 1850, and proud of it that
he worked for his living at 14. Hie has
talked for it pretty constantly for the past
27 years.
John Butler Wood, pioneer of the Bland
country (N.S.W.), who died recently when
within coo-ee of his hundredth birthday, was
son of John Wood, one-time a director of
the Bank of England, who came with his
family to Sydney about 1814. He brought
coin which he invested in land and stock on
the Nepean. Soon after his friend Mac-
quarie had crossed the Mountains Wood
took up land at Hartley Vale. Later, he
secured Wangoola, on the Lachlan, at wkat
is now Cowra. The son just dead, while
in his teens, was sent to manage the station,
and did a good deal of pioneering, eventu-
ally selecting Brundah, which the family
held for many years.
There’s a man nursing two stumps in the
Adelaide Hospital who deserves something
better. He forgot bis age in August, 1914
—forgot that he was with the Black Watch
in the Soudan 32 years before; forgot that
he held the long-service medal (30 years).
He just joined up, was at the Landing, and
a few months later —being then Sergeant
Peter Molloy—had to be sent back home
with a shattered foot. One leg had to come
off; and, recently, the other. But the old
warrior isn’t complaining. He has plenty,
he says, to comfort him —including the
Queen’s Medal with six bars and four boys
in khaki, the youngest of them with a
D.C.M.
W. C. Hill, who died in Sydney recently
at 81, was an Australian, and so were his
father and mother. The father, one of the
earliest white settlers, was Richard Hill, of
Bent-street. A quaint little cottage that still
buttresses the Australian Club was his
home. The mother was a daughter of
Francis Cox; another daughter married
William Charles Wentworth, the “father of
his country.” W. C. Hill was one of the
original partners in the stock and station
firm of Maiden, Hill and Clark, which later
became Hill, Clark and Co.
Edward Dyson has “Hello, Soldier!” a
book of war-verse ready, or just about ready,
for the book-sellers and the book-buyers.
“Hello, Soldier!” sub-titled “Khaki Verse,”
is unique in one particular at least, being a
famine-time volume printed on brown paper.
The illustrations, by Will Dyson, Ruby
Lindsay and George Dancey. are on a chance
supply of suitable paper, tipped in,
and the verses are drawn from Dyson’s large
Bulletin resources in the main, material
written in the course of the last four years.
The author claims that the avidity with
which his rhymes have been prigged all over
the English-speaking parts justifies the
assumption that they meet a want.
Amongst those who went down in the
United Kingdom elections is Dr. W. A.
Chappie, formerly of Wellington (M.L.). He
had a brief experience (five months) in the
M.L. Reps, as member for Tuapeka, but he
brought no light to dispel the political
gloom. A wealthy man, he went to Scotland
and found his way into the House of Com-
mons as Liberal member for Stirling at the
general election of 1910. He held the seat
until the fight at Stirling the other day,
when he was descried at the bottom of the
poll. He is 55 and a native-born Maori-
lunder.
President Wilson is believed to be the
only leading statesman who is a competent
typist. He delights in a battered old “grid
which he carries with him everywhere,
clattering it even in the train. The President
rarely uses a pen, and composes. all his
speeches, messages and other public docu-
ments straight off on the sausage machine.
“B.”: On one occasion Shiels, when a
Minister, tried to conciliate Robert Harper
by walking over to the Opposition side of
the Victorian House to explain away a mis-
apprehension. Harper turned his back, and
Shiels was his enemy thereafter. It was
David Gaunson who nicknamed him “Chic-
ory Bob.” Isaacs, when State Attorney-
General, hit him hard on one occasion.
While opposing a measure providing for one-
man-one-vote, Harper, who had been baiting
Isaacs, quoted a German or French writer,
and remarked: “Perhaps the Attorney-
General has heard of that authority?” “Not
as you pronounce it,” flashed Isaacs, and,
the Housq sniggered.
Dr. Gibney, formerly R.C. Bishop of
Perth, celebrated his 82nd birthday recently.
He retains much of his old vigor, and his
interest in the problems of the present keeps
him thinking hard. But history may prefer
to write his name as that of the bold priest
who was present at the capture of the
Kellys.
Adelaide Register staff, which claims to
retain its men in active work longer than
any other paper in Australia, has been con-
gratulating Robert Rusk on half a century
in the office —45 years of it on continuous
night-work. Among those who spoke were
J. S. Knox, also of 50 years’ standing in
the office, and C. H. Franklin, of 60, still
going strong.
THE JUDGMENT OF PARIS.
PEARCE: "You fellows are all right; but just

One of the best-known photographers in
the Commonwealth, W. H. Hammer, died at
Norwood (S.A.) the other day. He was
the principal of the old-established firm of
Hammer and Co. and partner and director
of Studio, Limited. Born at St. Austell,
Cornwall, 74 years ago. he came to South
Aus. in 1880 and started in the photo,
business. In England he was prominent as
an organist and conductor of choirs' and
bands; but £ s. d. claimed his time in
Adelaide.
General Rosenthal, just made a knight, left
Sydney with the First (Artillery) Division
as Brigade-Major. He had been a C.M.F.
soldier before. After the Evacuation he
created the Fourth Divisional Artillery—
practically out of nothing. He just settled
down in a tent at Tel-el-Kebir and manu-
factured gunners and drivers from Infantry
and Light Horse. Not five per cent, of his
then officers had any artillery experience at
all. Yet three months later he delivered the
Division in France, equipped and organised
and ready to go into the line. “Rosey” or
“Bubbly,” as lie is variously called, hardly
seems to know the meaning of fear or
fatigue. He is 6ft. 2in. and heavily built,
yet every day in France, wherever his H.Q.
were, he started to go round his batteries by
sunrise. The country, with its bottomless
mud and endless shell-holes, has to be seen
before Rosenthal’s daily feat can be appre-
ciated. He has been wounded oftener than
any other officer of his rank.
Alex. Cameron is one of the tipped for
the chairmanship of Melbourne Tramway
Board, in the gift of the Victorian Govern-
ment. He is a lawyer and was a councillor
of Malvern. When the electric tramway to
Prahran was projected he studied street-
transit matters and became chairman of the
Trust at £3OO a year, rising in these later
days to £lOOO. His former official colleague.
West O. Strangward, secretary of the same
Trust, is the secretary now of the Tramway
Board.

It is now announced that Fred K. Watson,
M.A., B.Sc. (England), has received an
appointment in the Ma State in connection
with the Yanco irrigation scheme. This
young man, after doing brilliantly in the
Adelaide School of Mines and Roseworthy
College (S.A.)', studied civil engineering,
agriculture and arts at Edinburgh, where he
graduated with special distinction. Then
the S.A. Government offered him a position
at a salary which an unskilled laborer would
strike over. Still the S.A. Government has
some money to spend, as evidenced by the
£250 per month paid to P. A. Anthony to
report on the Railways. But lie was im-
ported—from the little Malay States—and
that increases a man’s value wonderfully.

Just as England produced McCuddin as
successor to Ball, so Canada has developed
Barker, Y.C., D.S.O. and bar, M.C. and
two bars, Croix de Guerre, and the Italian
silver medal for valor, as the heir to the
laurels of its air-king Bishop. Starting
with the Canadian infantry in ’l4, Barker
fought at the second battle of Ypres, and
has been iat it ever since. In his first flight
as a pilot his was the only ’bus to land
after a stunt in which four British machines
were downed by a sudden attack while
they were observing for artillery. Later
he went to Italy in charge of a “camel”
squadron and did great work. Then things
became dull on the Italian front and Barker
forfeited his temporary rank of major to
return to France as a squadron oommander.
There be won the only remaining military
honor available.
“Marqui” on a big dead man ;—■
Roosevelt in late years carefully prepared his
speeches and delivered rather than read them from
manuscript. As he finished with each sheet he cast
it from him with an almost contemptuous flip. His
voipe was a light one though it carried well. When
he was particularly scornful it soared to a falsetto
as when, in the last Presidential campaign, he re-
vealed an intimately guarded secret of Newton D.
Baker’s life. Roosevelt alluded to his victim as
“Mr. Baker, the Secretary for War, an amiable
gentleman who knits.” Of course it got the crowd.
When Hughes—the Yankee Hughes—was accepted as
a Republican candidate in preference to Teddy, the
latter was reported as saying, having in view
Hughes’s non-committal attitude on the war, that
he didn’t see much difference between Hughes and
Wilson excepting the whiskers. The newspapers glee-
fully handed on this sting until someone, on Roose-
velt’s behalf, repudiated it. But it sounded authen-
tic.
HERE—AND THERE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is raining hard in Ploegsteert Wood
(I know it’s raining—it always did) ;
And round the shell-holes and through the mud
The Belgian farm-carts slither and skid.
But I am lying on Manly Beach,
Watching and smelling the vast clean sea,
The air is warm and the sky is blue —
It is more than good enough for me.
In the shattered streets of old Bailleul
The townsfolk wander in angry grief,
Noting the work of the German guns,
Tracing the path of the German thief;
But I can travel my city through /
And not be met by a scratch or scar;
A gracious thing when one thinks of towns —
Ruins of towns —like Hamel and Pas.
In filth and darkness and stinking slime
The French are draining and building hard,
While I am watching a tennis match
Or marking a Randwick racing card.
The yachts are dancing off Middle Head,
Their white sails shine and their pennons fly—
And the broken stone that once was Ypres
Stares up at the leaden Flanders sky.
It’s good to be safely back at home
And know the things that Australia missed;
To live where men have the heart to play
And laughing, trouble-free girls are kissed;
But on beach and links and course and courts
My mind, with a sort of nagging pain,
Keeps turning, turning to spots I know
Where no one will play or laugh again.
N.S.W.
SARI BAIR.

SOCIETY
MINCHINBURY
The story comes from Ivew (where some
of the Naicest Melbourne lunatics are
housed) that an old resident of the asylum
lately failed in his duty of driving a covered
van, containing beer and other refreshments,
to his hay-making brethren in the paddock.
The waggish patient drove himself and the
beer to Bendigo, where he had a happy two
weeks before the warders recovered possession
of him. The point of the yarn is in the
means the genial escapee adopted to avoid
detection. The covered van had ASYLUM
KEW painted rather conspicuously upon it.
The driver obliterated the letters ASY with
axle-grease, and travelled to Bendigo in
the supposed capacity of LUM KEW, a
Cbow trader.

“Senior Officer” from France :—-
Down to the signing of the armistice the A.I.F.
had been awarded 11,067 British distinctions and
557 foreign—2l9 of them Belgian and 190 French.
This tally does not include naval decorations or
1914-15 stars, information as to which is not avail-
able yet. The return will dispel the illusion that
foreign decorations are given away freely to our
troops. If you ever see a Billjim with a foreign
ribbon you can bet he is an outsize soldier, as it
would ‘probably only be given him on account of
his failure to get a British reward after repeated
recoihmendations. This is not the case with officers.
If you observe an officer who wears a foreign ribbon
and make inquiries, you will find that he got it
whilst on the staff or whilst he was adjutant. The
common or garden regimental officer —the man who
does the bulloeking in the field —never gets a chance
to acquire one. He lives too far away from the
office. The most decorated member of the A.I.F.
is, of course, “Birdy.” But the dinkum Anzac
who wears most is either Colonel Murray or Colonel
Wilder Neligan. Each left' Australia as an n.c.o.
The former has Y.C., bar to D. 5.0., D. 5.0., D.C.M,
.and Croix de Guerre; the latter has C.M.G., bar to
D. 5.0., D. 5.0., D.C.M., and Croix de Guerre. The
scarcest of the lot of medals i-s the bar to the
D.C.M., of which only 16 have been won; and you
can safely say each of these has been granted when
a V.C. has been recommended. The equivalent of
this in the case of an officer is the D.S.O. to a
lieutenant. Despite all this, judge not a man by
his ribbons. Many a red-liatted hero with a breast
full of ribbons has never heard a shot fired, and
•doesn't intend to.
* * • *
It begins to look as though Australia had
its leg well pulled lately; and it may be a
good thing that it had. Dr. Purser, who
stands so high professionally that he is vice-
; chancellor of Sydney University, writes,
now that the scare is over, that "all medical
men know there was a great increase in
the number of cases of epidemic influenza
in N. S. Wales during Sept., Oct. and
November especially, and the type of influ-
enza was more potent than probably since
the invasion of 1891, more severe complica-
tions occurring and many more deaths en-
sued.” Dr. Purser doesn’t say that all
the talk about “Spanish” ’flu’ having been
kept out was moonshine; but “examina-
tion of the sputum revealed exactly the same
germs [as those from the quarantine cases],
the germs staining in exactly the same man-
ner, and under the microscope were exactly
the same, and the culture growths were the
same.” Maybe, of course, the ’flu’ from
which a few people suffered and one or two
died at the Sydney quarantine station was
not “Spanish” ’flu’ either; and in that case
what was kept out was the thing that was
in alreadv.

“Wealsman” from Hobart: —
Our first mob of Anzaes to return—about Armis-
tice Day—got a tremendous reception. The next
lot failed to excite quite so much enthusiasm, though
the bands played “Johnny Comes Marching”
and “Conquering Hero” as vigorously as ever and
the rose-leaves almost bogged the wheels of the
n.ctor-cars. The third lot got a very fair reception;
the fourth batch a middling one; the fifth crowd
and the sixth received a welcome in a slowly de-
scending scale of magnificence. The last lot billed ,
to arrive, owing to a bungle in the arrangements
for their embarkation in Melbourne, didn’t turn
up till two days later. Meantime, the indignant
reception committee had resigned as a protest
against military muddling, and a scratch team,
headed by the Mayor, was only prevailed upon to
welcome the incoming drove of Anzaes and wounded
men because some large person pointed out how hurt
poor B.illjim would be if the Mayor didn’t welcome
him home with a speech and a brass band and flags
and fuss. The scratch team did as well as could
be expected, and most of the citizens who stayed
away had the good manners to wave a flag or a
handkerchief or the old man’s shirt from an upstairs
window as the procession crawled by; so Billjim
was quite satisfied. He will probably feel just as
satisfied if the motors and the flowers and the
speeches are cut out altogether. Though none of
them appear to have so far expressed rude dis-
approval of the proceedings, it is easy to see that
most of them would prefer something less showy
and more substantial by way of a welcome home.
A good many of them would rather the Mayor or
an old cobber should meet them quietly at the end
of the train journey and hand them a steady job at
standard wages.
* * * *
“Gypo” : As an officer in the Australian
Naval Transport Service for close on three
years, I can speak with fair authority, and
I agree to a certain extent that the Diggers
have grievances. All the food troubles
originate in the stewards’ and storekeeper’s
department. These people are out for all they
can get, and if they see an opportunity to
make a few pounds by cutting down the
Digger’s rations and selling what they save,
they’ll do it. Here are a few examples of
what took place on a transport carrying 1500
troops:—■
0) When the men asked for more meat at the
mess they were informed that they’d had their share,
but on slipping round to the galley they found they
could get plates of meat at Is. a time.
(2) The hot fresh-water tap outside the galley
was padlocked and hot water (for shaving) sold
at 3d. per mug.
(3) Steak and chips were sold at night at Is. 3d.
per plate.
(4) Tins of jam and milk were sold on the quiet.
Then, again. Billjim invariably complains of
the differential treatment of himself and the
officers in the matter of liquor. It was the
lot of a certain 1200 Anzaes to be at sea
when news of the armistice rang out. Many
of the officers * were “three sheets in the
wind” not long after, while the Diggers
looked on from afar off and muttered, “Half
your luck !” Even if the men get the stuff
from “somewhere,” they are fleeced for it.
Transports should be made either wet or
dry; they certainly shouldn’t be wet for some
and dry for others.
LOOKING AHEAD.
“ Well, how do you like your new mother ?”
“Not too had. I hope the newt one is as good.”
One Billjim at least has an idea that they
carry the coupon business too far in Eng-
land. This romantic warrior, with his lady,
wandered into Hyde Park, where- —though
they didn’t know it —a charge is made for
the chairs. The lady perched on his knee.
Just as she had dropped her head on his
neck a harsh voice from the fog asked,
“Have you got your tickets?” “Cripes!” re-
plied the Digger. “D’you collect a tax on
this, too?”
* * * *
“Bad Australian”: Some of the biggest
Australian mistakes weren’t even original.
When Bartolome Perestrello set out to col-
onise the island of Espiritu Santo, shortly
before the discovery of the larger island of
Madeira, he took among other live stock a
female rabbit, which after a few days at
sea brought forth a family. This was hailed
by the Dagoes as an omen that the expedi-
tion would increase and multiply and wax
fat in the Land of Promise. The omen
panned out in a way. The rabbits increased
to such an extent that whatever the settlers
planted was eaten off as soon as it showed
above ground. At the end of two years
rabbits were “a pestilential and irremediable
plague” and the pioneers were starved out
and forced to abandon the island and return
to Portugal.

peash only comes once a year.”

“Gouger”: Two days after the battle of
Bir-el-Abd our regiment was watering its
horses at one of the small oases about 12
miles from the battlefield, when someone
called my attention to a riderless horse,
saddle on and bridle dangling, coming across
the desert. The poor beast must have
smelt the water, for he stopped, pricked his
ears, and, catching sight of our thirsty
horses pressing eagerly around the well,
neighed shrilly, and came across the sand at
full gallop. He dashed among a group of
horses, knocked one man backwards down
the shallow hole, and thrust his nose in a
canvas bucket of water that one of the boys
was holding out for his own horses. It took
14 buckets of water before we could get any
control over the thirst-maddened animal,
which, by this time, had been recognised as
belonging to one of the men- missing since
the battle. There was dried blood all over
the saddle, and big patches of dried blood
down the horse’s neck, showing that a despe-
rately wounded man had clung there. “I’ll
take the poor beggar back to camp and give
him a feed,” said one of the lads. He mounted
his horse, and catching hold of the riderless
horse’s bridle rein, tried to lead him towards
the camp. But the horse was of another
mind. Ha walked very slowly, continually
looking around the way he had come, and
whinnying. Finally he stopped altogether.
The man leading him, getting impatient,
jerked the bridle. The horse reared, the
bridle fell, and off went the charger at a
tearing gallop the way he had come. Sev-
eral men went after him, but it turned out to
be a 10-mile ride before he stopped. When
they caught up, the waler was standing
quite still beside his rider. The man was

dead, but went west before the ambulance

SUMMER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Nt>w in her latest frock of golden hue,
Bedecked with filmy frolic of love-lace
And looped with laughter, Summer flaunts her
grace,
Her eyes mischievous melodies of blue.
Her fragrant hair with many a starshine pin
And clasp of sunlight doth the fair jade bind
To poets prating passion down the wind,
What time She lures churchwardens into sin.
Behind her flirted fan of whisp’ring trees
She sits and slyly ogles you and me,
Here where we watch the wide smile of the sea
And' dodge the moisture of the surf’s salt sneeze.
Within the scented hush of this deep shade
How might we rest and let the world slip by—
A' murm’rous dream beneath the glow-lit sky—
Were Summer your sole chaperon, sweet maid !
N.S.W.
HELEN BURKE.

“Bully Beef”: Every “picture” yarn that
deals with the Western Front fails to con-
vince in important particulars. In “The
Heart of the World” there is gas, but no
gas-masks. With gas coming over in clouds
the Highland piper stands blowing into his
dreadful instrument on the parapet. The
battalion goes over the top without masks;
and the batteries shoot without masks. In
real warfare xro one is allowed to be a min-
ute in or near a front-line trench without
carrying his respirator at “the alert” — i.e.,
under his chin and ready to be dashed on
at a second’s notice. Even the Australian
newspaper men who made a dash or two
to the Front (or thereabouts) had, immedi-
ately on landing in France, to go through
gas-drill at Boulogne; and their masks were
part of them till they left Boulogne again.
In the newest Chaplin film there is one mask,
and Charlie has it. But even that one is a
conti’ivance on the lines of the “P. 8.,” which
went out of date early in 191 S. In no pic-
ture that I have seen is there anything re-
sembling a real shell-hole. “The Heart of
the World” had one shell-hole, but it had
obviously been constructed with a spade.
The Chaplin fake has none. The notable
feature of all the town and village scenes, in
all the films, is the absence of devotional
pictures and crucifixes. In France, and even
more so in Belgium, it is practically impos-
sible to find a room without half a dozen pic-
tures of the Saviour and two or three cruci-
fixes.
* » * *
Most drivers of vehicles carry lights at
night for their own protection’ because there
is no knowing when an excited horse or a
depraved motor- may come along and spill
them into the gutter; but as a certain per-
centage. of every generation are born idiots,
Parliaments have passed laws ordering
lights to he carried between certain hours.
In Victoria the local statesmen passed on
the administration of the Act to Bumble,
told him he could pay a constable not more
than £25 a year out of the rates to see that
the regulations were carried out, and then
disconnected themselves from the matter.
The other day a Niinawading (Vic.) woman
lost her life indirectly through a cyclist rid-
ing without a light, and then it was discov-
ered that nobody need carry any illumina-
tion in Nunawading unless he felt so dis-
posed, there being nobody to see that he did it.
The shire council had offered the local John
Hop Is. 2d. a week for the work, but he
wasn’t agreeable; he was afraid it looked
like profiteering in war-time. So Bumble-
dom just didn’t bother, and the woman is
dead and the cyclist is next door to it and
the motor-bike and rider he ran into are
smashed. But the council saved its one-and-
tuppence

“Gouger”: A quaint wedding took place
on the Daintree River lately. The bride had
been a widow for 20-odd years, living by her-
self in one of the loneliest
parts of N.Q., her nearest
neighbors a couple of white
men across the river, half a
dozen aged kanakas, a tur-
bulent tribe of blacks and
the alligators which bathe in
the river and devour the
lady’s goats. The parson
was transported all the way
from Cooktown in a ketch,
which also carried the bridal
gowm. Owing to no meas-
urements being given, or the
fashions or the lady’s waist-
line changing in
the last quarter-
century. the frock
wouldn’t fit at all-
The bride groom
had to be hulloed
for across the
river, and he
finally put in an
appearance in his
working flannel,
cossack boots and
Sunday whiskers.
When the parson
mildly suggested
that he should wear
a coat, it was dis-
covered that he
didn’t own one. “Anyhow,” he said,
"she don’t want to marry the coat.”

“Haka”: In connection with the rumpus
at the Wanganui (M.L.) Detention Bar-
racks, the Commissioner in his report says:
“I have been able to discover little or no
exaggeration in the statements' of the prison-
ers, and as far as I have been able to check
them, they are fair and truthful.” To
which he adds: “On the other hand, I am
satisfied that many of the statements made
to me by members of the barracks staff were
untrue.” The Wanganui Detention Bar-
racks was the destination for Maoriland’s
conscientious objectors, and here is the kul-
tur treatment meted out by a staff that has
since been swept off the premises:—
A weighted pack was put on a prisoner’s back and
a rifle fastened to his side by means of handcuffs and
cord, one handcuff being attached to the sto.k of
the rifle, and the other to the prisoner s wrist The
barrel was secured to his shoulder by a cord. If
he was in uniform the barrel was passed through
the shoulder straps. Prisoner was then ordered to
inarch and if he refused he was pushed from behind
and dragged along by the arms around the yard
When he came to a corner he was given a push
which caused him to bump against a wall of tin so
that he landed head first. At times he was given
a punch, thumped on the back and neck, his heels
trodden on, and in some cases the boot was put in. In
one case a rope was used to drag a prisoner around
the yard, while In two instances prisoners were
escorted around by the hair of the head.
The inquiry was ordered last June, and the
report was laid before Parliament _on
Dec. 5, in nice time to avoid a discussion.
But the scandal should not be permitted to
be snowed under. The w’orst _ feature of
nearly all such “punishments” is that they
are class outrages, just as the “cat” is. You
never see a rich or socially well-placed man
given the “cat”; nor do you see his son
escorted around by the hair of the head.
