“T. 0.1.” : In the old Melbourne Cemetery,
which it is proposed should be resumed for
market purposes, is a stone
to the memory of James Jack-
son, of Toorak, who died at
sea in 1850 at the age of 47.
Although he died so young he
was Melbourne’s greatest mer-
chant of his time. His house
at Toorak, or rather the house
which he built when his cir-
cumstances justified it, and
which he did not live to en-
joy, was long the finest in the
place and served for years as
the Governor’s residence.
Hotham, Barkly. Darling and
Canterbury (earlier known as
Manners-Sutton) all lived
there. Before the gold fever
had attained its height a
prominent trader had leased
it from the widow Jackson at
£3OO per annum. In the
middle of the lease, a demand
suddenly arising in 1854 for a
hohse for Govei-nor Hotham.
the place was sub-let at
£lO,OOO a year!
“Trevallyn” writes a page of history :
The many are wrong who think that the flag of
the Southern Cross was first flown by the defenders
of the Eureka Stockade in 1854. It had been
hoisted three years before that—on February 1, 1851,
by the Australasian League.
This League was the idea of the Rev. J. West,
of Launceston, who afterwards wrote what is still
the best history of Tasmania. Its objects were to
bring about a federation of all the anti-transportation
leagues scattered about the colonies, and to work
for union under the Grown. The League’s motto
was “The Australians are one.” The five stars stood
for N. S. Wales (Queensland was still unseparated),
Victoria (which was just about to be separated),
South Aus., Tasmania and Maoriland. The Swan
River settlement was at the time almost deserted.
Tasmania was in a ferment owing to the landing
of the Neptune’s convicts after the Cape Town people
had refused them. After a conference of the Tas-
manian leagues, the Launceston branch was em-
powered to arrange an inter-State conference to draw
up a covenant. The Tasmanian delegates were Jas.
West and J. P. Weston. The covenant hound the
subscribers to have nothing to do with convict labor
and to use all their powers to stop transportation
to any part of the Australian colonies.
They drew up addresses to their fellow-colonists
and to Great Britain. That to Great Britain said:
“We address words of supplication, not of threaten-
ing. A few short years, and that which is now a
grievance will grow into a quarrel. By instant con-
cession, an act of justice will become a monument
of the Imperial clemency. But these colonies are
solemnly pledged each to the other by their mutual
interests, their future destinies, their fellowship of
weal and woe, and now by their League and Solemn
Engagement, to achieve the freedom of their com-
mon country.”
At a meeting of the Victorian Association held
on February 1, 1857, the covenant was signed by
the Tas. delegates and by Wm. Nicholson, Wm.
Westgarth, M.L.C., and Montgomery Bell, alderman,
and then a banner was unfurled. The Southern
Cross was on a blue ground; the Union Jack was
in the corner. The whole was bordered with white,
the date being added in letters of gold.
When the Tasmanian delegates left' for home
they were presented with a banner, bought by
general subscription, as a present to Tasmania.
The Mayor, in presenting it, said: “Gentlemen, I
pray you to receive it in the name of the people of
Port Phillip. May it remain nailed to the mast
until these colonies are emancipated from convict-
ism.”
The Tasmanian delegates returned from the con-
ference by the Swift. The banner which was pre-
sented to them was flying at the masthead, and
was afterwards carried round the town. Later,
it was presented, along with a silver casket of
sovereigns, to Henry Dowhy, a Launceston bookseller,
for the part he took in the anti-transportation agita-
tion. His son, Wm. Dowhy, a few years ago pre-
sented the banner to the Victoria Museum, Laun-
ceston.
Captain Bell, of the Raven, was the first to carry
the flag at sea—the ancestor, so to speak, of the
flag which the Australia flew at her jackstaff when
she headed one of the lines and took charge of the
battle-cruiser Hindenberg at the surrender of the
German Fleet.
One of the toughest handfuls that the
Sydney police have had to juggle with for
some time was a member of the crew of
the Huddart-Parker steamer Zealandia, who
raised Cain in the Government shipping-
office because a bonus which he expected
was not forthcoming. He introduced him-
self as a Liverpool Irishman, and the
language with which he did it would have
melted, down an asbestos tank. Two athletic
AND HE WAS A SWEET-TOOTH!
“I suppose you found the English girls
sweet?"
“ No-o-o. Only substitutes for sugar in
Blighty, you know!"
Hops started to put him off the premises,
and there and then commenced such a scrap
as happens only once in a leap-year.
The Liverpool Paddy kicked —anywhere and
everywhere—butted, wrestled, punched ana
put in the deadly knee-jolt. All of which
was not new to the Hops, but when the
animal buried his teeth in one of the Joans
insteps and tore from his boot three of the
brass lace-cyelets they reckoned that they
had run against the limit. Six months was
the reward.
SLEEPLESS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Last night I could not sleep.
The trailing hours went by.
Oft-tirne my eyes would weep
A nervous lullaby.
Darkness and dawn the . same—
I churned Life’s praise and blame—
And then....
Thank God, the milkman came.
N.S.W.

During the past few weeks we have received inquiries from our clients
and agents throughout Australia regarding the above possibility. We
also find a general feeling among the public that a big drop in prices
is possible for 1919.
We Take this Opportunity
of Advising You of the
Exact Situation.
Machinery and metal goods are not manufactured by magic. Any goods
available for delivery this year were already in the course of manufac-
ture, or the raw material for same purchased when hostilities ceased.
These goods, when placed on the market, will cost more than deliveries
six months ago.
Again, stocks of machinery have been -extremely short throughout
the world for some time past. Manufacturers the world over are
scrambling for raw material to supply the above shortage. As a con-
sequence, the metal market, since hostilities ceased, has advanced
instead of declining.
We hope lower freights in the near future will enable us to keep our
prices at present figures, but other Cooper branches throughout the
world are calling for stocks of Cooper machinery, and it will be diffi-
cult to supply all.

POLITICAL
POINTS
Senator Pearce is like a bad egg that is
broken to the public by degrees. First he
wasn’t going; Senator Millen, the man who
thinks that new manufacturing industries
are unnecessary,' regarded it as an insult
when another Senator asked him if Pearce
was going after Watt had said he never
heard of him going. Then he was going.
Then # his secretary was also going—at the
public expense, of course. Then his whole
family was going at the public expense, but
the idea that the family’s hotel bills and
pocket money in England would be charged
to the community was scoffed at as an out-
rage, just like the original report that
Pearce was going. By-and-bye it may be
found that Australia pays for everything,
including the trousseau and dowry of any
Pearce who gets married during the trip.
Wonderful how much depends on the way
things are put. It is proudly announced
that £lO,OOO a year is to be saved by doing
away with superfluous forms and documents
in Pearce’s Defence Department. In other
words, it is Found that Pearce’s oflice has
been wasting £lO,OOO a year. That amount
is to be saved by ceasing to waste it, just as
Herod increased the birth-rate by not mas-
sacring any more infants.
BACCHUS GOES TO THE DENTIST.
An inter-State conference of brewers’ associations
will be held in Melbourne to consider the proposal
for the reduction of the strength of alcoholic
liquors.—SUN.
The revenue of N. S. Wales was £1,694,921
more for the past six months than for the
corresponding period of 1917. In other
words, thanks to increased taxes and rail
and tram fares, the revenue has jumped
£3,389,842 per year. Yet the Ma community
still can’t, it seems, under the Holman
regime, build a yard of railway without bor-
rowing the money at 5$ per cent. Is thi|re
a curse on the State, or is there, a curse on
Holman, or is Holman a curse himself? Or
is the alleged revenue really a ln
fact, is there any such place as N. S. Wales t
A country where the alleged revenue doubles
and trebles in a handful of years, and which
is less able to pay for its own shirt or its
drain the more the income grows, must
be one of those places that ghosts come
from.
This is what the Age, hotfoot after
economy, didn’t print:—•
Although Sir Arthur Stanley has several months
of his extended term as Governor of Victoria yet to
serve, the question of his successor is being discussed
in certain quarters. It is considered probable,
however, that, in view of the known public feeling
against importing any more sub-Excelleneies, the
insistent demand for economy in administration, the
continued ignoring of promises made when Federa-
tion was accomplished, and the shrieking absurdity
of a small handful of people maintaining seven
Governors, the Imperial Government will decline to
any longer placate its supporters or its too-strenuous
opponents with Australian Governorships.
What it did print was same piffle about
Birdwood, lan Hamilton or somebody.

that the importing business can’t be inter-
fered with for the sake of any mere dashed
Australian industry. Frames for travelling
bags are on the free list, like almost every-

the new inrush of frames made by cheap
labor in protected Japan has shut the transi-
ent business up. Being spoken to about a
duty, the Watt Ministry opined that
the new source of employment might well
be silent. The Vay it put the thing was
that it “regi’etted” a
duty couldn’t be im-
posed. But as a duty
could be imposed

ernment regretted. An
idea is growing that
when an Australian
factory closes up the
perverted gang is
rather glad.

President Wilson., being a past-master in
beautifully deceptive argument, professes to
have discovered a way of checking Bol-
shevism :
Bolshevism is steadily advancing westward. It
cannot be stopped by force, but it can by food.
As a matter of fact, Bolshevism is the doc-
trine of well-fed agitators, who mostly re-
present rascality directing ignorance in the
way it shouldn’t go. In any case the Presi-
dent is ignoring the report, published not
long ago, of his own Committee on Public
Information: —
'The present Bolshevik Government is not a
Russian Government at all, but a German Govern-
ment acting solely in the interests of Germany,
and betraying the Russian people as it betrays
Russia’s natural Allies for the benefit _ of the Im-
perial German Government and its allies.
Stop it by food! Is Bolshevism in Australia
short of tucker?
From a gallery man: — •
Pearce’s “nerve” in going to England is nor
so remarkable when one considers that he has the
almost unanimous support of the Cabinet. He
is being despatched because he is no longer
tolerable to the Liberal wing; and even his
old Labor colleagues in the Government are rather
tired of Hughes’s demand for Pearee-at-any-price,
especially as it has been productive of so much
rancor and bitterness. If a few steamer fares
are the price that the Government has to pay
for a little respite, then Watt and Co. are willing
to pay and stand all the clamor that the scandal
involves. It is a dirty business all through; but
the worried Watt, who is doing three men’s work,
can be forgiven if he takes advantage of a position
which relieves him of a political incubus. Inci-
dentally Watt and his Liberal colleagues will next
quietly consider whether they can’t send Webster
to inspect the American silkworm industry.
Australia dug out about £5,427,033 worth
of gold last year—say £750,000 less than in
1917, and £1,650,000 less than in 1916,
and £8,500,000 less than in 1901. It
is estimated that a subsidy of 20s. per ounce
is required to arrest the decline of this
wonderful natural industry, which would
amount to £1,276,949 oil lhst year’s output.
This means that more than the whole tin
crop, or about tworthirds of the potato crop,
or twice the baby crop (officially valued at
£5 per infant) is asked for to pay for get-
ting out the gold crop. Is the time coming
when the natural resources of the country
must live by taking in each other’s washing?
Bricks are being flung at the Australian
manufacturer because during the war he
did some profiteering. But if he took big
profits he had this excuse—he didn t know
how soon the war would end and dumping
begin; and when that happened he
knew that, without a tariff, his often-costly
plant, etc., would most likely be scrap. Had
the Government given him the more or less
permanent protection of a tariff, he could
have supplied goods at a much lower price ,
and still been better off.
If the ’bus waited 50 years some people
would still miss it. For lack of shipping
Australia missed the best market that ever
was for its wheat, and lost hundreds of
thousands of bushels into the bargain. It
missed the best market that ever was for
munitions, while Canada caught it and
made millions. Only when the end was in sight
did Australia start ship-building; and now
naturally the Commonwealth Government
finds that it will pay handsomely to cancel
the ’bus-missing contracts and buy steel
ships. But if it doesn’t have those steel
ships made in Australia, so that the in-
dustry may be kept on the premises, then
Australia will be' just as badly off when
the next trouble comes. Of course there
may never be another trouble. But it is
just as well to remember that every great
war has always been the last one.
It is a good rule after a war to make
haste with the peace. At the first Congress
of Vienna in 1814 the diplomats had just
begun to reach for each other’s hair and
ears when Napoleon’s return from Elba
averted a most unholy scrap ; and just now
the Jugo-Slav State and Italy are barely on
speaking terms, because of a strip of Dal-
matian territory. Serbia and Italy both
fought Austria for the recovery of the strip,
and Serbia claims it on the plea that it
fought longest, suffered most, and represents
the bulk of the people. But at the moment
Serbia’s prospects don’t look very good. The
profoundly well-meaning but unpractised
Wilson has been to Italy and has been im-
pressed with the Italian side of the case, and
also by the architecture of Venice and its
gondolas. Serbia, on the other hand, has
very little architecture left and never had a
gondola, and Wilson hasn’t been there.
The Speck Parliament recently concluded
has had a unique history. It witnessed the
expulsion of a section of the Press from the
Gallery; the deposition of Labor-leader
Earle; the resignation of Senator Ready
and Earle’s substitution; the expulsion of
Dicker from a Committee and a resolution
condemning some action of his; a fight in
the lobby between two members; and a
joint sitting to elect a Senator in place of
Long. In addition it has seen the abolition
of Sergeant-at-Arms as a separate office ; an
accusation of attempted bribery against a
Minister which involved a Commission ; and
two other Commissions in consequence of
Parliamentary discussions involving serious
charges; while the House of Dodery in its
ultra-wisdom solemnly condemned the Pre-
mier for a statement of his in “another
place.” The Parliament has also lived
through a number of resignations and a
death —Earle, Woods, Watkins and Howroyd
were lost by resignation and Burgess by
death. Each new Assembly has, on an
average, five new members. This time there
are likely to be more. Soldier candidates
promise to be a conspicuous feature of the
elections when they happen—probably in
May.
Randolph Bedford in Ryan’s defence: —
A mean mind attacks Premier Ryan (B. 16/1/T9)
because “potatoes are dearer in Brisbane than they
have been for 30 years,” and proceeds: “Ryan has
cheap meat to his credit (it happened that the State
had a superabundance qf beef) ; but when it comes
to cheap anything else ihe seems much the same as
the rest.” Queensland Ihas cheap meat because the
Government not only has State shops, but keeps the
price of cattle down by the ownership of cattle. Cab-
bages have been 15s. a dozen wholesale in Brisbane;
but the Government doesn’t own cabbage gardens.
The fact remains that the Government has cheapened
all the public utilities it is responsible for initiating.
Despite even the tinkering of the State Produce-
agency Bill by the House of Zifs, it has made the
handcuffed and leg-ironed agency a new dividend to
the producers. Same issue of The B. speaks of recent
prebate-duty legislation as “catch-penny devices
which don’t pay much.” It may be a catchpenny
device, but it also catches an estate held by an
absentee millionaire which otherwise would have paid
nothing.
Webster, the Heaven-sent P.M.G., has
come to the defence of Pearce and his trip
to England. Hughes and Cook are in
Europe; but “they have more than enough
to tax their maximum energy and capacity.”
And as for Monash, “I say respectfully that
the military cannot be expected to grasp
other than the military side.” Well, even if
there is a civil side that Monash, the engi-
neer, can’t attend to as well as Pearce, the
carpenter, what is Andrew Fisher in Lon-
don for?
The public begins to learn how hale sol-
diers are treated on Australian transports.
Sick soldiers have been treated in just the
same or a worse way for two years past:
but the Censorship kept the public from
knowing anything about it. No wonder the
responsible politicians and some other people
are hanging on to as much censorship as
they can with all their claws.

INDUSTRY.

she will be forced to seek new fields for capital,
and will naturally turn to America, where the
world’s surplus will be available. —Beeby in U.S.A.
B EEBY: “Well, good-bye; glad to see you
so busy in there. If you've a few spare
millions, remember there are plenty
more of us boys who could do with a
little trip.”
As. an excuse for demanding a standard
wage of £5 a week for 40 hours’ work P. J.
Smith, Federated Builders’ Laborers’ secre-
tary, states that, “as many workers will be
returning from the Front, a reduction in
hours is required to enable them to be
absorbed.” But why doesn’t Smith follow
out his proposition? To absorb still more
and more “surplus” labor, the hours might
be reduced to 30, then to 20, then to 10.
Ori the other hand, Smith and his friends
might try a Protective tariff. Then employ-
ment at interesting work at high wages
would be so plentiful that nobody would
take on monotonous hod-carrying at 2s. 6d.
an hour, and these professors would be
able to insist on 3s. an hour for 36 hours
without creating an overflow of brick-shift-
ers waiting to be “absorbed.”
Still the same old centralising policy! N.
S. Wales Taxation Department is notifying
all and sundry that “income-tax stamps,”
from half a crown to a pound, are on sale
“at the State Treasury, the office of the
State Commissioner of Taxation, the Head
Office of the Government Savings Bank and
at the Oxford-street and Haymarket
branches.” The real object of the stamps,
of course, is to get in the money before it is
due; but even for such a thing as that equal
facilities might be given to country people.
There is no reason why all branches of the
Government Savings Bank shouldn’t be used
for the purpose.
As the French Revolution progressed
leaders who hadn’t kept pace were- propelled
swiftly out of the front door and bumped
their heads on the pavement—men like
Desmoulins, Philippe Egalite, Danton
Vergniaud, Fabre d’Eglantine and many
more. In the Russian revolution, new as it
is, the first and second crowd have already
disappeared, and the latest crowd is busy
disqualifying each other. And in the brand-
new German revolt the second lot is even
now fighting the first lot with a third lot
pushing behind. As for the Australian re-
volution, it moves so fast (on paper) that
there is a fair chance of its Robespierre or
Trotsky being hanged, in effigy, before the
first shot is fired. Even Ryan, of Queens-
land, doesn’t look Quite the demigod he was
a few weeks ago. Before he gets back from
England this time they may even knight
him.
ONE BIG JOB.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“There is a time coming,” said one of the speakers,
“when we shall require every policeman on our side.”
The firemen, too, were also to be recruited, for “even
under our system there may be fires,” another
interjected naively. —Report of One Big Union Con-
ference.
Out upon arbitration! This is plain:
The boss is only always out to rob;
And we shall organise and vote in vain
Until we smash the man who finds the job.
He thought out ways to make the wheels go round.
Out on such thinking! Out on all who strive
By paying twenty shillings in the pound
To keep the old, bad scheme of toil alive!
Bring the policeman in ! Arrest the cash!
Show them the stocking on the other leg!
By simple universal biff and bash
We kill the goose that lays the golden egg.
Let it be slain ! There will be cash enough
For one great pay-day in the coming years.
Well, let us have it. ’Tis the final bluff;
Then let the scheme dissolve in tears and beers.
Ho ! Bring the fireman 1 Things may take a turn
Not quite so pleasant when the food runs short;
And hungry crowds at last may want to burn
The One Big Unionists who spoiled the sport.
Keep the hose handy! Pour it on the mob
That through our talking would not pause to see
The truth that taking up the One Big Job
Was the sole road to Labor victory 1
N.S.W.

HE PIANOLA COMPANY is the largest manufacturer of musical
instruments in the world. To-day it dominates the music business
of the world. In all countries the Pianola Branch establish=
ments are the leading music houses, and in Australia
it is the one piano house with big branch organisations
in the four principal Capitals with hundreds of employees.
This success has been mainly built up through the tremendous sale which
the wonderful Pianola, which has become a household word the world over, enjoys,
and which instrument has contributed to the musical enjoyment of millions of
music-lovers.
The latest model 88=note full scale Pianola, found in the Steinway,
Weber, Steck, Stroud, Aeolian and Farrand Pianos, represents an array of
instruments of the highest quality, offering an unequalled range for selection, and
the fact that prices are moderate from manufacturer to consumer has been a big
feature in the Company’s success. These beautiful Pianola Pianos represent the
result of years of experimental work commencing with the old type of cabinet player,
and evolved from that model to the present high standard by clever men who have
invented and NOT IMITATED. The Pianolo Company was the first big
house to recognise the advantage of a Pianola action and Piano combined in
one case, and pioneered the trail which has been followed by hundreds of
imitators.
The Pianola Piano contains patents representing extremely clever devices
for rendering music with the highest musical expression that can never be
approached by others, and the difference between THE ONLY PIANOLA
PIANO and a Player Piano of other make claiming to do what the great and
world-renowned Pianola accomplishes is so marked that it led an enthusiastic
Pianola owner on one occasion to remark: “The Pianola Pianos are like the
Battleships of the British Navy. When new ideas come along your Company
is always the first to adopt them, and I notice you do not hesitate to
eliminate all unnecessary and non-useful cheap devices and displays which
may attract an unthinking public. Keep going as you are, and, like the
British Navy, rule supreme; so will the Pianola out-distance all competitors
and imitators.”

SAYS PHOSPHATE DOUBLES
STRENGTH AND HEALTH.
Numerous notices have appeared in the
Press from time to time telling of the re-
markable benefits to be derived from the
regular use of bitro-phosphate instead of
drugs and medicines. Investigation shows
that bitro-phosphate, which is obtainable of
any good chemist, enjoys its great popu-
larity because of its peculiarly valuable
property of quickly restoring the strength
and vitality of the depleted nervous system.
Neurasthenia, nervousness, sleeplessness
and physical and mental weakness are
almost invariably due to a starved nervous
system. This condition can only be cor-
rected by supplying to the nerve centres
the necessary phosphoric food elements, the
lack of which has caused all the trouble.
For this purpose specialists almost invari-
ably prescribe taking with each meal a
tasteless o-grain tablet of bitro-phosphate,
which, while being inexpensive, is un-
doubtedly the most remarkable nerve-builder
and restorative of health and strength
known to medical science.— Advt.

FATHER: “She scratched yer, did she? Well,
well; that ain’t Huffin’, to howl about”

’owlin about. It’s wot she said about

“Ses”: I have just discovered another
use for the kerosene-tin. A new settler at Pankstown
was fencing in his poultry-farm, and when adzing
the rails, having an awkward implement in an awk-
ward pair of hands, he stood each foot in a kerosene-
tin for the protection of his shins.

“E.A.D.”: A tall, slim gum-tree is
bravely holding its own at the foot of an abrupt
hill in Connemara. The sides of the hill are scarred
by tearing torrents, and one, brown and frothy,
rushes within a few feet of the stranger. It faces
Killary Bay, one -of the wild, black-watered fjords
of Ireland, and beyond are treeless mountains now
in full autumn splendor of purple heath, yellow
gorse and red bracken. The gum’s lower branches
rustle the little white cabin of the weaver of the
village. “Bedad,” he said, “it stays there like that
all the winter. Och, it’s like a tree that the mother
would be gone from it. It comes from Amerikee.”
I assured him it did not. “Och, well, Miss, anyhow,
it’s a poor orphint, and it’s never afther taking its
sjeep. I think I’ll be making a coat for it for the
winter.” ,

“Samblebe”: Does a queen bee or a hive-
committee send out a scout to find an empty residence
for the new swarm about to depart from the old dug-
out? This I vouch for happened at Buchan (Vic.)
on 22/11/’lB. I was seated under an apple-tree
in my own yard when I heard an angry buzz, and
looking up saw a bee tangled in a spider’s-web
woven over a small hollow. I liberated the honev-
gatherer and destroyed the web. The bee entered
and re-entered the cavity in the tree many times
and I concluded that it was a scout. I placed a
box near by and darkened it with a bag. The
insect entered and explored the interior of the box
and after taking photos from various angles made a
line for h.q. Next afternoon the swarm was in
possession.

“Ida D.” : Though this has been an ex-
ceptionally dry season, the gumtrees are bearing
a heavier crop of blossoms than they have done
for years past; and in the early spring the bush
flowers were much more profuse than usual. Also,
in our paddock (south-east corner of N.S.W.)
springs that have been dry for three years have
started to run freely again. I have seen dry,
sandy ereekbeds become full of water an hour
or two before a heavy thunderstorm. What’s the
answer?

‘‘G. P- Brown” : Hollo’s legs were short
and concave, quite unsuitable for fast locomotion.
By way of amends Nature had given him long arms
and hefty biceps, alertness of eye and a mobile neck.
His ability with his fists was superb; his reluctance
to “put them up” most pronounced. Brillen had the
best pair of runner’s legs in the township. His
thighs were abnormally long; his knees knocked as
he walked, and from his knee to his ankle was as
short as a creditor’s good morning. His temper was
fiery. Brillen and Rollo quarrelled over something
or other, and the former ordered Rollo to put ’em
up ; but Rollo, always peaceably inclined, made pour-
parlers until Brillen struck at him, whereupon he
waddled away at his equivalent for a run. “No you
don t! remarked Brillen, as he made fistic impact on
the overtaken Rollo’s shoulder point, spinning him
around with a vigor that made Rollo’s arms take a
defensive pose in an endeavor to restore equilibrium.
Brillen’s menacing eyes in contiguity to the up-
raised arms formed too suggestive a combination
even for the peaceable Rollo, so he smote his pur-
suer and downed him. Then Rollo resumed his
retreat. Brillen’s running ability soon enabled him
to come up with the enemy again. Once more he
was sent down, and then Rollo moved off again.
The natural runner soon had the natural fighter at
bay for the third time. On this occasion Brillen
led with a round-armed right, which Rollo parried
and simultaneously made violent contact under
Brillen’s left ear. Brillen lay dazed for five min-
utes, Rollo standing by, contemplatively. Then
Brillen painfully arose and supposed that the inci-
dent could be regarded as closed. Rollo supposed so
too, but said it would have been better if Brillen had
won a bloodless fight by letting Rollo run home
peacefully. “Nice yarn it would have made,”
replied Brillen, “for me to say you beat me at
running!”

“Tee Bee” : I want to give some of our
crack shots a chance of doing a big thing for
Austialia. Ihis is my idea. The prickly-pear
plant is heavily charged with moisture. Therefore
I want to get contracts for sowing seeds of water-
cross over big blocks of pear country. To get the
work done quickly the seed would have to be
shot into the prickly-pear from cartridges. After a
season s shooting we could go back over the ground
and watch the cattle fattening off the water-cress
and notice the pear shrivelling up from exhaustion.
“The Wanderer”: George Farley, of
Gloucester River (N.S.W.), owns a heifer that
hasp t had a square drink for three years and a
hall. She’s a queer lot ; her owner says that if
anyone tries to drive her she circles round and
round and falls on her ear. She dw'ells on the
top of a mountain, and has spent her life circling
round on five or six beaten tracks, which she has
worn as bare as a Bourke holding in drought time.
Ihe hill is of a porous nature and water cannot
be struck anywhere. The heifer never leaves the
top, where there is an area of about ten acres.
This is covered with kangaroo and blady-grass and
the supposition is that the animal gets sufficient
drink by licking the dew as it falls.
VERY DIFFERENT.
CUSTOMER : “Have you got my boots done, Mr. Snob?”
MR. SNOB : “No.”
CUSTOMER : “But you said you would have them ready to-day.”
MR. SNOB: “No, I didn't; I only told you to call to-day.”
“Azac” : It has been claimed that the hare
produces from three to five young ones in each litter
and from five to six litters in a year. If the
fecundity of hares is as great as that, what becomes
of them? It would, I think, be nearer the mark
to say that mamma produces from two to four young
ones in a litter and only one or at the most two
litters per year.
* * * *
“Wandi”: The mule is a stubborn animal
in some ways, but when a mob of him is on the
droving track I respect him. Horses are good :
with one man riding in front and one at the
tail you get them along serenely all day. They
don’t jib at gates or crossings like sheep and
cattle, but at night, when you let them go, they
have a habit of wandering and scattering and
making back. Donkeys are fairly good too, and
goats are not bad. But the mule takes the palm
for good conduct on the overland. I travelled
six weeks with a flock, and after the first week
all I had to do when starting away from camp
was to call out “hee-haw,” like one of themselves,
and every other ass would answer and, trotting to-
gether, follow after me like so many foals.
“Billy T.” : Brother Praetor had proclaimed
himself champion pig-sticker of Waddy and district,
and felt it not inconsistent with his position as ex-
superintendent of the Waddy Wesleyan Chapel to
challenge all-comers to a friendly contest, offering
to back himself for’ five pounds. There were no
takers, but John Notch, the local publican and
butcher, feeling himself professionally reflected upon,
offered to take up Praetor's bet of three pounds
that he could catch Notch’s black /pig in Notch’s
back-yard, kill it, scrape it, and suspend it fully
dressed in the doorway of Notch’s storeroom within
the space of one hour, Praetor being blindfolded
to the satisfaction of a committee of three all the
time. The event was the most popular sporting
fixture in Waddy in ten years. The hot water,
the tub, the scraping board, the knife and steel,
and all the appliances and means to boot were
placed in position under Praetor’s eye. Spectators
were confined to the fences and the roofs of Notch’s
sheds, and then the slayer was carefully blind-
folded, time was called, and the quarry was let
in. Praetor went after the porker quietly and
with the guile that had made him notorious as a
manipulator of pigs. Eventually the doomed animal
was seduced into a corner. Praetor precipitated
himself upon it, and a moment later it was in
position, and the knife was busy with its vitals.
Strict silence had been imposed, but excited men,
many of them adepts themselves, applauded
liaetoi s artistic stroke. The pig was slain
(scarcely a drop of blood ran to waste), the scald-
ing water was slopped on, and Praetor’s favorite
scraper, a stout billy lid, went to work. The
expert was at no discoverable disadvantage in being
blindfolded, so familiar was he with the anatomy
and peculiarities of pig’. The animal was gutted,
scoured, and presently hung from the hooks provided
in the doorway “as clean as a Christian corpse,
you,” as Brother Best eloquently attested —time 67
minutes 7 seconds. But Praetor did not get
Notch’s money, 1 and the justice of the committee’s
decision in favor of Notch was a cause of battle
in Waddy for long months after. As a matter of
fact, Praetor had not killed and cleaned the black
porker in the time specified. Notch, inspired by
subtle guile, had turned two animals into the yard,
and Praetor, in his blindness, had killed the wrong
pig!

“Melos”: Tell “Pompy” (B. 28/11/T8)
that I have seen a gin with twins. The lady (a
chronic loiterer in a North Coast town), although
as black as the 10 of spades, had a pair of light-
colored urchins —so light, in fact, that I had seri-
ous doubts concerning the bona-fides of the case. Her
ladyship’s candour, however, convinced me that
there was no deception on her part.

THE STAR HOTEL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Staying at the Star Hotel” means, in the
language of the Hush, camping out.
The city streets are aglow to-night,
And the noise is harsh and loud
As hurrying on in the glaring light
Go the pleasure-seeking crowd.
But there’s one whose thoughts will drift again
Where the bushland breezes swell;
Where the stars are clear over creek and plain,
And the box-trees whisper the old refrain:
“Come back to the Star Hotel 1 .”
Here there is noise where the townsfolk tread;
At the Star there is peace and cheer —
A fire in the gums, and a blanket spread,
And a quart-pot standing near;
The clink of hobble-chains in the trees,
And a softly-sighed “All’s well”
From the leaves that stir in the cool night breeze,
As the campers smoke and lie at ease
Out there at the Star Hotel.
There are men to meet from the runs up North
With tales of the Backblocks full,
And men from the South who are making forth
At the magic call of “wool,”
With the yarns of tallies’ that shearers know,
Or of sheds that proved a sell
When the drought seemed a thing of long ago
And weeks of rain made the tall grass grow,
Out there at the Star Hotel.
There are blokes who are going to lift v. mob
From the edge of the One Tree Plain,
And fellers “on spec” of a station job
Telling the tales again;
Swapping their yarns by a boxwood blaze
To the tune of a bull-frog bell,
Of silver cheques and of bumper pays,
Giving a sigh for the “other days’’
In the glow of the Star Hotel.
Bv the black lagoons they are camped to night
And along the Billabong ;
In the quiet bends of the Kyalite,
The big green gums among;
Hearing again the curlew’s call
Till the weird jnotes weave a spell
As the hush grows deeper, and stray stars fall,
And the lure of the bush is over all
Who stay at the Star Hotel.
The noise in the city to-niglit is loud,
And the streets are white in the glare;
But some there are in the surging crowd :
Whose thoughts are otherwhere. <
They follow a track that leads away
To the parts where the bush-folks dwell;
Across the rivers and plains they stray >
To the camp-fires lit by the mates who stay :
Out there at the Star Hotel.
MAORILAND.

“Mon C.": Barwon Mary, a black gin, was
the mother and Jimmy Gowan, a white man, claimed
to be the father of a family of healthy half-
castes. Jimmy was employed on the station and
lived in a hut near the homestead. Bill, the
groom, had a standing invitation to Gowan’s hut and
for over a year dropped in almost nightly for a
quiet game of euchre with Jimmy and the missus,
and all went well till one night Jimmy spotted
Bill giving the missus the seductive eye. Of course
there was a scene, and Bill was ordered out of
the hut with the promise of a hot time in the old
town if he showed himself there again. But Bill
was prepared to take risks, and Gowan, coming in
unexpectedly one afternoon, found him chatting
over a pannikin of tea with Mary. Then
the fun started. Jimmy floored Bill with a
right kick to the stomach and proceeded to knock
the stuffing out of his rival. It was then that
Mrs. Jimmy started a corroboree around the strug-
gling pair, screaming: “Jimmy, Jimmy, don’ kill
that pfeller—he’s th’ father of your las’ chile.”

“Rexieorn”: “Lantana’s” greenish-brown
“snake with fins” from Cessnock (N.S.W.) is
merely one of our many species of legless lizards,
sometimes known as slow-worms. “Lantaha’s”
specimen is probably the „ common legless lizard
(Pyijopus lepidopus), which has a round head that
end. There are 22 scales girdling the middle
of the body, the 12 uppermost being fairly
strongly keeled. The color is very variable, and
the rough-looking, keeled scales give it a very
unsnake-like look. It is really very hard to dis-
tinguish between legless lizards and snakes without
a minute examination, the main structural differ-
ences being skeletal; however, if “Lantana” ex-
amines his find he will notice the following
characters which will place it as a lizard without
legs, it having only two rudimentary ones, as
stated by him. The ear is exposed as a hole
in the side of the head. (This is not visible in

snakes.) Lizards have eyelids; the legless lizard
having a circular scaly one. (Snakes have none,
(heir eves being protected by a scale, shaped like
a watch glass.) Legless lizards have a more or
less fixed, ordinary tongue. (Snakes are provided
with u long, slender, forked tongue which is re-
tractile into a sheath inside the mouth.) The
l:zard has scales all round the body, whereas the
snake (sea-snakes and another distinct species
excepted) have large oblong-shaped belly plates.
r lhe lower jaw is united in lizards, and not exten-
sible as in the snakes. This will be noticed during
eating. The lizard has to chew its prey, whereas
the snake extends its jaws and swallows whole.
Hie queer little lizard is quite harmless. It
gTOw-s to a length, say, two feet, and about
three-quarters of its total length is taken up by
the tail. As in the geckos, its tail is very brittle
and breaks off easily when handled, after which
another, though rather rudimentary affair, is grown
in the original one’s place. The reptile is fairly
common all over Australia and Tasmania.

“Ragar”: The natives of the Torres Strait
islands have their own ideas about showing respect
to the dear departed. One trick is to anoint the
body with half the contents of a bottle of cheap
scent sold by the enterprising Orientals of Thirsty.
The rest of the bottled stink is corked up and
left on the grave. There is a tiny grave on Moa
at tlie foofc of which is a baby's feeding-bottle—
the probable cause of the unfortunate infant’s death.
’I hough some of the larger islands have cemeteries,
graves are scattered all over the place. Generally
decorated with empty and inverted rum-bottles,
they are often found beside the corpse’s late resi-
dence. Where the relatives have been more
ambitious—or better off—a “proper marble” has
been procured from Townsville or some other coastal
town. The setting-up of one of these stones is
the excuse for a general gorge. Such an event
recently took place at Darnley, where a “proper
stone” was erected over the grave of the late
Mamoose—head man or king. The ceremony was
followed by a three-days’ gorge of pig, yams,
turtle, etc., which was shaken down—and room
made for more—by vigorous dancing and singing
by all hands.
“Werrimee”: In some hand-shearing sheds
a couple of stands are set apart for learners. Here
the novice suffers the pains of apprenticeship. What
looks so easy when performed by an expert turns
out to be horribly painful to learn. After the be-
ginner has hacked through a few woollies he is a
puffing, perspiring, bloodstained streak of misery,
praying for the day’s end and glorious rest. Minutes
seem like hours; he struggles along, adding to his
aches and pains, till finally he leans against the pfen,
a limp and dejected wreck. The next day is one of
agony. His wrist is stiff and sore, and he can
scarcely stoop. After nearly a week of this sort of
joy the learner may shear about 25 in a day. Then
his numerous physical ailments begin to leave him
and he has a notion that if he lives long enough
he will some time or other shear a hundred in eight
hours. When the shears take long, full bites, instead
of nibbling at the wool, and the sheep, so awkward
and annoying at first, drop easily into their proper
positions, the apprenticeship is over.
JOY IN THE SCHOOL-YARD.
ELDERLY STRANGER: “Can you explain to
me, sir, the appearance of excessive joy,
not to say ecstasy, apparent on the
countenances of these innocent child-
ren?”
NATIVE : “Well, yer see, loss, their teacher
’as just been run over be a motor-car.”
“Laura Duncan": Ain surprised at
“Barcoo’s” statement that Cork is the furthest
south - a buffalo has been located. In 189 S, when
I came to Mooraberrie station, Windorah,
as a bride, the horns of an ancient and
most disreputable buffalo stood in the living
room (they are still there) and most of
his hide had been made into dinkum hopples.
This buffalo was shot by William Duncan, with
a Schneider that Noah used when the rain stopped,
at a waterhole on the rabbit fence, which for the
ensuing 20-odd years has been known as the Buffalo
hole. The beast came from the Gulf with travelling-
cattle, got into the mob and sulked, and the drover
could not get him out. He wandered as far as S.A.
and back, and was seen on Canilgo station by Mr.
Wymberley, then manager. An old blackfellow,
Cbookie Chook, of Mooraberrie, always became very
excited and mysterious when he marshalled any of
his dusky friends up to see the “debb'il debbil’S”
horns. “Me and Mitta Duncan bin shoot ’im,”
he made known. It transpired' that Chookie
Chook’s share in the slaughter consisted in climb-
ing the highest tree to be found—an unusual feat,
as these desert blacks never climb unless under
great provocation.

“Across Australia”: I watched him from
a nearby cattle-pad. He was standing in a medita-
tive attitude on a log beneath which flowed the
sluggish waters of the Styx (N.Q.) —a tall and lanky
being. Balancing daintily on the slippery foothold
he took off his felt hat and tied it on to the end of
a ten-foot stick. Presently a fish came up to the
surface. Swiftly and skilfully the improvised
landing-net was shoved under the disturber of the
peaceful calm and he was lobbed into a flour-bag.
In half an hour the bag was full.
* * * *.
“Tim Buctoo”: The prize rat lives out
beyond Boulia (Q.). When I saw him he was turn-
ing: a grindstone in a northerly direction, whiie a
large centipede was going south around the periphery
of the stone making a frantic effort to keep on top.
“Training him for racing?” I inquired. “No,” said
the rat ; “I was wondering how long he’d keep
going before his feet wore out. But that’s a good
idea of yours; a bloke could make a lot of dough
out of the silly coots ’round here with a fast santer-
pea. I. think I’ll work it.” I left him still .turning
the stone.
* * * *
“Tintack”: Binghi was never much of a
surgeon, yet he liked mutilating himself or his
missus. In the old tribal days operations of all
kinds were practised, from the simple one of break-
ing a tooth off to mending a broken limb. Others
were bestial and indescribable. The N. S. Wales
North Coast tribes always appointed a fisherwoman
in each family. This worker was known by the
absence of two joints of the little finger on her
left hand. Strong spider-web was tied round the
second joint and left on till the flesh mortified.
When that had taken place the finger was held
in the opening of an ants’-nest till it was eaten
away.
“Umntu”: “Melaleuca” (B. 17/10/’18) is
wrong' in saying that bees, were first introduced to
Australia by Dr. T. B. Wilson in 1834. In “Two
Years in New South Wales,” by P. Cunningham,
surgeon, R.N., published in London in 1827, is
the following: “European bees were introduced
some years ago, but falling into careless hands,
little attention was paid to them for a while.
Lately, however, some . swarms have been obtained
by a careful individual, and we may soon hope to
see the colony stocked with them.”
*■# * #
“Nimrod”: There is nothing about which
so much pseudo-scientific rubbish is written as the
boomerang. Bought half-a-dozen of them once from
Carnage’s, in London. They were made in Eng-
land—just flat, crescentrshaped pieces of .ash or
hickory, with no warp or twist about them; nor
was one side any more convex than the other,
as far as I could judge, and it didn’t matter which
side up they were thrown. I am no expert, but
could throw any of them 50 yards, and they
would “come back.” If you take a piece of
cardboard) about the size of a playing card, and
cut it into boomerang shape, either crescent or
obtuse-angled, you can flick it with your finger
across a room and back. Even with any old flat
piece of metal, such as the end of a kerosene
tin, one can put up a fair performance with a
bit of practice. The warps and twists that are
sometimes, though not alvvays, seen in native
boomerangs are evidently the result of accident,
not design, and have no particular effect on the
flight of the implement, except probably a detri-
mental one.
*♦**.
Mrs. D. M. Bates has been telling the
S. A. Royal Society something that it gravely
considered about Binghi’s habits and customs
at the Wirilya native camp, and at other
places on the State’s West Coast. In nam-
ing children the abos. are often influenced by phvsi-
cal circumstances. A girl was named Raincloud be-
cause a soft shower was falling when she was born.
Sea clouds hung over the coast when another girl
arrived, so she was Summercloud. If a bird or
animal, or even an insect, is seen near the spot
where a birth occurs the child will receive the
name of the creature. Boys’ birth-names mav be
dropped when they are initiated. Men have ‘been
named The Whistling Eaglehawk, Dream Moon and
Crow. Natives now frequenting the West Coast come
from districts far north and east of the East-and-
Y est lailway, and the tribes are hopelessly mixed.
One man has married his wife’s mother, ' and his
wife’s daughter by another father. The natives
affirm, the man became blind “because he did this
thing.” An interesting circumstance durmg Mrs
Bates’s residence in the camps of the Euela and
\\ est Coast natives was the meeting of two cor-
roborees, the starting-points of which were North-
Eastern Queensland and North-Western Australia.
The travelling of these two performing troupes to
their meeting-point occupied years.
A SHRINKING SOUL.
[FOR THE BULLEITN.]
The one with large, pathetic eyes
Came to me at my gate.
He was a man of average size
And only normal weight.
His clothes had never been the best,
And now were quite the worst.
He was, I learned, bv Man oppressed.
By Fate unduly cursed.
But, ma am,” he said, “not for myself
Is it I plead. Please lend
A trifling meal, a little pelf
For my poor, starving friend!”
He pointed where the other stood
In pensive thought alone,
A man, it seemed, whose health was good,
Who weighed some eighteen stone.
“That big, fat chap?” said I. “Tut-tut!”
lie whispered: “Ma’am, I know it—
Bill’s dyin’ of starvation, but
He is too proud to show it!”
VICTORIA.
BILLY T.

More Gallipoli
The Dardanelles Campaign (11. W. Nevin-
son) is a rftelancholy volume. It would
have been so easy for Britain to retain the
traditional Turkish friendship. Right up to
1913; when Liman von Sanders went to Con-
stantinople, Abdul might have been bought
by the Entente, and bought cheap. Eyen
after the first irrevocable cast in the “im-
mortal gamble” there was hope. It was
frustrated by hesitancy, by lack of foresight,
even by plain stupidity. Because the Ger-
mans had reduced Liege and Namur by
high trajectory fire from giant howitzers, it
was argued that forts like Seddel Bahr and
Kum Kali could be blown out with naval
guns! Any bombardier in a field battery
would smile at such a notion at this time
of day. If the famous 29th Division had
arrived earlier; if there had been one grand
assault (say at Sari Bair) instead of a
number of minor ones; if there had been
no “inertia,” as Mr. Nevinson euphemistically
puts it, at Suvla on August 8; if any one
of a dozen conceivable things had or had
not happened, the road to Constantinople
might have been thrown open and the war
ended so far as Turkey was concerned.
Much of Mr. Nevinson’s brilliant work is
an exposition of how and why these things
did not happen. Much of it consists of
generous tributes to the Digger of the period
—tributes which Englishmen of the Nevin-
son type have always been readiest to pay.
(Copy from Angus and Robertson. Pub-
lished by Nisbet and Co., Ltd., London.)
Publishing in Australia
“0.L.” : Something might have been added
by Thomas J. Symons, formerly in charge of
the publishing department for George Robert-
son’s (Melb.), to his remarks about that
firm’s publishing methods (B. 19/12/T8).
Symons’s par. followed a suggestion (B.
28/11/T8) that there was room for improve-
ment on the 40 per cent, commission plan of
publishing at the author’s risk, allegedly
adopted by the recently-retired Robertson
firm and by most other Melbourne pub-
lishers. “During my connection with the
firm we published about 200 books,” Symons
explains, “and of these only 18 books were
ar the author’s risk.” This statement needs
some trimming, for the Robertson co.’s pub-
lishing machinery was also used by many
authors who did not have the Robertson
firm-name on their volumes. These books,
actually “published” by Robertson's, in part
at least, were of course at the author’s risk,
and Robertson’s condescended to handle them
for the 40 per cent, commission mentioned ;
while the same financial rule also applied,
naturally, to author’s-risk volumes on which
the firm’s name did appear. Of this sum
25 per cent, was supposed to go to retailers
supplied by the firm and 15 per cent to the
firm itself for its publishing service. The 40
per cent, deduction, however, was also
applied to all volumes sold over
the counter at Robertson s shop, so
that firm can hardly be said to have
tiea’ted the author with liberality. Cole’s
and Mullen’s, on exactly the same footing
as Robertson’s in this over-the-counter
trade, charged only 25 per cent, for
similar facilities. Again, circulars re-
lating to the publication of books by Robert-
son's on a subscription basis have reached me
in days gone by. Such publications would
not have been at the author’s risk, and there-
fore not included in Symons’s list of 18
prints of that class; but neither would the
venture have been at Robertson’s risk.
Where does Symons put this sort of publica-
tion in his calculations of his late firm’s
efforts? The Australian author, I believe, is
a long-suffering citizen with a lot of genuine
grievances. I began collecting my own lot
of grouches over 20 years ago, though not
naturally of a gloomy or pessimistic disposi-
tion, and I confidently allege that the author-
bloke, in his dealings with publishers, is
not usually treated even with reasonable con-
sideration. “Safety first” is the unwritten
motto of the general run of Australian pub-
lishers —no doubt a very business-like prin-
ciple and all that; but it should be possible
for the publisher, while playing perfectly
safe, to act more fairly to the men and
women who provide him with his job and
daily bread. The job is not necessarily a
parasitic one, but some publishers have cer-
tainly made it so.
A Satchel of Books
The Feminine in Fiction, by L. A. M. Priestley
(George Allen and Unwin). A little book of illus-
trative extracts and comment from the best-known
novelists on the Feminist question. Pleasantly done.
Echo Personalities; Frank Watts, who writes this
book, states his purpose very clearly on the cover.
He “makes an attempt to estimate the value for
educational practice of recent investigations into
the nature -of those mental characteristics of
crowds, Of hysterical subjects and of defective
subjects which do but echo the characteristics of
authentic personality.” And he states on his very
first page: “The conception of the study of the
abnormal as an aid to the understanding of the
normal was naturally impossible while the obvious
and more startling differences between normal life
and abnormal life obscured their fundamental
similarities, indeed, so long as the abnormal was
thought to comprise factors at once mysteriously
incomprehensible and unique, so long was the
serious and disinterested study of the subject held
to be a matter suitable merely for the trivial and
indolent attentions of the more eccentrically minded
among philosophers, litterateurs and doctors.” The
book more than lives up to the expectations thus
aroused, but the chapters about simple methods for
the teaching of mentally deficient children don’t
assort well with the writer’s thundering diction.
If a mentally deficient child was attacked in that
fashion its blood would be on Watts’s head.—
(George Allen and Unwin.)
The Papal Conquest, by Rev. Alexander Robert-
son, D.D. (Loyalist League of Victoria). The Pope,
according to the author, aims at the conquest. of
England because there is a lot of gold there. Much
alleged information as to the awful ways of the
would-be conqueror are set forth side by side with
the information that his powers are waning. If one
part of the book is right there is no reason for the
rest; and if one part is false there is a large sus-
picion resting on the remainder. So the book fails
to depress anjbody except the Orangeman who loves
depression.
A Floating Home, by Cyril lonides and J. B.
Atkins, with' illustrations by Arnold Bennett (Chatto
and Windus). A Thames barge under sail is cheaper
and more comfortable than a London home in the
suburbs. The authors and illustrator make a brave
attempt to prove it; but even wilted geraniums
have a sweeter smell than the banks of the most
storied river in the world.
The Kaiser in Heaven, by Alex. C. Welsh (Aus-
tralasian Authors’ Agency, Melbourne). A pamphlet
of verse of fair quality. The author states that it
was “Written Saturday, 22nd December, 1917, be-
tween 9.15 am. and 12.10 p.m. at Capri, Echuca...
with much joy.” It looks as if it might have been
written in seventeen minutes less.
Verses Afloat, by Claude R. Beresford (The
Mail, Adelaide), the name Beresford should carry
some distance in matters of the sea ; and these verses
are not unworthy of their subject. If they never
touch high poetry they never entirely lose grip, and
there is sometimes a tang of real salt about them.
The Changing Year, and other verses, bv L. .T.
Villiers (H. E. Stone, Adelaide). The author of
this little collection of sonnets and rhymes did not
live to see his book ; and the getting together of the
work has been left to other hands. A sonnet cycle
of the months, in Australian vernacular, is Villiers’
leading achievement; and very remarkable sonnets
they are, with a melody ringing through the gro-
tesque phrasing. Worth many volumes of more pre-
tentious and precious writing.
His Keeper, and other poems (Australasian Authors’
Agency, Melbourne). The writer doesn’t give his
name; and the poverty of the verse possibly explains
why.
The Dough-boys, by John O’Gorman (Herbert
Jenkins, London). A yarn about the Americans at
war. The camp sketches look very unmilitarv, but
the deeds are great enough. A boy in Boston or
Chicago may like it.
Hospital Sketches, by Frances Lyndall (George
Allen and Unwin). Good observation, tenderness
and homely simplicity make the little book worth
while. Many soldiers of many races pass through
the hands of this nursing sister; and she sees all
their faults and has a good word for them all.
The High Purpose, by Alan Cross (Australasian
Authors’ Agency, Melbourne). A very small book of
verses mostly on war subjects. The work, which
is sometimes bright and tuneful and often has a
hint of force, seems like that of a literary beginner
who might do bigger things.
Press, Platform and Parliament, by Spencer Leigh
Hughes, M.P. (Nisbet, London). Mildly interesting.
As a pressman he led the sheltered life' of the House
of Commons gallery, which is very different from
the battle-mm der-and-sudden-death existence of the
man who reports riots, epidemics, slum mysteries and
tragedies, wars, revolutions, hangings, conspiracies
and meetings of infuriated aldermen. As a political
platform-speaker he adhered to the primrose way
and never had an egg thrown at him at Warwick.
And as regards Parliament he is a highly respect-
able member himself, with no desire to be cut
dead in the refreshment-room for giving away trade
secrets. Some chapters are devoted to travel along
beaten tracks where brigands and cannibals and alli-
gators are not, and one to a dispassionate account
of his dogs, which were two very polite dogs, both
dead. A really horrible dog would have brightened
the book.
Yellow English is the work of Dorota Flatau, for-
merly of Sydney and now of London, and the book is
reported to have sold over there hot off the stove. In the
circumstances of the hour The Bulletin is prepared
to believe the report. One almost suspects that in
Yellow English the reader’s leg is being gently
tugged, and the suspicion seems to get some support
from the author, for the first page is decorated with
an explanation about “the characters being purely
imaginary.” If in the course of a novel pretending
to reveal conditions of society and the attitude of
public men towards great national issues, incidents
and speech and relationships are -draped, so to speak,
on and about a collection of literary dummies, the
suspicion of the whole thing being a canard becomes
inescapable. There are depths for consideration in
Yellow English, which is published by Hutchinson
and Co.
A pleasant little book of mirth and inverted logic
is The Philosophy of Mr. Bertrand Russell. It deals
with identity, immortality, ambiguity, generality,
symbolism and other subjects, and treats
them all lightly and agreeably. Also it treats
most of them convincingly; for instance, the
proof that a man with an unlimited income hasn’t
anything is too solid to be worth arguing against.
There may be a flaw in it, but the only way to
decide is to get an unlimited income and see what
happens. If Solomon had been a flapper and had married
Lewis Carroll of “The Hunting of the Snark” their
son might have written this book, but more probably
he would have bought a farm near Hay and attempted
to grow wheat. (Geo. Allen and Unwin, Ltd., Lon-
don.)
Diogenes at Athens' and other poems by Row-
land Thirlmere (Selwyn and Blount, London).
The title-pibce is a play in rhyming verse intended
to show the condition and spirit of England at the
beginning of the war. The dialogue, which goes in
a series of lopes and jumps, is so -tedious that, it
becomes almost unreadable. The other verses l lire,
for the most part, uninteresting and without
strength of thought or grace of manner. This,
entitled “An Australian Soldier,” makes curious
reading:—•
If was not his great wound of which he died:
Your blank, impassive faces killed his pride.
For slaying friendship and youth’s confidence
There is no weapon like indifference *
Our returned Diggers don’t seem to have noticed
the murderous effect of those “impassive faces.”
The Minister of Evil (Cassell and Co.), by William
Le Queux, gives a further instalment of the revela-
tions contained in “Rasputin the Rascal' MoffkJ’ The
story is allegedly the confession of Feodor Rajevsld,
the son of a Polish violinst, who. knew and saw -much
of the amazing charlatan with Che shaggy whiskers.
The Inky Way
The Express. Wagga Wagga (N.S.W.),
which has been celebrating/itk diamond jubi-
lee, was established by James Thorburn
Brown in 1858. He held it for a short six
months, when he was /Succeeded by Fowler
B. Price. There were but five provincial
papers in the State men— Courier (Yass),
Post (Albury), Free Press (Bathurst),
lleralp and Chronicle (Goulburn).
E. H. C. Oliphant, Melbourne champion
Hun-hater, formerly of the Mining Stan-
dard. joins Map. Herald staff 5. as leader-
writer. He nifi/le his start on the inky way
as proprietor of an organ of public opinion
in (iippsland, and drifted from there to the
country subeditor's desk in the Argus office.
Twice he lias appeared as a playwright
under Repertory Theatre auspices; but
there’s /no need to keep on harping about
that.

POINTS
CHARLIE
CHAPLIN'S
MILLION
DOLLAR
COMEDIES.
SUPER
FEATURES
“Fax” : Charles Wyndharfi, who . has just
finished his long innings, had less claim to be
knighted for his theatrical services than, say,
even Squire Bancroft. Wyndham was only
a breezy light-comedian to begin with,
making one of his first London successes in
a piece called “Brighton,” a disguised
American farcial comedy which didn’t seem
so very puerile in those days. He got a
lease, later on, of the little Criterion Theatre,
and there he stayed till he was one of the
oldest-looking comedians that ever played a
youngish part. He never was identified
with great productions or great perform-
ances. But he got a good social start in
London long ago, and his American friends
were many and influential, and one way and
another he came to die a “Sir.”
Charles Wyndham studied medicine before
he went on the stage, and was an army
surgeon on the Confederate side in Uncle
Sam’s uncivil war. He got his earliest
Yankee pull that way.
“The Cracked Tenor” : Now that Paderew-
ski has taken on the job of leading armies
in battle, I wonder what the insurance cos.
will say about the policies on his hands. In
premiums he paid
±'Boo annually, so
that if anything
went wrong, and
he could no longer
earn money at the
pianb, he would
become entitled to
a cash payment of
£lO,OOO. Once when
he cracked his
finger-nail and was
unable to play for
a week, he drew
£5OO as compensa-
tion.
Melbourne is to
see Muriel Starr
once again at the
conclusion of the
“Nothing but the
Truth” season,
although we had
been warned to
watch out for
“Friendly En-
emies.” Muriel is
“ Katinka ,” at Sydney
Criterion.
Thaddeus T. Hopper
(Phil Smith).
to give us a new piece somewhat similar in
design—a New York play known as “Three
Faces East.” The title will keep the public
guessing, anyway.
“J.F.11.”: In some reminiscences of an
old playgoer recently published in London,
reference is made to the meteoric career of
Charles Coghlan, a handsome and brilliant
actor who captivated the playgoing public for
a season and afterwards dropped into mis-
ery. “He was a victim to the same weak-
ness that destroyed Edmund Kean, G. V.
Brooke, Charles Dillon, Leigh Murray and
Robson.” Old Australian theatregoers re-
member G. V. Brooke and Charles Dillon.
George Coppin had occasionally to come be-
fore the curtain and apologise for the
absence of Brooke who was “indisposed.”
“Drunk, you mean,” shouted a candid Bal-
larat digger one night. “Well, you can
put it that way if you like,” said George,
as he retired behind the scenes. This old
London playgoer asked a well-known stage
wit what was really the matter with Cogh-
lan. “My dear fellow,” he replied, “when
the leading actor goes wrong it’s drugs, but
with the poor low comedian it’s whisky.”
“Ancient Prompter”: In the co. that
toured Australia with J. L. Toole, middle-
aged playgoers may recall a well set-up
young fellow named Lowne, who appeared
in light-comedy parts. Charles Macready
Lowne, to give him his full name, has since
made an enviable reputation on the London
stage. Recently he lost his father, who was
in his 89th year. Lowne, senior, when a
stage-struck youngster, became acquainted
with Macready and sought the great man’s
aid in connection with starting as a profes-
sional mummer. He never started. In Mac-
ready’s diary, under the 'date Nov. 29, 1850,
appears this entry: “A Mr. Lowne called
just as I was going out to dinner to thank
me for having saved him from going on
the stage.” Then follow's the fervid declara-
tion : “I feel grateful to God that I have
been instrumental in so much service to a
fellow creature.”
“E.T.”: “Cockaigne” (B. 9/l/’l9) should
have known that Frank Thornton was “tell-
ing the tale” when he gave the assurance
that his “Private Secretary” was original.
Thornton was merely a copy of Penley, “do
you know” gag and all. But the gag was in
the part long before Penley played it—and
before Beerbohm Tree played it, for that
matter.
“Fax” : Film-exchanges seemingly have so
far been merely an indirect benefit to show-
men who take shares and keep them going.
Lately the directors of Co-operative Films
in Melbourne gave a luncheon ’to their
clients to celebrate the renewal of the co.’s
contract with the Metro producers. It was
freely admitted between drinks that the
Co-op. had been the salvation of inde-
pendent showmen in Victoria by enabling
them to dodge monopoly. Yet shareholders
haven’t thusfar drawn much dividend. In
fact, the chairman dampily remarked that
he didn’t know of any film exchange which
was paying anything fat. However, in more
sanguine mood he might have remarked that
the picture business was still in its early
youth.
“Ancient Prompter” : Lie,” by Henry
Arthur Jones, mentioned (B. 16/1/T9) as
having been adapted to picture purposes, is
not to be confounded with “The Liars” of
the same author. Jones was over-fond of
imitating himself in his plots, effects, titles,
etc., but he produced “The Lie” 18 years
after “The Liars.” The former is a drama
done in. New York in 1915; the latter is
of course our old favorite comedy of the
Rrough-Boucicault days, originally staged
at the London Criterion in 1897.
“The Saving Grace,” the Haddon Cham-
bers play which would have received
its first production in Australia at the hands
of Cyril Maude had not the MS. of the
fourth act been submarined on the way out,
has been staged in N’Yark. The critics are
inclined to regard it as less than a comedy
and more than a farce. Maude, in the lead-
ing role, is praised with discretion rather
than with enthusiasm.
Hugh Marelyn, who is trying to induce
J. Bull to take an interest in his own com-
posers, talks of producing an operatic ver-
sion of Masefield’s “Nan.” The story should
provide fine opportunities for a musician
with a sense of drama.
“Fax” : Australian playgoers, even of long
standing, have almost forgotten Clifton
Alderson, an exceptionally tall actor, who
came twice to this country. The first time
he was imported by George Coppin as chief
supporter of the inefficient Laura Villiers
in a co. which produced “The Dancing Girl”
and other pieces, Julius Knight being the
spry “juvenile” of the co. Subsequently
Aldersbn came along in a light comedy
capacity, with a co. that played “The New
Boy,” or something of that sort. Nowadays
he is “dramatic representative” of the
Sacks, Limited, firm, which runs three or
The lady mentioned in a recent Sydney
Sunday Times par. might be pardoned for
classing it amongst things better left un-
said :
Miss , who will also appear in ,
is a prominent American musical comedy actress of
striking beauty, whose father was tormerly well
known in Australian waxwork circles as Jo Jo, the
Dog-faced Man.
four theatres in London. The theatrical
world is always discovering new names for
old jobs, and “dramatic representative” is
seemingly one of them.
“O.Iv.”: When are Australian managers
going to abandon the ancient custom of
importing panto “boys” from Europe or
America? Every good comic-opera chorus
of local girls contains at least two or three
potential “principal boys,” who would only
need a little training in the easy tricks of
that simple line of business. The managers
should have a school of instruction in
principal-boy duties, open to ladies of the
chorus only. Every young woman student
endowed wuth the essential voice and figure
would then have a hope of looming large
on the Christmas bill, with other opportuni-
ties to follow.
“C. Ockney”: Matheson Lang, here a few
years ago in “The Passing of the First-floor
Back” and “Pete,” is emulating Oscar Asche
in England, being now his own manager, his
own leading man and his own author. So
far he has made a success of it, and is
raking in profits with a piece called “The
Purple Mask,” which he adapted from the
French under the pen-name of Charles
Latour. It has already been played at three
West End theatres.
A very practical prize was offered at a
recent competition for unknown conjurers at
the Maskelyne “theatre of mysteries” in
London. It was a professional engagement
for a year at that house. The.winner was
one Conyere, a mere boy —said (improbably)
to be an Eton schoolboy—and within a fort-
night he was on the bill there.
“ EVERYBODY’S DOING IT,” AT
SYDNEY TIVOLI.
Softsoap scandal between Mrs. O’Mara
( Chas. Workman ) and Mrs. McQuibble
(Lottie Sargent).
A very expensive law-suit' over the play
“Within the Law” has just been decided in
London, after hanging on for nearly five
years. A. H. Woods, an American mana-
ger, claimed that the late Beerbohm Tree
had made various breaches of the contract
with him, when successfully staging the play
at London Haymarket, and in the “pro-
vinces.” He wanted an account and heavy
damages from the executors, but the judg-
ment was against him.
“Monsieur Beaucaire,” with charming
French music by Messager, is to be played
as an English comic opera some time this
year. The story doesn’t seem to invite a
musical setting. “A Marriage of Conveni-
ence” would seem more promising, if a
chorus could be introduced into it.
About a quarter of a century has evapo-
rated from the sea of time since Australia
saw Maxine Elliott in the flesh, but she is
now re-appearing on the picture-screen, and
there she still holds her own as a large and
lovely creature. The photographs are kind
to Maxine insofar as they rarely bring her
close enough to the camera to reveal any
markings of age about the eyes and mouth,
but they can afford to be quite truthful
otherwise. Her grace and graciousness are
good to see. The lady, who one time was
Sirs. Nat Goodwin, retains all the “majesty”
of comeliness that distinguished her of yore.
She is a queen among actresses —a Diana
of the movies.
“Bellowdrama”: Whether our advance-
agent didn’t know a pub from a printery, or
whether the cash-box was empty, never trans-
pired ; certainly the Wild Dog Banner
didn’t get the usual ad. The Wild Dog
dramatic critic, nevertheless, witnessed the
opening performance, and on the following
morning hit us and the Tasmanian West
Coast with this :
The “Belle of the Bowery” Dramatic Co. opened
its season in the Gaiety Theatre last night. The theatre
was well ventilated, and during the evening the
orchestra rendered one or two pleasing items.
We packed our stock-in-trade early, and
boarded the first outgoing train.
“T. D. Emdee”: There has been some con-
fusion over the two Normans in Bland Holt’s
co. Albert was Bland’s stipendiary villain;
Harry (no relation) was stage-manager,
Holt himself doing the producing and direct-
ing. I recollect the entrance of the Nor-
mans into the profession. Albert, one of
the finest-looking men on the stage and the
only one I know who could wear tights with-
out pads, gained a lot of experience in Dan
Barry’s co. Later on he joined Plain Bill
Holloway and supported Essie Jenyns.
Albert was a good all-round Sliakspearean
actor. He married Blanche Lewis, also in
the business, and a sound artist. Eventu-
ally he became a martyr to rheumatism and
I have seen him walk briskly^—Heaven only
knows at what cost of physical pain—on
to the stage when he could hardly hobble along
off it. When he retired the Australian stage
lost a fine player. Harry Norman joined
Holt about 32 years ago, when in his teens,
I should say. He grasped the technical side
of the business rather speedily, soon “held
the book” and eventually became
ager. But he was at no time out of the
ordinary as an actor.
“E.T.”: Australia’s old limelight friend
Robert Courtneidge, not content with man-
aging and. producing, is seeking further kudos
and kwids as writer. He is the author of a
musical-comedy “book” entitled “Petticoat
Fair.” The thing was tried on the provincial
dog a few weeks ago with a strong cast,
which included comedians William Cromwell
and Edwin Brett, both well known on this
side of the world.
ACTIN’.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Life all round’s a jolly farce.
Dad pretends he’s awful sore
Jus’ because Sain cleared away
lted-hot on the scent of war.
Now Sam’s wearin’ his M.C.
Dad’s as scotty as can be—
Jus’ actin’!
And though Mura is awful proud,
Not a word she dares to say,
Else she’d have Dad cursin’ loud
At the pigs the live-long day;
So she cracks she’s also sore
’Cos Sam’s sniffin’ round for gore—
Jus’ actin’!
E’en our chickens love to play
At pretendin’ what they’re not;
By their cacklin’ one would say
They were layin’ quite a 10t....
Like some human frauds we know
Cacklin’ on through life they go—
Jus’ actin’!
VICTORIA.
RITA SUNYASEE.
HIS AUDIENCE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
I met him after all these years •
At some convivial gathering.
Time has intensified his airs
Of self-assurance. He can sing
His own warm praises as, of old,
And thrice the company was told
Of his success—how much of gold
He,-could from fervent patrons wring.
He cornered me with such a tale.
“I have my clientele, you see.
No artist, sir, on this poor vale
Can hold his audience,” said he,
“As I these faithful friends of mine.
Whatever I essay’s divine
To my. good following. In fine
Through good or ill they swear by me.
“They come whene’er I choose to act;
They listen to my every word;
And every time the place is packed,
And every heart is truly stirred.”
I listened, wondering at the same,
For still I dabble in the game,
And William Horace Spence’s name
For years and years I had not heard.
To-day 1 caught his strident roar.
A terrace house. I stood apart,
And presently from out the door
Beheld a dozen youngsters dart.
Here was his paying audience!
This said the- plate upon the fence:
“Professor William Horace Spence,
Instructor in Dramatic Art!”
VICTORIA.
