[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bolinski’s getting on in life,
He’s well towards seventy-five,
Yet faces he the city’s strife,
And long he may contrive
To live to see the new years dawn
While shicksers still have goods to pawn.
Bolinski has a neighbor, Cohn;
Their friendship fairly new;
The train Bolinski travels on
His neighbor uses, too;
And riding in they talk of trade
And ways by which, good money s made.
The eve of New Year’s Day and stout
Bolinski went with Cohn,
And took his season ticket out.
Cohn turned his neighbor on:
“Vot risks you’re runnink,” wailed the sage—
“A yearly • ticked at your age 1”
VICTORIA.
BILLY T.
“J.F.11.”: Passed out, Horace Cox, long
time head of the big Srm that produces vari-
ous London weeklies — Field, Queen, Law
Times, etc. It was from his house that “An
Australian in China,” by Dr. Morrison, the
Geelong boy who is now Political Adviser
to the Chinese Republic, was issued. Horace
Cox as a youth ran away from a farm and
made his way to London, where he sought
out a relative who was running the Law
Times, got a job and eventually a partner-
ship. He remained on the premises over
50 years.
“Quidam”: Especially in the country, no-
body gets so scandalously overpaid for his
services as the undertaker. For instance,
for taking a corpse from the hospital to the
cemetery in one up-country town, a distance
of about a mile, and carrying out a funeral
of the plainest description, the charge was £B.
For trotting out with two ancient horses and
a venerable hearse, worth at most £25, and
planting a deceased in the local cemetery,
an afternoon’s work for one man, the bill
was £lO. Compaied with the charges for
other services rendered to the public this is
grossly exorbitant. A motor-car can be hired
to do the same trip for £2 or under; and
the actual cost of the coffin was trifling.
It’s about time the cost of dying was con-
sidered along with the cost of living.
“F.N.T.”: I see (B. 2/l/’l9) that “An-
zac” is now on the map as the name of a
village in the Canadian Rockies. A cloth-
ing-manufacturer in the U.S. recently
adopted the name as a brand for a make
of boys’ clothes. Isn’t it time, if some
American tradesman hasn’t already fore-
stalled the Commonwealth Government, that
this country’s administration registered
“Anzae” under U.S. laws-—and that of
other countries, is necessary—and prohibited
its use for trade purposes there, as is done
here? Melba, when she reached N’York on
one of her pre-war trips, found that a local
product had been given her stage name,
whereupon she promptly had “Melba” regis-
tered and warned off the offenders.
“D.”-: F. E. Smith, the new Lord Chancel-
lor in England, is a society man with brains.
His metier, hitherto, has been “leading” in
a cause c&ebre in which he already knows
the principals and most of the witnesses on
both sides. Not by any means a great law-
yer, but a great advocate by reason of his
knowledge of his world and of human nature
generally, of his readiness of speech, and of
his extraordinarily attractive appearance.
He has had his failures. For example, re-
cently, in America, where he went as a sort
of ambassador and ended by being accepted
as a mild joke. The antithesis of the im-
pression created by his fellow-lawyer, Lord
Reading—born Rufus Isaacs. Smith comes
of bourgeois, Nonconformist stock, and his
father was at one time a sergeant-major in
the “Regulars.” Leaving the Army Smith
senior became a barrister, and “F.E.” pro-
ceeded to Oxford wifh a scholarship. Since
when he has steadily gone ahead —socially
(which means much in London), at the Bar
and in the House.
“Horatio” whoops:—
Some years ago when my hard-hearted relatives
iri the Old Country put it to me plainly that I must
either stop drinking and cease from borrowing, or
else keep on drinking and live in Australia on a
strictly-limited allowance, I chose the hero’s part—
that is to say, Australia and the s.l. allowance. At
that time I hated Australia because it sounded like
a lonely cabin in the woods. But, thanks to the
policy of centralisation and the splendid Micawber-
like attitude of Australia’s politicians, all the dear
boys are clustered together in one or two cities, and
nobody cares' a damn for the cabin in the scrub.
This atmosphere suits my temperament. 1 have had
a devilish good time ever since I landed, and only
noticed a slight slackening of the fill-’em-up-again
spirit during the period of the war. Now all that
wretched fag is over, and I can see a splendid time
coming. Even in the dear Old Country, so used up
downing the beastly Hun, they are arranging
grand pageants. Now, if they can do that there,
what shall we not be able to do when we start! I
see a dashed top-hole time ahead in dear Australia,
where money simply doesn’t matter. These Royal
Commissions and Auditors-General make a frightful
fuss when some johnny in power goes and pays a few
thou, more than he need have done; but I’ve got
no sympathy at all with these pettifogging book-
keepers. The Australian knows how to live like a
gentleman, and when he buys a thing he doesn't
count the change like some bounder of a clerk out
on a holiday. These bally pawnbrokers who are
always overhauling the accounts are just the sort of
people who would drink by themselves instead of
asking a sportsman to drink with them. I like this
care-free spirit with money, and I am looking for-
i ward to the Australian celebrations when peace
comes. The only thing I am sorry about is that
Australia hasn’t got a Minister for Fireworks and
Public Rejoicings to see the thing is done well, and
that everything is of the best. But the Australian
is such a dashed adaptable beggar that perhaps every
Federal or State Minister in Australia will turn
into an ideal Minister for Fireworks and Public
Rejoicings when the time conies. These are great
times, and this is a magnificent country. In the Old
Country there were often times when, in spite of my
rjatural optimism, I was made to feel that I was a
hopeless loafer with a love of insolvency. In dear,
sunny Australia I feel that I am a man and a
brother absolutely in tune with the splendid
fellows who are running 4he country. It is the right
environment for me. The sunshine suits me, the
drinks suit me, the damn-the-expense attitude suits
me. In fact, everything suits me. What!

The Right Way
to Buy a Suit of
Clothes.
BUYING right is not only a
matter of price but ol getting
value. The “right” in buying
London Tailoring comes in the,gain-
ing of tested fabrics combined with
fine workmanship, not only where you
can see it but where you can’t.
The outside finish and workmanship
of London Tailoring is self-evident.
Some time during the life of a suit
the inside workmanship will manifest
its presence—by keeping the shape,
by wearing better, and by holding the
stylish effect until the very goods
wear out.
The right way zo buy a suit of clothes is to measure
the number of days’ service you will get for each
£\ of cash. Buy London Tailoring on that basis,
and you will buy right.

THOUGHTS AT THE ZOO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“There are crowds of surprising things about
Taronga Park.”
There, are surprising things about this place:
The elephant’s long trunk, the lion’s grace,
The snakes that eat the frogs, the monkey’s face.
The way some creatures spit and others chew,
The various stunts the camel doesn’t do,
The tiger’s stripes, the weary kangaroo.
They say that education can be found
In merely looking up and smelling round
Among the prisoned beasts that hokl the ground.
It may be true. I only see some pain
To creatures eager freedom to regain,
And not a little danger, to be plain.
High flavors, hair, great bulks of uselessness,
That narrowing space and trenches still distress;
ITow these may educate I cannot guess.
Children within the town are hungry still.
The tiger’s cost a dozen mouths would fill;
One camel’s breakfast pay a doctor s bill.
The summer breezes with a gentle kiss
Mix the aromas for the evening’s bliss,
Lest the proud State-kept stink the stars should
miss.
N'.S.W.
MARY McC.
“The Oldest Inhabitant”: Can any reader
identify for me the I’ilgl'im Inn, which stood
atop of Capstone Hill some 80-odd years
ago? It was the first pub met after leaving
Jacob Josephson’s Governor Bourke, at Emu
Ferry.
“C B.”: I met him at night in Fitzroy.
lie fell into step beside me, and after a
couple of preliminary Observations, men-
tioned that it was a hot and thirsty evening.
I agreed. He spoke words of indignation
anent the tyrannical Government which
dried up the wells of gladness at six o’clock.
I agreed some more. This, apparently,
caused him to decide that 1 was a promising
subject, and drawing a bottle from under-
neath his coat, he proffered “a good pull for
a tanner.” When I declined his attitude
was that of a man whose valuable time has
been trifled with. A few nights later a
friend of mine was similarly approached in
the same suburb. The recent energy of the
Ipolice in raiding unlicensed shanties seems
to have called into existence the peripatetic
groggery.
“Riverina”: In 1916, when cheese was
plentiful among the MX. troops, our boys
had a playful way of increasing the rat death-
rate. A Digger would spear a piece of
cheese with his bayonet, then put the muzzle
of the rifle round a corner and wait. As
unfailingly as a politician to a banquet along
would come a rat and begin the feast. A
little extra pressure on the trigger did the
rest.
“Thomas De Kaye” : Re castor oil (“G.A.”
28/11/T8). The plant in Australia can be
made to produce excellent quality seeds. As
a matter of fact, the commercial castor seed
is found on plants growing wild in all the
States, but more especially in W.A. and S.A.
There is a market in Melbourne for 3000
tons of castor seed per annum. Up to the
present all the seed used has been brought
from Java and India, and recently the price
has been in the vicinity of £3O per ton. I
have tried to wake up various Departments
of Agriculture on the matter, but officialdom
merely opened one eye, turned over and went
to sleep again. In India a fair yield is three-
quarters of a ton per acre, so even when the
price is normal (about £l2 per ton), the
returns per acre on suitable country should
be encouraging. Harvesting is not difficult,
and can be done by women and children.
A. E. V. Richardson, Supt. of Agriculture
in Victoria, who returned recently from
America, gives an instance of the distaste
in that part for “knocking” one’s country.
He dropped into two big wheat provinces
in Canada, where they had been suffering
from an Old Man drought. Yet he hadn’t
heard a whisper about it until he struck the
localities. Knowing that if the same thing
happened here the papers would be full of
it, he asked and was frankly told that they
didn’t see any sense in advertising droughts
and other calamities. Still, the way of
the newsrag is hard. A few years ago, in
a dry spell, the usual flaming reports started
coming into one big office in Sydney. Hav-
ing a brain wave the editor decided to
smother them, arguing that they didn’t do
the drought-stricken cocky any good, or the
country either. Silence followed. Then
came ferocious yells _ from cocky, accusing
the paper of callous indifference to his suf-
ferings. The only visible explanation was
that cocky saw in the drought an oopor-
tunity that was being wasted to bleed the
Government for remittance of rent and for
free seed and fodder, special railway rates,
etc.
“The Oldest Inhabitant’s” yarn:—
The N.E. corner of Market and George streets,
Sydney, at which a big Are occurred recently, is one
of the most noted spots in old Sydney. In Governor
Bligh’s time the corners of Market-street and the
western end of the street itself were a leasehold of
John Fleming, who was permitted to join the New
South Wales Corps and rose to the rank of ser-
geant. Having cleared his time, he desired to return
to England, and sold his lease to John Blaxland for
£7OO. In course of years Blaxland parted with the
lease, Market-street was opened up, and the original
Dan Cooper secured both corners. On one he built
the Waterloo Warehouse, which was for years a com-
mercial landmark. John Hunter now occupies the
corner. On the opposite, or northern, corner, Tom
Burdekin had an ironmongery business, which he
sold to one John Thomas Wilson, who in 1839 cleared
out with some thousands of pounds’-worth of pro-
perty, leaving his creditors lamenting. Soon after
that, Thomas Baker, who had an inn, The Crown and
Anchor, in George-street, nearly opposite the G.P.0.,
removed his sign to the corner of Market-street,
where about the year 1850 Henry Roberts took over
the business, and it has been in the Roberts family
ever since. On the site of Blow’s Crown Studios 80
odd years ago was one Hawley, an ironmonger, and a
door or two south stands the old house in which John
Sands first started business. In Market-street, ad-
joining Roberts’s Hotel, stood the City Theatre.
Some portion of the building is gtill standing. A
couple of years ago Farmer’s Limited bought the
freehold of Roberts’s Hotel (14 years of the lease
unexpired), which gave the firm control of the entire
block, George, Market and Pitt streets, one of the
most valuable sites in Sydney.

Constipation is dangerous be-
cause it means that poisonous
materials which should have been
expelled from the body are being
retained. The appetite fails, diges-
tion becomes disordered and the
breath offensive. Pinkettes, the new
laxative, are a real help in correct-
ing constipation.
They do not aggravate the com-
plaint as strong purgatives do; and
they do not wear out in their effects.
Taken according to directions they
assist the patient so that the use of
an opening medicine is not a con-
stant necessity.

Melba’s second farewell concert in Syuney
was a testimonial to John Lemmone. A
record audience, squeezed into every crack”
of the Town Hall, had in scores of places to
take its music standing. The G.-G.’s seats
were crowded so close to the platform that
the v.r. circle slowly backed, for si craned
neck does not make a pleasant evening. The
Dame, in white velvet, illuminated with dia-
monds and emeralds, wore as accessories
long-winged sleeves of green tulle _ that
floated over the vista of skirt. Assisting
were Leila Doubleday, her melodious fiddle,
Francis de Bourguignon, his piano, and
Andrew Black The veteran singer, not so
often' heard of late years, appeared at John’s
special request. He began as nervously as
a schoolgirl, but warmed up later in an old
Scottish song. The Sydney Madrigal Soci-
ety lent its aid. W. G. Layton was hon.
treasurer, and J. E. Brownlow the hard-
worked manager of a concert that panned
out £2OOO for invalid John.
In 1914 Sydney yatchsmen, as a body,
gave up the pleasant habit of flying before
a stiff nor’-easter on summer afternoons.
With the Peace Conference fighting its way
to a settlement, the Harbor fleet has just
had its first big rendezvous for nearly five
years. Surgeon Alex. MacCormick, commo-
dore of the R.S.Y.S., on the Morna, flew
the signal “Follow me in line,’’ and with
the G.-G. and a big party on board led the
way to the anchorage off Steel Point.
Walter Marks, commodore of. the Prince
Alfred Club, is away on war service, but a
vice-commodore took his place in the toast
list on the flagship Argus. There were no
shipwrecked . mariners; but some ■of the
It.S.Y.S.’s guests were aboard a steamer
which had its anchor caught in a rock. So
they sat tight in the same place till 7 p.m.
Some of the masseuse girls who put on
freshly-starched pinnies for the event never
had even a bird’s-eye view of Excellency
Ferguson at Prince Alfred Hospital the other
afternoon. He was there, nevertheless, with
Lady' Helen and a bodyguard. Prof. Andy
Stuaft —the institution has grown from a
small hospital to a hamlet under his eye —
Albert Gould, Dr. Nash. Matron Boissier
(who got the Royal Red Cross for her ser-
vices at the Front), Dr. Sinclair, Superin-
tendent Clayton, Secretary Epps and A.D.C.
Duncan made an imposing retinue.
Welfare-work, that balm which is rubbed
by the best of l!.S>. employers into the sensi-
tive cuticles of their employed, is getting
more attention here. A promising sample
was opened last week by Melba in a top
storey of Farmer and Co.’s workrooms, in
Kent-street. A Social Welfare Hall —it
sounded dull, but a few casual guests of
the co. were aghast to find themselves the
Cinderellas of a frilly party, the frilliest of
it being the attractive staff. The Dame, in
a blush-pink evening-goWn, was assisted out
of a gorgeous black-and-gold wrap before
mounting the platform. Directors Norman
Pope and James Chalmers addressed all
hands briefly. Neither would give away the
inner secrets of their hearts re helpings of
Welfare to come, but they agreed that the
time was ripe, and that from this new
centre greaj; things might proceed. The hall
—large and handsomely decorated —has a
stage which the staff can use for concerts,
theatricals, etc. Electric light falls in softly
from ground-glass globes.
Remember' Gerald Mussen, once a news-
paper man in these parts? He has been
taking a hand in another sort of welfare-
work —for the big smelting company at Port
Pirie (S.A.). A Palm Beach has been built
for employees and their families, well away
from the dust and dirt of the drab port,
and the other day it was opened with cheers
and other refreshment. Workmen who don’t
miss more than I-forget-how-many days in a
year can have a fortnight on full pay in cot-
tages provided for them, water and lighting
free and champagne and chicken at cost
price. The Broken Hill Prop, is watching
results; and.if it gets any encouragement it
will follow suit at Newcastle.
An anxious Australian girl has, like many
others, been waiting for the ships to resume
their Maoriland trips. Lyle Bowen, who
was to have been married in Sydney on
Christmas Eve, hopes now to see her ’groom-
to-be within a week. He’s Frederick Flan-
agan, -for many years \ aluer-General over
there. Headquarters in Wellington.
Nobody has mentioned the thing that
made the stewards on these M.L. boa.ts stick
out for concessions for taking 'flu’ risks.
When the Makura was sent into quarantine
there weren’t enough stewards to go round
among the three or four hundred passengers
who were put ashore; so a few waiters had
to be hired in Sydney. They got 15s. a day
and 2s. 9d. an hour overtime, and four
hours’ overtime wasn’t a rare thing. Twenty-
six shillings a day—and the stewards, doing
the same work and belonging to the same
union, got five-and-soinething! From that
hour the Union Co. was booked for trouble.
The spinning-wheel which turned in war-
time with Bill’s socks as its objective is a
novel addition to the toast-rack and butter-
cooler of the modern bride. The Moore-street
Camelero depot girls gave Daphne King
(lately wed to Dr. Roberts, of Candelo) a
handsome wheel. The new Mrs. Roberts
was for some time chief spinner for this
comforts fund.
John Spencer Brunton and his impressive
lady—an amateur contralto who would have
had a Career if fortune hadn’t lapped her in
happy domesticity—went off in the Niagara.
A youthful soldier son on the other side is
the magnet. ' The Bruntons, who hospitably
turned their Darling Point mansion upside
down a year or two ago to give Melba the
rooms she fancied, have the Dame as a
fellow passenger.
En route for home, Lieut.-Col. Ted Norrie,
who owns a D. 5.0., an Aussie wife, and a
daughter, all acquired since he left . for
Anzac, after a term at Rabaul. He is a
son of late solicitor Norrie and Mrs. Norrie,
of Rose Bay.
One batch of home-coming Aussies who
travelled via Panama gives a cheero for Pav-
lova, the Russian dancer, who was in Colon
when they called. She sent along a big sup-
ply of smokes and fruit and such-like to
our boys.
Edward Mulcahy, who succeeds ex-Sena-
tor Long, is a much-married man. He has
three times faced the music. First and
second wives were sisters, and all three were
products of the Speck. A son lives in Syd-
ney, and a daughter is married to the son of
Hobart’s archdeacon.
Homeward bound, Major E. O. Milne, son
of late Railway Commissioner Milne, and a
1914 inan who did great transport work. He
is accompanied by his wife,, and packed
away in their luggage along with the D.S.O.
is a new Croix de Guerre.
Engineer McEnally, who set out for Papua
on the Matunga, but reached Ilunland in-
stead, will be home directly. Mrs. McEn-
ally, of Manly, his wife, is greatly rejoicing.
Also, with his course set for Aussie is Cap-
tain Donaldson, who was captured on the
same ship.
Australia grows the finest wool, soldiers
that rival the world’s best, and the largest
crop of musicians to the acre. Its vocalists
are becoming so
plentiful that some
industrious person
should compile a dic-
tionary of striking
native nouns. Where
the aspirant decides
to adopt a profes-
sional label, a name
with a eucalyptus
flavor promises to be
a rare advt. in the
years to come. Mar-
jorie Beeby, daughter
of the Minister who
is in U.S. just now,
will, for her part,
stick to her own
home-grown tag. A
pupil of Roland
Foster at the Con-
servatorium, Marjorie at 20 summers is
already counted amongst the intellectuals.
With a generous helping of her dad’s brains,
the Sydney girl has a voice of exceptional
beauty; and she hopes to go to Europe
presently to learn the rest of the musical
catechism. Oratorio is likely to have
another star in the next few years, and
its name will be —guess !
Through a Brisbane periscope;—
Robert Philp and some of his household, and W.
H. Barnes, MX.A. for Buiimba, his wife and his son
Cedric and his wife, have . fled south. They are
going to look in at Melbourne on their way to Tas-
mania.
G. H. M. Addison, architect, fed a bunch of
grey-haired boys and slightly younger soldiers at
our best cafe on Monday night to celebrate the home-
coming of his Anzac son. There was a partnership
with dad waiting for this young man.
Aid. McGuire arrived at the last City Council mtet-
ing with fury in his eye and a folded English news-
paper in his hand, and read to the almost suffocating
mayor a par. stating that Mr. Watt had forbidden the
flying of a Red Flag on the Brisbane Town Hall. It’s
easy enough to mangle “Trades Hall” into “Town
Hall.” But England remains calm.
James Allan, of the Australian Y.M.C.A., is back
again after 10 months in England. His job was
chairman and treasurer of the London executive in
England and France.
Novelist Kathleen Watson-Dearden and her daugh-
ter are holidaying in the bush near \ arraman. Son
Charlie is keeping his eye on the paper-pulp manu-
facture which goes on at the Queensland Pine Co. s
milt there.
Cupid has ended the public career of Matilda
Maud Henneasy, only female clerk of Petty Sessions
in Bananaland. A few weeks ago Matilda said I
will” to one McGrath, of the Esk Club hotel.
Pianist Francis de Bourguignon, born in
Brussels and trained in its Conservatorium,
took his diploma and became a teacher
there at 18 —a
record so far as
that musical hub is
concerned. On Au-
gust 4, 1914, he
enlisted. After a
fortnight’s training
he, like thousands
of other young Bel-
gians, went straight
to the trenches. Bel-
gium had mislaid
its soul. “Only one
out of each family,’"
the young pianist
says, “had to go
through any mili-
tary training, and
even this was not
carried out with
efficiency. Then, too,
for £65 you could buy a man to take your
place in the conscription list—and the
bought man, what could he be?” After
fighting at Termond and Malines, the
pianist was severely wounded at Antwerp.
“I knew no more,” he says, “till I woke
up in a London hospital, to wonder what
had befallen me.” Given 12 months to
I recuperate, in 1915 he came to Australia
and met Melba, who has not only engaged
him for many of her concerts but has
been most kind and hospitable to him.
Coombe Cottage lias been his Australian
home. Returning to report himself in
London for service again, a Board dis-
charged him as medically unfit. He toured
South America; then went on to North
America, and in 1917. Melba, who was
then in the U.S., again engaged him for
a tour. He goes home to Brussels within
the next two or three months. A crowded
career for a young man in the early
twenties. Photo by Judith Fletcher.
With Mrs. Connell in the chair, mothers,
wives and sweethearts of soldiers and sailors
met in the Town Hall vestibule to return
thanks to the Red Cross and other volun-
tary war-workers. Then there was talking
and talking and more talking—from Chaplain
Green, Chaplain Wilson, State Treasurer
Fitzpatrick and Opposition-leader John
Storey; and a silken scarf of red, white and
blue was sent to Lady Davidson (unable
to be present) from the Women’s Associa-
tion.
The French Government has handed Mrs.
R G. Bowen (formerly Corinne Bruce-
Nicol) the Medaille de la Reconnaissance
Francaise for three and a half years’ devo-
tion to French Red Cross work in Queens-
land. Of French extraction, Mrs. Bowen
is the wife, of that Lieut.-Commander who
was badly wounded at the Rabaul landing
and is now in Westralia.
Lieut.-Commander Moyes, R.A.N., from
Sc-uth Aus., recently married a good-looking
Strathfield girl, Miriam King. The ’groom
is an F.R.G.S., having been a member of the
Mawson party.
“Half a crown a minute” is the catchword
in Prince Alfred Hospital’s struggle for
cash. As the Sydney citizen worms his
way through the crowded streets, seductive
voices challenge his steps. “One thou-
sand pounds for a shilling,” is the lure.
This fat war-bond is one of the prizes in
the P.A.H. Art Union. Somebody will
achieve the record deal on April 30. The
huge healing haven in the ’Varsity paddocks
actually costs 60 times 2s. 6d. every hour
to keep it solvent.
The Peace Ball Committee has slightly
altered its objective, which is now the
raising of the wind for the Church of
England Home for kiddies. Tickets one
guinea. Donations accepted with enthusi-
asm by Mrs. Stuart Osborne and Mrs.
Walter Brunton, hon. treasurers. Likely
to be the most dressy. event of Easter
race week. /
The two extra-good-looking -laughters of
Mr. Willgoss (Gordon and Gotch) are en-
gaged to marry respectively a King and a
Prince. Fortunately only the names—not
the jobs—of the bridegrooms-elect are royal.
Matron Creel, who succeeded Matron
Nellie Gould at Sydney Hospital, was in
the recent list of nightingales due for Royal
Red Cross decorations. She is—or was a few
weeks ago—chief comfort in the big Aus-
tralian Hospital, Cairo.
From an Australian girl:—
The Hotel Vancouver, 8.0., is these days the
happv meeting-place for war brides and Australian
soldier-husbands who are' dashing from France across
America to regather up their families. Mrs. Jimmy
Burns was an exception to the general rule. She
has gone across America to London to meet her man.
Captain and Mrs. Paul—she was a Toowoomba Mac-
Callum —have just met and gone home with a small
son. Lieutenant and Mrs. Jack Chandler had a
second honeymoon at Honolulu before going back to
Queensland. Lieutenant Fairbairn had no wife to
meet him, but he didn’t lack a sympathetic audience
when he told of his adventures as a prisoner in
Germany.
Kathlene Macdowell rushed straight to New York
as soon as the ship was anchored at Vancouver.
She is now busy rehearsing a new play which is
said to promise her even more scope than “Daddy
Long Legs.” Margaret Wycherley’s return to
America was hindered by ’flu’ in Auckland. She
had to go to Wellington and ship by the Moana
to San Francisco, where her husband, Bayard
Vieller, impatiently awaited with the script of his
newest play, in which the heroine is said to be a
cross between Joan of Arc and Mary Turner of
“Within thG Law.”
Lots of American women thought they’d rush to
London and Paris as soon as the ink was dry on
the armistice. Officialdom in stern tones said “No.”
For six more months they must wait, and then they
may not. be allowed to go. In revenge they bought
more clothes and danced new dances at the rate
of a thousand steps to the bar.
Torn Simpson, Tom Monaghan and one of
the Ellison brothers sail away in the
Marathon. They’ve just finished their
course at Duntroon. Instead of going
straight to the Front they will have to
do 12 months “finishing” service on the
other side. Some Neutral Bay girls sped
the boys with a launch picnic last week.
Lieut. Ted Billyard Leake, D. 5.0., of
the Royal Navy, and one of the heroes of
the Vindictive-Zeebrugge incident, was wed
in England during November. The Austra-
lian man’s bride was a Midlothian lass,
Lelia, daughter of Captain and Mrs.
Traquair. An uncle of the ’groom is that
brisk youngster, Captain Moseley—B6 not
out in April—who spends his mornings
working at the War Chest head depot in
Elizabeth-street. Dad is the ex-Sydney man
who lent Ilarefield to the Diggers for a hos-
pital.
A big Sydney clan is interested in a S.
Australian engagement—Miss “Johnny”
Fearon, whose father manages the B.N.S.W.
in Adelaide, to John, son of the late well-
gilded William Barker, of Baldina station.
The 1 girl is a niece of Mrs. Tony Weaver.
A former Gallipoli man, | “Bill” Sheppard,
late of the 17th. was married to the girl of
his heart in St. John’s the other day. The
graceful bride was a Junee maiden, Wini-
fred Plumley. Captain Sheppard, who
added fighting in France to his Gallipoli
experience, returned thanks for the good
wishes of his friends, at the Wentworth
Hotel.
Mrs. Haffenden Smith, whose students’
patriotic concerts have been frecpuent items
during the war, is off. to the other side in
March.
The singing season, which usually closes
up like a defiant oyster during January
and February is still open. Two Melba
concerts this month, the Phil. Pops next
month, and Dolores in the Town Hall on
February 1, 4 and 6 keep the public pretty
well nourished.
The Fruit and Vegetable Fund (16 Cab-
rington-street) means to go on helping
soldiers’ wives and dependants till the last
i is dotted in demobilisation. So you can
go on sending your turnips and ’taties to
the above address. The railways and most
of the coastal shipping cos. generously for-
ward the goods free of charge.
A Maoriland girl, lately in Sydney, got
a gentle shock when the first Harborside
shoplady called her “dear” in the ordinary
way of trade. Miss Maoriland Was used to
the more formal “Madam”; but she soon
got accustomed to the friendliness of her
sisters behind the counters, and saw that
it was a sort of tribute to her youth and
gentle methods. Aggressive matrons with
four extra chins got “Madam” right off the
ice every time.
Died recently at St. Vincent’s, Sydney,
Ernest Joseph Wheble. late of Mayfield
station, Coonamble, aged 64. He was second
son of Lady Catherine Elizabeth AVheble, the
eldest daughter of the third Earl of Howth,
who lived in the picturesque Howth Castle,
on Dublin Bay.

The memory of Major Miles' Seton, shot
dead in London the othey day, will flourish
in many a damsel’s heart. A handsome
bachelor of 45, with tremendous health,
charming manners and more income than he
knew how to play with, he seemed a gift
for any female who was clever enough to
penetrate his inner defences. But he was
rather a reserved man, and when his large
medical practice in East St. Ivilda per-
mitted he spent much time in secret wor-
ship of his Blood. He was a direct
descendant of Mary Seton, maid-of-honor
to Mary Queen of Scots, and favored friends
were allowed a glimpse of a vast family tree
which showed him as a lonely twig on one
of the lower branches. His next of kin is
a lady living in Scotland, so she will pre-
sumably get a big Cornwall oroperty which
the warrior inherited from his mother.
The shooting of the Major seems to have
all the ingredients of a crime passionel. He
was on home service: the Colonel was in
France. The cable-man, his mind dis-
tracted by emotion, said on Saturday:—
Lieut.-Colonel Rutherford throughout has Ibeen
quiet and collected, though he momentarily broke
clown when his wife’s name was mentioned in court.
His friends believe his action was dictated by a deep
sense of chivalry.
Mrs. Rutherford is referred to, significantly,
as “a charming woman with fair hair and
blue eyes.” Seton was for a couple of years
on the staff of Harefield Hospital before
going on to H.Q. staff at Horseferry-road.
The recent announcement of the death of
Mrs. Ferrier Hamilton, at Mrs. McGrath’s
place in Scotland, might have been more
interestingly paragraphed. The name of the
letter lady concealed her identity from most
of the readers. Melbourne knew her well
enough when, as the daughter of J. Mac-
pherson Grant, Minister of Lands, she mar-
ried Frank Stephen, the brother-in-law of
.John Maddc-n. She divorced giddy Frank,
and then, in England, she married Fitzroy
Fletcher, whose death left her enormously
rich. The war had started ere the wealthy
widow married Captain McGrath, of the
Armored-car service—a splendid figure of a
man who is hardly too old to be the lady’s
son. Incidentally one may remark that he
is a connecting link between his wife and
another notorious divorce case. His first
cousin—also a McGrath —is the second hus-
band of beauteous liuby Wallace. You see.
a lot of social interest attaches to the late
Macpherson Grant’s much-married daughter
when the details are filled in.
Tenor Balboni was quoited with wreaths of
laurel after he had gratified his outraged
honor in “Pagliacci” on Saturday at the
Princess’s. Tenor Lois was also lassoed
with a huge bangle of vegetation, and the
loot of various florists fell in profusion about
the feet of the leading canaries, Leah Myers
and Nellie Leach. Nellie, as Nedda, sang
her columbine part in short, stiff, pink skirts
over slim white legs, an effect reminiscent of
the pink frill round a ham-bone.’ Manager
Rigo, greatly pleased with the reception,
spoke several words of thanks and played
“bandies” with conductor Slapoffski, who, he
said, was a fiddler when he (Rigo) was a
call-boy at Covent Garden over 30 years
ago.
In the auditorium, Louise Dyer, wrapped
up in the hearthrug or something like it,
sparkled at the head of her usual procession
of guests. Ex-Gaud Mayor Stapley and his
wife sat in the front of the d.c. with the
sad expression of epicures. The Monash
dame fidgeted with a pink scarf which
threatened to strangle her at any moment.
Solicitor Bona brought along his slender
bride, and there were parties of Kozminskys
and Finks, not to mention organist Steele
with a tableau in black and white. Down-
stairs, Walter Kirby, in command of a whole
box, got into a state of almost religious
ecstasy. He kept his best bouquet for Bal-
boni, and his manner of hurling the vege-
tables ought to make him a terror at
rounders.
Other boxers included the Conways, Lin-
tons, Barry Thompsons and Minogues.
Attorney-General Hall (N.S.W.) and his
wife, were stalled with J. D. Fitzgerald. The
musical Buessts occupied a few seats. Little
Benno Scherek and his wife listened criti-
cally from the front row of the circle. The
Tom Hogans and Mrs. Pohlman were also
present, and Mrs. Wharton hostessed a
party. Fritz Hart was in a state of sup-
pressed excitement, as 18 of the Conserva-
torium girls were in the show.
For the first time in years Melbourne saw
a smart, daintily-dressed grand opera chorus.
It was a startling novelty. When Italian
opera arrives its chorus is invariably shabby.
If the season is a success the chorus puts on
a new apron or a new head-dress, and is
happy—but only a trifle less dingy.
Walter Kirby gave a reception at the
Oriental to meet members of the Rigo Grand
Opera Co. Attorney-General Hall and J.
D. Fitzgerald, from Sydney, made two of
the speeches, and Gaud Mayor Cabena and
ex-G.M. Stapley also orated. The Melbourne
musical set swarmed, and the stars gave
thanks in song. One Henley, a Melbournite
who has spent years in Naples and Florence,
sang loudest. Kirby stretched a point as
host and sang once. The doctors have put
him on musical rations for the time being.
Miss Hems, a Sydney girl who is prima
donna of the first production, was present
in a pretty frock with half an ostrich round
her neck. Leah Myers, another Sydney
star, turned up in the neatest tailorings.
Captain Tahu Rhodes, of the Grenadier
Guards, has been spending two hurried
months in his native land. Badly gassed be-
fore the armistice, Captain Rhodes got leave
and fetched out his wife, the Hon. Helen,
daughter of one-time Governor Plunket and
niece of our own Ladj' Helen Ferguson, for a
short sojourn in Christchurch. The youth-
ful pair returned recently by the Arawa.
Picturesque Mrs. Tracy quitted Melbourne
again last week ; she returns to her old job
as official lecturess for the Commonwealth
in Britain. Her subject is Australia, and
she used to deliver, on an average, 12 dis-
courses per week. Several people, now in
Australia, who saw and heard her do it have
declared that, taking one consideration with
another, they reckoned her the most effec-
tive platform speaker in skirts they ever
listened to. Mrs. Tracy’s only trouble last
week was that she would have to leave be-
fore she could arrange for a local perform-
ance of son-in-law Howard Carr’s impres-
sive musical composition, “The Three
Heroes.” This was included by Sir Henry
Wood in every performance of the Halle
Orchestra during its latest tour of Britain.
Quite a number of soldier-boys are angry
with this Letter for having commented on
the emptiness of the vice-regal box at Flem-
ington on New Year’s Day. The spacious
enclosure did look cold and empty on Jan. 1,
but that was only because there was a
lack of maimed Diggers to claim a place in
it. From the time when Flemington began
to be attended by one-legged boys in khaki
the vice-regal box has been open to them and
no other people. At the Cup meeting it was
full of invalids, more than one legless soldier
being carried there in his cot. So the boys
won’t hear a word said in criticism of tiie
V.R.C. The Club has been good to them
abroad and at home. Up to now it has spent
£BO,OOO in comforts and luxuries for Aus-
tralians on active service, and the expendi-
ture is. still proceeding. And didn’t the
V.R.C. invest the whole of its reserve fund
in the War Loan?

The Western District has been spending
a lot of petrol in speeding across-country
to give Captain Gordon Chirnside the glad
hand on his return from France to his
native sheep-country. The young man has
been in khaki for four years, and for the
latter part of his stay in France was A.D.C.
to Birdy.
Squatter Robert Landale passed out last
Friday at 52. Squatter Bob was the son
of Squatter Robert and took oyer the
management of his father’s sheeperies long
ago. The Landales have been noticeable
figures in society for many years, the girls
being beauties with much money and many
startling frocks. Bob Landale was a direc-
tor of Dalgety’s and a prominent Riverina
turfite.
Brig.-General Robert Smith, of the Mel-
bourne Wool Exchange, garnered the follow-
ing war-honors during his absence abroad:
C.M.G., D.S.O. with bar, M.C. and Croix de
Guerre. Smith, who was given a rousing
welcome home last week by the members of
the Exchange, is a son of that wool-wise
John Smith who runs the old Como wool-
scour at Abbotsford.
The Atlee Hunts are making a sailor of
their lad. Bob, who is one of the Marathon
contingent on its way to help man the Aus-
tralian warboats.
Octogenarian Mrs. Joseph Duffett, whose
death is reported from the fatted pastures
of Dunkeld, was the widow of a man who
brought a very substantial bank-account
with him when he decided to join the
western district squatterhood. The late Joe
Duffett was in an earlier day a lawyer with
a succulent family practice, the profits from
which were largely devoted to the purchase
of grazing property. Son Edmond, well-
known among the sheepmen of the western
district, died last year.

Norman Brookes met his wife in Sydney
at the end of last week, and they are coming
on together to Melbourne. He is just back
from Red Crossing, a job he has been at
since the verv early days in Cairo. He
could have had a knighthood, but declined—
for one reason, because others in the Red
Cross movement whom he considered had
done as much as he were not offered the
decoration. If, indeed, one can call it a
decoration.
Lieut. Philip Aitken, who marched to the
war as a private, has the Military Cross.
Phil is the youngest of the Scotch Aitkens
of Torbam, Toorak. His father directed
Dalgety s for many years with a rough man-
ner which concealed a kind heart. They
are all made of solid stuff and do their job
without fuss.

The John Maddens’ home, Cloyne, dropped
to the status of a hash-house when the

Adelaide’s secrets and so forth :
Half social Adelaide was at the station on Monday
to say good-bye to the Galway lady and Miss d’Erl-
anger, and the travellers were nearly smothered in
flowers, letters and packages before the train pulled
out. His Ex., who is to stay here till April of
next year, and Miss Macaulay went with them as
far as Aldgate, and then motored back to Marble
Hill.
Major Gordon Campbell, M.C., has had to turn
down the offer to be manager, treasurer and selector
of the Anzac cricket team that is to play in Eng-
land. His doctors have ordered treatment for a
knee injury that will take some weeks, and as
soon as that is done with he is coming back to
S. Aus.
Dr. Souter, author of many a fine verse, has had
word that his daughter has been given the Royal
Red Cross. In the early days of the war she was
at Keswick Hospital, and left in August, 1915, for
Bombay with the Indian contingent of S.A. nurses.
A couple of years later she went on to Salonika,
where she has been ever since.
Westralia’s new commandant, Colonel Heritage,
passed through at tl.e beginning of the week on his
way to the new job, with barely time to shake
hands with various officials on the platform, the
Melbourne express strolling in three-quarters of an
hour late. Another passenger on the train was
Archdeacon Clampett’s son—an Anzac sergeant.
Tramway-manager Goodman is back in Adelaide,
after a trip to England and America that dates
from July, ’l7.
Polo was revived at Plympton on Saturday. A
good many club members are still not back from
the war, but enough old hands turned up for a
sciateh game. Captain Neville Colley, who was No.
1 of the Adelaide senior team in pre-war days, has
joined the Indian Army.
W. B. Poole, who joined the staff of the Savings
Bank in 1869, and was appointed manager in 1896,
is retiring from his job in March.
Elsie Crowther, daughter of the popular
Brighton doctor, has said “Yes" to Lieut.-Col.
Victor Hurley, of the Army Medical. Elsie
got home by the Niagara last trip and is
collecting welcomes and congratulations
together.

to Doris, a daughter of racing novelist Nat
Gould.

member for Swan, began the New
Year by taking unto himself as wife Miss
Tobin, of May lands (W.A.). Before her

of the Shop Assistants’ Union over there.
Catherine Hay Thomson (“Tommy”
among her fellow-press women) has changed
her name to Mrs. T. F. Legge. The event
transpired at a registry office. “Tommy”
was one of the founders of the Austral
Salon, and is a prominent member of the
Writers’ Club and the National Council of
Women.

Cambridge” Cross is established in Mel-
bourne. She finds that reflective articles
of a high moral tone find a ready market in
Yankeeland, but her friends are hoping that

Kings.”

ried his violin about with him as another

Walter Kirby, still on th,e sick list, tenored
one song at Fasoli’s the other night, and
brought in a good sum for the Little Sisters
of the Poor and the old people in their care.
The Little Sisters live from day to day and
never hoard money or goods. The chari-
table are expected to do that for them.
Melba is rather excited about getting hack
to Paris. A few months before the war she
was in her Paris home when she got word
that her father was ill. She fled ,at once,
telling the maids to leave everything as it
was. Then the Hun broke loose and in-
stead of a few months’ absence she hasn’t
seen Paris fob nearly five years.
Some years ago a Melbourne theatrical
girl married a bright lad with considerable
prospects in his profession and a fairly-
sound cash position. Both the parties had
a ready knack of spending, however, and
presently the bills began to pile up faster
than the receipts. After a whirl of expen-
sive good-times, hubby at last ran full on
the rocks. Other things happened, and the
lady got a divorce. After a Avhile the voting
man went off to the U.S. to do better'. lie
did, and now r the slashed bonds are to be
sewn up again by a re-marriage, the ex-
missus having already made her cross-Paeific
departure with this romantic end in view.
Nellie Wilson, back on the Australian
stage, has been in America for years. Sister
Elsie. May lives in Los Angeles. Elsie
married Julian, an actor who was liere with
J-ulius Knight. Julian is now a big film
producer in Los Angeles.
An Australian actress making good in the
more or less United States is Margaret
Linden, whom Melbournians will remember
as a beauty girl in various shows, including
“The Barrier.” She is at the George M.
Cohan theatre in N’York.
.Young Frank Williams, an artistic Adel-
aide soldier, is hoping to produce in the
early autumn in Melbourne a ballet of his
own invention. The performance is to
benefit blinded Billjims. and Melbourne’s
society. girls have, promised to help. The
theme is enacted in a peacock setting, and
the dresses and scenes have all been de-
signed by the Adelaide man. The “Garden
of Eden” ballet (produced in the “Samples”
revue last year) was his triumph.
Mrs. Bob Greig, whom the “gods” love
Beatrice Holloway, has had to put her-
self in the doctor’s hands, owing to nerve
trouble. Her work in “Bubbly” is being
shared by Noel. Geddes, Ethel Bennett and
Clarice Hardwicke. Bob Keers, musical
director at the King’s, is also on the sick
list. During the week his appendix was
amputated, but he is bearing the loss with
fortitude.
William Cosgrove is in Melbourne with a
drama of his own and is getting ready for
a pounce on the next vacant theatre to pro-
duce it. Cosgrove stage-managed that open-
air production of “As You Like It” in Gov-
Hb use grounds and played parts in
Wilkie productions.
Australian visitors to Calcutta write ex-
cited letters about the Victory celebrations
in that comic-opera locality. Thev lasted
tour days and four nights, and Aladdin and
his arc-lamp weren’t in it. The illumina-
tions were gorgeous things made up of mil-
lions of tiny oil-lamps. There were colored
processions by day, and fireworks, dinners
and dances by night till the local owls sent
in their resignations.

Tradition : Just as tradition makes one proud
to be a Britisher, so it promotes pride in your
choice of Dewar's. Long years of good
opinion stand behind the fascinating flavour
and seductive mellowness of Dewar's Whisky -
It is, and always has been; the choice of the
experienced.
Charm l Whenever anything possesses charm,
then genius had a hand in its production. The
persistent power to please of Dewar's Whisky
comes from a genius in blending and distillation
—hence the elusive charm which places Dewar's
far above the ordinary into an ever-widening
circle of praise from men able to discriminate.
Ripeness ; There is a moment in the process
of maturation when Whisky reaches its fullness
of perfection. At that moment and in that
state of absolute ripeness Dewar's is offered
to its patrons —never before or after that one
culminating point of prime condition. And so
Dewar's is ever a constant source of pleasure
and benefit to mind and body.


The British Empire emerged from the furnace of
the world’s great war with her reputation for busi-

because her leaders know the value of King George

Byron Moore, secretary of the V.R.C., is
just turned 80, and still going strong. The slim
and wiry sec., full of ideas and capacity for work,
is a long-standing satire on Government methods of
dealing with public servants. Long years ago, when
Graham Berry was king of Victoria, brainy Byron
Moore, who couldn’t help being industrious in any
billet, figured among the crowd of civil servants
sacked on “Black Wednesday.” The Vic. Lands
Department dispensed with his services, whereupon
he settled' down into a man of affairs and notable
citizen. Had he remained at the Lands he would
doubtless have been restricted to' the old groove.
Moote had no political ambitions after the “Black
Wednesday” episode, but Walter Madden, a fellow-
clerk in the Lands, who was passed out at the same
time, subsequently got into Parliament and became
Minister of the very department in which he had
labored in vain for a modest salary.
Desert Gold has gone—she was shipped to
M.L. last week. Australia loses in her a champion
of champions. She is six and has been racing hard all
her life, and up to the last not only were her legs
as flat as boards and sound, but she was so little
tired that anything that beat her had to
smash a record to do it. Thus at the
last Randwick meeting Poitrelle beat her (w.f.a.)
over IT mile, but he at the same time
had to beat the record Comedy King put up when
he beat Trafalgar. Cetigne had to break a record
in the Craven Plate, and Magpie had to break
another at Flemington to down this incomparable
mare. Almost up to the day she sailed racegoers had
ground for hope that she would be seen at the next
Autumn Meeting of the A.J.C.
Why has Desert Gold, although raced so long on
the iron courses of Australia, never gone wrong?
Apart from her breeding and her perfect conforma-
tion there is another possible explanation. She is
like an ambidextrous man. She is a freak
inasmuch as she can race on either leg. Other
horses, if their riders make them change legs in
a race, are all at sea. They lose lengths as they
spreadeagle about the course. Desert Gold changed
legs of her own accord, at any stage of <a race,
according as she tired on one side or the other,
and did it so perfectly that she never lost a nose-
length as a result. It must have saved her tre-
mendously first and last.
She emphasises, by contrast, the calamitousness of
the unsound horse—which is to say about 90 per
cent, of the horses imported here from abroad. For
example, Redfern. Redfern arrived a little over
twq years ago, with the reputation of being the
second-best horse in England. Because his owner
died, and because in England an owner’s death voids
all nominations, William Cooper was able to get
Redfern for £5OOO. He turned out to have an
enlargement as big as a quince on, the off foreleg
just above the pastern joint; likewise a nasty splint.
Of course he has been a ghastly disappointment as
a raeetaker, and there is no reason to think that
he will ever be anything else. His future, if he
has one, will lie in the stud.
There are two explanations of the count-
less cases like Redfern’s that are seen in Australia.
One is that Britishers, who know as much about
horseflesh as any people in the world, are not such
idiots as to readily ship away their good stuff.
When they have in the past —as notably to Russia
and the Argentine—the prices have been fantastic.
The other is the damage that is done to many of
them on the way over and immediately after land-
ing. After standing for a couple of months their
tissues and tendons get soft, and they tend to go
easily. If they are raced soon —in fact, at any time
under from six to nine months—they will break
down for a certainty. In any case none of them
last—in the sense Desert Gold did. Lanins is a
good example. He won a w.f.a. well in Victoria,
went out at 5 to 2 for the Melbourne Cup
and wasn’t sighted. Starting in another w.f.a. on
the last day of the meeting he was beaten easily.
He came to Sydney for last Easter meeting and
started by taking a w.f.a. in an effortless fashion.
Then he petered out, the fatal work being completed
on the strenuous courses of Queensland. The answer
to any query regarding the whereabouts of the
importations of yester-year is mostly the same,
viz., “at the vet.’s.”
* * ♦ *
Died in England, last week, Earl Cowley,
aged 53. Twenty years ago he was one of the
best steeplechase riders in the United Kingdom.
Amongst other great jumpers owned and ridden
by him was Chair of Kildare. But his heart lay
in the Divorce Court. He put in more time there
in connection with his various matrimonial rows
and those of his friends than any other layman
in London.
Kennaquhair was tried over six furlongs at
Rosehill last Saturday', but that great stayer couldn’t
work up the requisite pace. Carrying 9st. 81b. he ran
nowhere in a race that was won by a neck by
Bimeter (Bst 51b.) from Sydney Damsel (Bst 71b.).
One of the outstanding idiocies of every
racecourse is the backer who is there to bet on
what he calls “information.” The bookies make
90 per cent, of their incomes out of the fools
who think they have exclusive knowledge. There
are no rich trainers. . And yet trainers should be
in a position to know, if anyone is. The likeliest
man to achieve the impossible feat of permanently
defeating the man with the bag is the jockey ;
and it is a fact that there are a few rich jockeys.
No one can be quite so sure of the complete!
deadness of a favorite as its rider. But he may
not know that another formidable animal in the
race is also dead. When both come out as triers
next day, as so often happens, their riders and
all those to whom their riders have given “in-
formation” are under a misapprehension,, and the
only certain profiteer from the episode is McNoses.
The wise jockey—a rare fowl admittedly—is he
who refrains altogether from betting. He is
liable to be laid fairly large sums to nothing by
owners about mounts which they are keen to see
win; and where he is on a good thing of the
sort it is practicable for him to get other sizeable
sums to nothing from the more or less professional
backers. More jockeys’ fortunes are accumulated
on these lines than via their stipendiary punters.
* ■ " * * *
About the most ruinous neddy in N. S.
Wales is the ex-M.L. performer Bimeter. In the
commonest suburban company he has been running
rottenly for seasons. Being unbacked at Rosehill
on Saturday he proceeded to get away in front of
many recognised good sprinters and also to hold
his own in the dash down the straight. Bimeter
has 31b. less in the Challenge Stakes on Saturday,
but no rational citizen will back him on that
account.
* * * *
In the Rosehill Handicap last Saturday
Phast Boy, with five furlongs to go, was 15 lengths
behind the ultimate winner, The Bounce. By the
time the judge was reached he had got to within
a head. Interesting to know u'hat Phast Boy ran
that final five furlongs in, seeing that the time
for the mile and a quarter was 2min. OJsec.
Fate, seemingly, has denied King Offa an
opportunity to prove that he ought to have won the
Melbourne Cup and given backers of the favorite a
joyful day. The leg that troubled him somewhat
after his win at Caulfield is troubling him some
more, and Sam Bloomfield has advised the owners in
England to make a happy father of their haughty
quadruped right away, in which case Offa will be the
second English horse (and “King”) who ran his
last race in the Melbourne Cup jostle, after winning
the big 1 Caulfield event. There was a vast differ-
ence, though, between the careers of Shepherd King
and the other monarch. The former had scored about 20
losers in England and Australia ere taking his one
and only trick. Offa ran but six times, all on this
side, and three successive wins stand to his credit.
At the stud he will, with good luck, be a very
valuable property, provided he transmits “sound-
ness” to his stock.
One fact which may be said to be established
about practically all these English horses is that
however good and game they may be and however
apparently right about the legs, they can’t stand
being raced more than, say, once in a week.
Magpie, who ran second to Gay Crusader in the
Guineas, and who is probably the best English
horse ever imported to the Commonwealth, won
well at Caulfield in a w.f.a. race against a big
field. On the first day of the Melbourne Cup
meeting he downed Desert Gold over her own
distance. One might fairly have argued that he
vvas unbeatable. Yet, later, Wallaroi beat him
(w.f.a.) over a mile, and Kennaquhair beat him
over a mile and a half. King Offa, sore as he obvi-
ously was at Caulfield, would still gallop when he
was asked to ; but not many horses, “imp.” or other-
wise, are like that.
The A.J.C. handed Prince Alfred Hospi-
tal £ll2O last week for special purposes in con-
nection with A.I.F. patients. The exact sum which
the A.J.C. has given to similar charities since the
war started is £83,944 9s. 9d.
In the recent Westralian racing carnival
visiting jockeys took' 14 of the 24 races. Of course
the visitors got the pick of the mounts ; they always
do. Orrell scored six firsts and four seconds, and
Herbert (the strenuous Englishman) four firsts, six
seconds and two thirds.
Before the issue of weights for the New-
market and Australian Cup there are lists out for
the “double,” the price against early fancies
(coupled) for either event being limited to 200 to 1.
The double-event layers lose no time in thrusting
favdrites under public notice. But as a rule the
actual starting prices of the winners of the two
Autumn handicaps at Flemington work out at more
than 200 to 1 for the combination.
* * * *
Tennis-champion Norman Brookes is
back in Australia from Mesopotamia and France.
As a Red Cross Commissioner for Australia he
went oversea in 1915 and returned at the end of
1916; thence to Mesopotamia via England in 1917.
There he was appointed deputy-assistant-direotor of
local resources, with headquarters at Bagdad and
the rank of major. In the ancient city he put
up some great tennis play when the chance offered.
Against a number of first-class English racquets
he won the Bagdad champior.iship, Major Leith-
Ross being runner-up. Brookes describes the courts,
made of a composition of chopped straw and mud,
as the best he ever played on.
* * * *
At Sydney Stadium on Saturday night
“Frosty” Darcy, a 19-3'ear-old brother of the late
Les, met one Cullen in a 10-round 2-minute con-
test. Young Darcy is a bandy-legged welter with
the relentless, not to say cruel, mouth and jaw
in action, and the glad smile out of it, that
characterised Les. He took exactly 40 secs, to
exterminate his unfortunate adversary. The deed
was done by a short hook to the jaw administered
in the family’s best manner.
The Kav-Uren ceremony followed. It w r as
a mournful affair. When Uren attempted to hit
Kay, which he occasionally did, he discovered that
the Westralian had removed himself elsewhere.
Kay rarely attempted to hit Uren, so far as the
writer could see; but, anyhow, there is no doubt
that if he had tried Uren would have been in
another part of the building when the blow fell.
After 20 rounds of ineffectual swiping, dodging and
scuffling the referee decided that Kay’s was the
less rotten performance, or words to that effect,
and the crowd agreed.
Bob Williams and Pat Gle-eson collided
at Sydney Stadium on Monday night, but after
20 fairly strenuous rounds the referee decided to
leave the lightweight championship of N. S. Wales
with Robert. It was a near thing.
“L.E.”: We liad pushed the Boche back
about 15 kilometres during the morning, and when I
went along the communication trench the game was
in full blast. The kip-tosser had headed them half
a dozen times, and the tail backers were thinking
about mortgaging their deferred pay. A clank of
ironmongery followed, and I turned off to investigate.
Against the spinner’s roll of franc notes the chief
tail supporter had wagered his morning’s souvenirs—•
•1 spiked helmet, 1 gas mask, 2 wrist watches, 7
badges and 1 pair of field-glasses, the lot reposing
on a silk cross cut from a Hun flier’s wing.
* * * *
C. Y. Grimmett, who will bowl for Vic.
v. N. S. Wales this week-end, is one of S. Mel-
bourne’s very best. Six for 21 v. University and
four for 17 v. Fitzroy are a couple of his efforts
this seaso.n with the ball, and he can also bat.
* * * *
“Q.P.”: The Vic. C.A. did well to refuse
to halve the umpire’s screw. Nobody earns his £1
harder than the umpire. Sometimes he earns a lot
more. Crockett’s notorious ordeal on Sydney Cricket
Ground, for instance, during the match v. Warner’s
team could hardly have been expressed in terms of
cash.
* * * *
Major A. G. Moyes, M.C., who returned
from France the other day, was one of S, Australia’s
best batsmen in 1914. He will never need a pad
on his left leg again.
* * * *
S. A. Middleton and J. B. Lane (both
captains in the A.1.F.) are among the many Aus-
tralian sportsmen to win D.S.O.’s. Sid Middleton
went to England with the Australian eight in 1912;
also with the N. S. Wales Rugby Union “Wallabies”
in 1908-9. “Paddy” Lane played cricket and foot-
ball for N. S. Wales before doing his bit in Rabaul,
Gallipoli and France.
* * * *
W. S. McCloy’s 236 (not out) for Pad-
dington against Central Cumberland at Parramatta
(N.S.W.) on Saturday, is the best individual effort
of the season, and ranks next to Victor Trumper’s
335 (1903) and 260 (1908), and J. R. Mackay’s
251 (1904). McCloy swings .the club adroitly in
Sydney baseball and is a great field.
* * * *
North Sydney swimmer-schoolboy Keith
Kirkland has just done the 100yds. in 59sec. Early
in the season he swirled through a similar distance
in 59 l-ssec. ; and, later, won 100yds. High Schools’
championship in 62sec. He and young Crackanthorp,
of Manly, are likely assailants of world’s records
over their distances.
The first woman to win a long-distance
swimming race in Adelaide is Eileen McKee, only
16, who arrived in front at the Grange jetty after
swimming from Henley Beach, in the annual con-
test. She had 14 minutes handicap, and in a face
of 1 mile 350 yards it proved too much. Still
she well deserved the win, finishing fresh after using
a vigorous double over-arm all the way. Greig,
with five minutes, finished about 100 yards behind.
Eileen McKee’s time in the water was 40min. 4sec. ;
Greig’s was 32min. 7sec. The best time was made by
Beaglehole, of Moonta, one of the two scratch
men—-29min. 31sec. He finished eighth.
* * * *
Strange that no girl swimmer arises to
challenge the supremacy of Fanny Durack and Mina
Wylie. Whenever Fanny stands down Mina scoops
the pool. Latest instance was on Saturday when
Mina, in the absence of her young friend, took the
50 yards N.S.W. championship for the second year
in succession. She equalled her record for the dis-
tance—3o 3-ssec.
* * * *
J. B. Wood, of Brundah (N.S.W.)—pro-
bably the oldest Australian native living till last
week— bred some champions in his time, and the
“3” and “J 3” brands passed anywhere for first-class
horseflesh. Wood went in for course performers when
young, and won three Bathurst Cups in the days
when the race was a three-mile stunt.
* * * *
The good sportsmen of Adelaide, who have
let sport of many kinds fall quite out of use during
the war, have started polo again in a scratch fashion
at Plympton. At the 1914 tournament there were
eight teams in the field, three from the city, the
rest from the country ; but a new lot of ponies will
need teaching. The veteran Downers, Law Smiths
and secretary Laughton were prominent at the re-
opening day.
* * * *
Now that the war is over the prizes for
the Hobart Regatta—alleged to be the oldest thing
of its kind in the world —have been bumped to
£360. This is below the pre-war limit of £5lO,
but a long way above the level of the last few
years. Very few of the Speck’s first-class rowers
have got home yet, though. The coming sports
(February 4) will be the 82nd.
Hunting is to re-open in Victoria next
season. As in the case of picnic-racing it has
been dead since very early in the war, and many
old-time supporters have either been killed, like
Geoff. Kiddle, or badly knocked about, like Reggie
Power.
* * * *
A feature of recent motor-cycle records
in Australia has been the success of Barnet Glass
tyres. In the Sydney-Melbourne reliability trial (680
miles), two of the three riders who gained full
points used them. Fred Berry travelled on them
from Brisbane to Sydney (725 miles in 31hr. 57min.),
and so did S. Ferguson in his recent streak over the
mi.e in 38sec. at Sellick’s Beach (S. Aus.). They
will resist the worst sort of bush tracks; they will
even defy, for many months, a Sydney suburban
road.
* * * *
R- Spears, a N. S. Wales bikist, had the
best all-round record for the just-concluded season
in America. He scored 23 firsts, 2 seconds, 10
thirds and 7 fourths, beating the classic Kramer
by 76 on the aggregate of points. Two Australians,
Grenda and McNamara, were second and third to
Kramer, Grenda being only a point behind. This
is the third year in succession that Australia has
produced a champion in a decadent sport. In
1917 Gullett led the field, and in the previous year
McNamara was best man.
One Odium, of Queensland, the other
day hurried a bike and side-car over a mile
in 54 seconds, apparently establishing a world's
record and chipping 1 2-ssec. Off Australia’s pre-
vious best. Of course, no Victorian or Ma State
crack who is worth his fee to the firm that
employs him will allow that to stand unchallenged.
Two or three earnest lunatics may now be expected
to fkiriously endeavor to make Odium look like a
funeral in Geelong.
“New South” : How many know that the
fresh-water perch of the eastern side of the Divid-
ing Range—and the eastern only—is a “game”
fish? Below the railway bridge at Menangle
(N.S.W.) there is a weir. Above it the Nepean
flows deep and clear for miles. With a boat and
a man to handle it, a 10ft. split-cane rod, light
salmon casts and an assortment of small-size Jock
Scotts and Durham Rangers, you will get as
good sport as is obtainable with grilse or sea-trout
in a Scottish or Norwegian “pool.” The scenery
is reminiscent, too, for the river passes through
precipitously-sided gorges, magnificently timbered,
and is edged in rhany places by the same wonderful
rock formations as one finds lower down when it
has broadened out and become the Hawkesburv.
In addition to being a heroic fighter, the percli
of the Eastern Divide streams is one of the best
table fish in the world.
* * * *
At a patriotic sports-gathering held re-
cently in the presence of some of the local Nicest
at one of Victoria’s wealthy rural villages, the
afternoon’s events included an item which wasn t
oil, the programme, yet caused more excitement than
all the rest of the show. It happened during a
“musical-chairs” competition for men on horseback.
One competitor conceived himself affronted by
another, and they pulled around side by side to
argue. The next moment, without dismounting, the
pair proceeded to “mix it,” while the crowd hummed
like a beehive. The pair banged at one another as
opportunity offered, and finally tied themselves into
a profane knot from which it took all the available
police and several officials to disengage them. It
- was a lively battle while it lasted, and each com-
batant received a decoration.
* * * *
“Duglio”: The 2nd Aussie Siege Battery
claims a football record. Since its first Rugbv
match at Vierstraate (Belgium) in July, 1917, it
not only remains unbeaten, but as yet lias not had
a point scored against it. A Battery team, in seven
matches against brigades and divisions, it has piled
79 points to nil. The last tight squeeze happened
at Turcoing lately, when the best it could do was 11
to nothing against the 44th Royal Irish Rifles.
Handcock (North Sydney) leads ’em, and is assisted
by Stewart (Balmain), Hartmann (South Sydney),
and Cliff Rankine (Geelong).
SHIP-BOARD IDYLL.
(Written after a visit to a Transport Inquiry.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
An undulating tropic sea
Resembling fresh-made glue;
And slowly steaming over it
The transport Plunk C 2.
Abaft the forrard monkey-winch
Observe the poker school.
The padre offers up a prayer—■
Then opens up the pool.
“I’ll bet fi’ bob!” "Fifteen!!” A quid!!!”
“I’ll raise yer ’arf-a-jim!”
The colonel chucks a pair of knaves:
The padre sings a hymn.
“Up ten!” the P.M.O. remarks.
His leggings are awry ;
His bashed-in hat expresses loss;
Likewise his fevered eye.
In vain the purser boosts the pool
With gesture, stamp and shout;
Vain, too, are Private Brown’s attempts
To freeze the padre out.
“I’ll see yer!” howls the bos’n’s mate
(Who holds a king-high “straight”).
The padre places on the deck
Four aces and an eight.
From matins until evensong,
From dusk till day begins,
The service lasts —with one result—
The padre always wins.
They halt for food. Now mark their look
Of undiluted hate.
Lo! he who has absorbed their all
Is taking round the plate!
N.S.W.
10-2-4.

GREGORY NORTH RABBIT BOARD.
Applications for Superintendent of
Works.
APPLICATIONS are invited until Tues-
day, 18th March, 1919, for the posi-
tion of Superintendent of Works.
Applicants to state age and general experi-
ence in the construction and maintenance of
rabbit-proof fencing and rabbit destruction.
Salary four hundred pounds (£400) per
annum. Camel outfit provided in dry coun-
try, applicant to find necessary horses and
plant for the other portions of the district
Applications, together with copies of testi-
monials, to be addressed to the Chairman,
Gregory North Rabbit Board, Boulia,
Queensland, and endorsed “Application for
Superintendent of Works.”
For any other particulars apply to the
Clerk of the Gregory North Rabbit Board,
Boulia, Queensland.
E. S. SCHOLEFIELD,
Chairman.
Boulia, 3rd December, 1918.

taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on the MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted; and no MS. will be returned
unless stamps accompany.

RECOLLECTIONS OF EAST BRUNSWICK.
Poem of Early Boyhood.
I’ve experienced varied changes,
And wandered many ways
Since I left the East of Brunswick,
Where I spent my boyhood days.
Bridk drays in lines did wendeth,
And buses rattled to and fro.
In and outward from the city
In those peaceful years ago.
I can picture the old homestead
With pear and apple tree’s,
Which did gleameth in the sunlight
And ripple to the breeze.
The fragrant blushing roses
And the willow’s spreading shade,
Where with my brother Laurie
In the summer day’s I played;
Our rambles o’er the cow field —
Where the bending thistles grew—
There was a charm within the old years
More deeper than the new.

How the cockies drift in the same boat;
From year to year he’s the same old bloke,
To hear one say he’s the silliest of goats.

Year out year in, and yet
If he cannot pay they get him set,
End of the year, to pay his cheque.

Was he right? No, he wasn’t right.
I hear you ask, “Why did he wrong?”

Fair women love and brave men fight;
“Her” eyes are filled with love’s own light;
And poets write by candlelight—
As poets do when writing a song—
To pay their rent and please the throng.

Dear Mister Sir, I’d like to learn
What socialism means.
Should rich men's things be put to burn
Or chopped up like French beans?
Is it?
Should I a rich man’s goods bespoil
Because I own not his?
I value well for what I toil
At work or business.
With grit.
True socialism, to my mind,
Can best be brought and seen
By each one striving, well inclined,
To lift and not to lean.
Can it?
Please tell me if I’m wrong or right
■To hold to such a view,
And if I’m right, with all my might
I’ll still that course pursue.
You bet.

TO ARTISTIC (AND INARTISTIC)

There is a very small but very ancient
sect called the Universalists, which holds
that everybody goes to Heaven. A lot of
people feel that way without knowing that
they are Universalists. That is one reason
why the Kaiser may not be hanged. People
hate the idea of hurrying him to Heaven.

■How to secure early
delivery of your post-war
SUNBEAM
The demand for Sunbeam cars for
delivery when we are again able
to attend to private requirements
is, already such that early ship-

Business, Robbery, etc.
“0.L.” : Furious protests have, lately been
made by cheese-manufacturers and others in
Victoria because the rennet they have bought
is poor stuff, forming acid curd without any
whey worth mentioning. It’s just another
case of the manufacturer not being satisfied
with the local product. The other morning
Araminta uttered a loud wail from the kit-
chen and called me in from the potato-patch
to look at the wreck of a “junket.” Aramin-
ta had tried to make the dish with the aid
of a new sort of rennet-tablet, with dis-
gusting results. I found that the tube was
marked ‘Made in Sweden,” and suggested
that she should try to get a local make of
tablet. Next day Araminta brought home a
tube of Melbourne-made rennet-tablets,
which turned out as gotid a “junket” as I
ever tasted —not that “junket” at its best is
much of a food to chew on, of course. Still,
the Australian goods came right up to the
scratch, whereas the Swedish rennet had
simply turned the milk into slimy pig’s-feed.
Being patriotic and saving money at the
same time looks easy and sensible, yet there
are lots of people who don’t seem able to
see things that way.
* * * *
“0.K.”: From a sick bed in Victoria I
arise to point the finger of scorn and com-
plaint at the unsatisfactory brewers of this
thirsty country as regards beer. I’m one
of the devils who are sinners only when
they are sick, and prefer to be saints when
they are well. We want light lager-beer
for appetising purposes when pain and
suffering wring the brow —light lager of the
sort that used to be imported before the
war. And we can’t get it. The local
brewers had four years in which to discover
the right place, with the right climate and
water supply, for the manufacture of the
mildest possible brand of beer. Does the
blessed brewers’ combine give the public a
chance to get the want of its invalids sup-
plied locally? Why, a country brewery is
required to limit its output to a small
neighboring district! There is no en-
couragement for brewers in the most likely
places to strive at converting a local water
into pure and innocent beer suitable for
the sickest sinners. Australia may soon
arrive at the secret of glorious develop-
ments in sparkling wine-making, but the
day when a certain district shall be identi-
fied with the palest of sparkling beer seems
still far off.
* * * *
“Quidam”: Our local stores sell candles
weighing 12oz. to the package for Is., while
candles of similar or better quality weighing
16oz. are priced at Is. Id. A large section
of the public, considering only price, buys
the Is. variety, which is also served, as a
rule, to anyone who simply asks for a
packet of candles. Yet there is no question
as to which is the cheaper; 12oz. of candle
at Is. = Id. per oz., while 16oz. for Is. Id.
means that the purchaser is getting the
extra 4oz. for Id. If 16oz. were pre-
scribed as the weight of a packet of candles,
anyone would see at a glance which of the
two brands was the cheaper, and any genius
who found a way of producing a slightly
cheaper packet would gain a proper reward.
At present the person who cuts weight
without equivalently cutting price gets in
ahead every time.
* * * *
“Leo Lear” : I lately received notice from
the Bolsheviks who hold a mortgage over
the family clover that the said loan would
not be renewed if stinkwort were found on the
premises at re-valuation time. This suggests
a way to keep at-present clean land .free
from worts, briars, pear, burrs and other
pests. If no mortgage were renewed on land
that had become infested during the term
of the deed, owners would scurry a bit to
keep clean land clean. Any area of reason-
able size can only become a pest nursery
through the carelessness or laziness of the
owner. That, of course, does not apply to
the huge outback lease areas, half of which
a man never sees for years on end.
* * * *
The big co-operative butter factories of
Victoria’s Western District are threatened
with extinction. Strawberry still yields the
usual quantity of juice, but when the authori-
ties fixed the price of butter—and conse-
quently the price the factories could afford
to pay for milk —they omitted to fix the
price of condensed milk. So the Nestle
firm, which bought out the Farnham factory,
offers 2d. per lb. of butterfat more than the
factories, and the fool farmer, forgetting that
he is simultaneously ruining the factory in
which he is a shareholder, rushes his cow-
juice to one of the myriad Nestle receiving
stations. Any co-operator who can’t see
further ahead than that deserves to hit a
snag.
* * * *
“F.J.”: Sometimes it is the Australian
timber man’s own fault that business goes
by. While enlarging his plant a Melbourne
woolscourer lately stipulated for Australian
hardwood. A Victorian lumber crowd pro-
mised to deliver the material within six days.
The contractor gave them 15 days, to make
sure. The firm’s patience lasted for five weeks ;
then the boss cancelled the order and sent
for American stuff. This was delivered at
the works next morniilg. The American
wood was grown eight or nine thousand
miles away: the Australian had to come
only about 30 miles.
INDIRECT ACTION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“In my diocese there are 20,000,000 people. Our
operation's are in the hands of British, American and
Canadian ministers chiefly. I should like to see Aus-
tralia represented to a greater degree. I am not
making any appeal here, but if God sends me any
money I shall be very glad.’’
—Bishop Banister, of Kwangsi and Hunan.
“I am not here to cadge for coin,”
The Bishop told the press.
“But, all the same, if some should try
To force their lucre on me, I
Will take it, none the less.
“I will receive whatever God
May send me without stint.
Please give this fact publicity;
It won’t be my fault then if He
Should fail to take the hint!”
VICTORIA.
H. O.
