A Ballad of Judgment.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IT was the judgment horn of God;
The green world heard it blow.
An angel with a flaming rod
Strode on the clouds of woe
To shake the graves and try the souls
That there had lived and died ;
And great winds bore the judgment scrolls
At that tall angel’s side.
Oh, they should be as standing corn,
And they should rise as reeds,
For every man of woman born
Should" answer for his deeds.
>*«■ Break loose, the mould that holds the
germ,
For bone with bone must wed !
Let go, let go, the creeping worm!
Let up the living dead!
It was the judgment horn of God;
Yet none thereat awoke,
No moving dust below the sod
Its shroud of silence broke.
Rise up, rise up, ye sleeping souls,
And answer high and fair;
For wide are set the judgment scrolls
And every name is there.
The grass was simpering to the wind
That tossed a seedy spray,
But never soul of man would mind
The calling of that day ;
For once it blew and thrice it blew
Above the green wind waves,
And still the creeping grass-roots grew
Damp-down into the graves.
It was the judgment horn of God
That pealed the seventh blast;
The four winds bore the sound abroad — •
And will they rise at last?
Uprose the storm that shakes the south
Up spake the east and west;
The north, with thunder in its mouth,
Tossed high a spumy crest.
But never grave gave up its dead.
Oh, strange it was to hear
God’s pealing voice roll overhead
. That could not make them fear.
But up from east and south and west
There came a rustling sound,
Strange paper moving from its rest
The wide dead earth around.
Up sprang the books of all the world,
Mind-written with a pen ;
Like leaves before a tempest hurled
Grew thick the thoughts of men.
They stood for judgment page on page
Below the wild red sky,
Age pressing hard on toiling age—
The things that could not die.
Afid some there were with trembling
leaves,
But all were open wide—
The earth’s last garnered harvest sheaves
That judgment must divide.
The deep sky looked upon them all,
The graves were very still;
And through the hush a music small
And faint began to spill.
A music small, a growing sound,
A melody of tears,
That rose to harmony around
The place of all dead years.
It swelled, it grew, it filled the world,
t And through the doom-lights strong
Vast flags of glory were unfurled
To match that ageless song.
The little tilings that God had made
To keep man’s soul from death
Flamed into light that could not fade,
Fanned by that holy breath.
~ And up the way of star and sun
'- 1 It swept in triumph high.
The man-song God had made and won
Lest God Himself should die.
The angels prayed, the great winds fell;
The creeping worm let go.
The slow earth stirred; and rapture fell
On those who dreamed below.
Oh, up they rose as standing corn,
And up they stood as reeds;
For every man of woman born
Had answered for his deeds!
Of wrongs and shames and sins and woe
The large account was dead,
Burned in the sky’s vast furnace-glow
Of glory overhead ;
And God among His angels wrote,
In white fire sweet and strong,
That all the stars of Heaven might note
The pardon of the song.
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.
The Affair.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MR. GREGORY WICKS, R.N., of
H.M.S. Emu, having spent a busy
week incidental to the proper
charge of the functions appertaining to the
““position of first lieutenant of the vessel of
war acting as guard-ship, for the time be-
ing, at Port Jackson, came ashore on a
Saturday afternoon in May, 1816, with
every intention of seeking diversion. He
felt that onerous duties faithfully carried
out entitled him to an expectation of those
measures of relaxation and recreation which,
even if there might be some persons
in the Settlement sour-minded enough to re-
gard them as dissipation, he had unques-
tionably earned.
Accordingly, in this holiday frame of
mind, he bent his steps—“shaped a course,”
he would have put it —towards the Bar-
racks. There, he would seek out the friend
of. his bosom. Ensign Patrick Calder, of the
46th, and afford him an opportunity of shar-
ing in those diversions, innocent or other-
-4 wise, which he contemplated with a certain
vague enjoyment of anticipation.
Passing up George-street from the Cove
he encountered the smiling and genial per-
sonality of Captain William Blake, the
acjutant of his friend’s regiment. Captain
Blake was on his way to the North Shore
to shoot gillbirds. He hailed the young
naval officer with delight.
“Bless my soul, Pansy, my boy” (he used
Mr. Wicks’s well-known nickname), tis
ages since we’ve seen ye at the mess. What
have ye been doing w r ith yourself t ’
“Oh, nothing much, Mr. Blake. The Cap-
tain’s been ill ashore, and I’ve been tied to
the ship. That’s all. Is Patrick in bar-
rßcks
The adjutant’s face fell immediately and
became grave and serious in a fashion that
was comic because it was so obviously the
unreal aspect of his countenance. He affected
to heave a sigh.
“Pansy, my lad, ’tis very distressing. Poor
Pat Calder! I fear he’s done for. Poor
Patrick
“Why, what’s the matter with him, sir?
He was all right a night or so ago, wdien I
saw him last.”
Mr. Wicks was a little mystified by the
adjutant’s seriousness and a little dubious
of the twinkle in his blue eyes.
“Come into Tim Naylan’s with me, Pansy,
and I’ll tell ye,” said Blake. “We’re almost
at the door of the Spotted Dog. We’ll
crack a bottle and I’ll tell ye all about the
>Mr U Wicks retraced his steps a few yards
and the two of them entered the low door
of the dingy tavern of which the red-headed
Timothy Naylan was the licensee about
those years. It was a favorite resort of
himself and Mr. Calder, and of other char-
acters even less reputable than those two
bright young officers. They passed through
the dark and empty tap-room and entered
an apartment at the back, whose little win-
dow opened on to an untidy yard. Captain
Blake banged the butt of his gun on the
table and presently the shock head of Mr.
Naylan thi-ust itself into the space between
the half-open door and its jamb.
“Good afternoon, y’r honors both,” it
said; “an’ what might I be after doin’ for
yez?”
“A bottle of Madeira, Tim,” said Blake,
“and see ’tis not corked.”
“I’ll look to it mesilf, Captain,” said their
host, hurrying away. 1
The two officers sat down at the greasy
table, and when Mr. Naylan had provided
bottle and glasses and they had solemnly
toasted one another, the adjutant enlight-
ened Mr. Wicks concerning his friend.
“Well, Pansy, my boy, the trouble with
our friend Patrick is that he’s bewitched.”
“Bewitched !”
“Yes; lie’s taken leave of his senses.
When I tell you that not for more than a
week past has he been late on parade, ye’ll
understand that there’s something the mat-
ter with him. But when I tell you also that
not for quite as long a time have I had to
exhort him to mend his ways in any respect
whatsoever, simply because they are all that
the ways of a young officer should be, ye’ll
come to the same conclusion that I’ve come
to —that there’s something seriously the
matter with him. He’s not himself. But,
my dear Wicks, when I tell you, further,
that Colonel Molle has himself commented
to me on his extraordinarily good behaviour
ye’ll feel as alarmed as we all feel for the
welfare of Mr. Patrick Calder. Don’t laugh.
I’m really serious about it. All these symp-
toms point to something very much amiss—
and-—l—know—what—it—is,” he concluded
impressively.
Mr. Wicks’s sunburnt face took on an
anxious expression as he stared in bewilder-
ment across the table at the other. He did
not speak for a moment or two. The pro-
blem of his friend’s changed behaviour was
inexplicable to him. When he did speak
there was grave anxiety in his tone, as well
as in his face.
“ ’Tis not like Patrick,” he said at last,
slowly, and shaking his head. “Damme if
it is. He must be ill—or he’s got religion.
What d’ye know of it, sir?”
Captain Blake emptied his glass, replen-
ished it—as well as that of Mr. Wicks —
winked solemnly three times, and said, seri-
ously, “Pat Calder is involved in an Affair!”
Mr. Wicks smiled with relief, as if this
information Avere not so bad as some he had
been expecting.
“Oh, that’s all, is it?” he said. “Wejl, a
fellow’s got to expect something of that
sort if he mixes in shindies like Pat does.
Who’s he fightin’?” He seemed immensely
relieved.
But Captain Blake shook his head.
“No, no,” he said, with some mournful-
ness. “It’s not nearly so simple as that,
Pansy, my buck. “ ’Tis not a duel —an
affair of honor. ’Tis an affair of the heart.”
“The devil it is!” said Mr. Wicks. “Well,
but he’s had ’em before, hasn’t he? Several
I know of. What’s there in a bit of an
adventure with a petticoat to make him act
sc; strangely? Surely it must be more than
that.”
“Oh. but this is the very devil of an
affair,” said Captain Blake. “ ’Tis with Mrs.
Moisey. Now, what do you say to that?”
Mr. Wicks whistled, tilting his chair
back, and regarding his companion with a
look that w*as blended of astonishment,
apprehension and exasperation.
“By God!” he cried. “With her —at
Government House. O Lord! Lord save
the cat!” he exclaimed piously.
“Now listen to me, Pansy.” said Captain
Blake earnestly, after tossing off the last
of his wine. “You’re bis closest friend. ’Tis
your part to think of some means of rescue.
I leave it to you. Go see him in his quar-
ters and laugh him out of it. Do anything.
Promise me ye’ll do your best, won’t ye?”
“Indeed I will, sir. But ye know Pat
Calder!”
“I do —and I know you. Make him ridicu-
lous with the lady. ’Tis the only way of
saving him. I trust in you. Now, I must
be going—the boatman’s waiting for me.
Get you along to the barracks and see what
he’s about. Spy out the lay of the land.
Bring things about so that she’ll laugh at
him. ’Tis what he can’t stand, the young
rip! The regiment depends on you, Pansy.”
“I’ll do my best, sir.” said Mr. Wicks,
with some misgiving. “I’ll do what I can.”
In the verandah of Mr. Calder’s quarters
Mr. Wicks found that gentleman’s servant,
Private Michael Muldoon, busily engaged in
polishing a pair of riding-boots with yellow
tops. He was doing this with such extreme
care—extending his boot-clad arm from time
to time to survey the. shininess he was in-

not heal; to make matters worse, she knocked it again, opening it up a second time
and making a very bad place of it indeed.

ducing in the leather, spitting upon the
brush in his other hand, and delicately add-
ing to the lustre in unsatisfactory places—
that he did not observe Mr. Wicks until the
latter spoke. When he heard his voice he
sprang to quick attention, boot and brush
beside each leg.
“Good afternoon, Michael. Mr. Calder
in?”
Private Muldoon replied in a confidential
whisper—seriously, solemnly, and a little
mournfully. _ ,
He is, thin, yr honor; but ann yr
honoi s paidon, X m not thinkin ho II bo
wishful for to see ye.
“See me? Of course he will. Tell me,
Michael, is this business as serious as Cap-
tain Blake’s jusrt been tellin me?” He
spoke in a low tone, confidentially also.
“ ’Tis so, thin, y’r honor. lie’s a-dressin’
for to go ridin’ wid th’ lady now. .’Tis
worse, much worse. I dunno what’s come
over him. He’ll bite y’r head off if ye go
nigh him. ’Tis but jist now he t’reatens me
wid Ord’ly-room for not havin’ enough shine
on these boots, an’ I swear ye cud. see y’r
face in ’em.”
“Good Lord! Well, Mike, I’ll see what I
can do with him.”
“Good luck go wid ye, sorr,” whispered
Private Muldoon, as Mr. Wicks stepped
across the verandah.
Mr. Wicks did not remain long inside.
Presently Private Muldoon heard the voice
of his master raised in peevish and angry
tones.
“Oh, go to Hell, Pansy. I tell you I
can’t go with you. I’ve an engagement.
Never you mind who with. ’Tis not your
affair. I’ll do as I d—d well please.”
And Mr. Wicks came forth with a flushed
face and the light of battle in his eye, obvi-
on sly a little angry. But by the grin that
came over his countenance he was also a
little amused, and bent on some scheme of
bis own, thought Private Muldoon.
Mr. Wicks walked down to the barrack
gates and passed out into George-street.
Here lie paused for a few moments as if in
deep thought, slapped himself on the thigh,
threw up Ins head with a laugh and strode
o to w "* r d s the Brickfields. .... ...
By George, yes,” he muttered to himself.
I 11 do it. She 11 _ fix me up. _ 111 cook
Patsy’s goose for him—the villain!”
Presentlv Ensign Patrick Calder returned
the sentry’s salute, as he, too, passed the
guard-room by the gates. He was resplen-
dent in his very best fawn-colored riding-
suit, a beaver hat, a black silk stock and
the highly polished boots which Muldoon had
been operating upon.
“Th’ young devil must be a-goin’ to get
married,” remarked the sergeant of the
guard to his corporal, when they beheld him
through the open doorway of the guard-
room, as the resplendent youth passed by.
He went along the main thoroughfare of
Sydney towards the Cove, and turned into
Hunter-street. A little way down the hill
he came to the open gateway of a stable-
yard and went in. When he emerged he was
mounted upon the best bay hackney that
Mr. Thaddeus Driscoll, the well-known
livery-man, could provide. From here, a
magnificent figure, he rode at a dignified
walk towards Government House, over on
the further hillside, across the bridge that
spanned the Tank Stream. A groom was
leading a horse from the vice-regal stables
up and down the gravelled drive before the
verandah, and on the latter, in conversation
with his Excellency, was the lady who was
the cause of so much perturbation to Mr.
Calder’s more intimate friends.
Mrs. Moisey was a lady of that ambigu-
ous age that is just over or just under thirty.
She was a schoolgirl friend of Mrs. Mac-
quarie’s, and a widow, and sometimes —to-
day, for instance —she did not look any more
than twenty-four. She was dark, and pretty,
and vivacious, and had red lips that were
never meant to be long without kisses. After
the late Colonel .Moisey’s death she had had
an illness, and the doctors had recommended
a long sea voyage. So she had taken the
longest one that it was possible to take —
from Gravesend to Sydney —and had now
been a guest at Government House for close
upon three months.
“Ah, here you are!” she laughed to Mr.
Calder, as he handed his reins to the groom,
and bowed to his Excellency and the lady.
“Never tell me, dear Mr. Calder, ’tis we
women who have to be waited for. La, Mr.
Macquarie, have I not been waiting for Mr.
Calder this ten minutes past?”
She had a jolly voice, and a twinkling,
laughing eye, and his Excellency was moved
to gallantry by them.
“My dear Teresa,” he said, “I believe it
is the first time I have ever congratulated
Mr. Calder upon his remissness. His delay
has been my good fortune. I surrender you
to him grudgingly—most grudgingly, I assure
you.”
It seemed to Mr. Calder that the great
man regarded him with a more favorable eye
than usual. That might easily have been
the case. Before they rode off, to his
astonishment, his Excellency drew him aside
and remarked—a little drily :
“Mr. Calder, I am delighted at last to hear
a good report of you from Colonel Molle. I
trust that you will retain his favorable
opinion. There was room for improvement,
sir, as I think I have mentioned to you once
or twice.”
“Thank you, your Excellency. I hope to
be able to retain the respect of my superiors.
It will not be for want of effort on my part,
I assure you, sir.”
The Governor assisted Mrs. Moisey into
the saddle.
“Take great care of her, Calder,” he called
after them, as they rode away. “Mrs. Mac-
quarie and I hold you responsible for her
safety, in every way.”
“Oh, shut up!” murmured Mr. Calder, so’
that only the lady heard him. She was con-
vulsed with stifled merriment.
They rode out into Macquarie-street, and
southward by the new Rum Hospital, towards
the racecourse —where Hyde Park is to-day.
Then they skirted the back of Mr. Palmer’s
farm of Wulamulla, crossed the South Head-
road, and so into the sandhills which some
queer stretch of imagination had likened to
the hills of Surrey. It was a wild kind of
No Man’s Land in those days, covered with
scrubby growths, and fragrant with the aro-
matic smell of evergreen shrubs and eucalyp-
tus. When they came to an eminence
whence they could' see the far-off blue gleam
of Botany Bay and the distant mountains
of Illawarra, Mrs. Moisey proposed that
they should dismount and rest, whilst they
enjoyed the wide and beautiful view that lay
to the southward.
This Patrick Calder considered to be. a
most commendable scheme. It would give
him an opportunity of declaring his passion,
which was the usual very fervent passion of
a youth of twenty-one.
He began immediately. With great bold-
ness he seized the charming widow’s hand,
raised it to his lips, and kissed it with fer-
vor. The charming widow made but a half-
hearted objection.
“Oh. Mv Calder,” she murmured, “you
mustn’t. ’Tis —’tis dereglcment. I cannot
permit—oh, oh”
He had kissed her lips. She tore herself
from him, and retreated a pace or two.
“How dare you, sir! I shall inform Mr.
Macquarie. Oh, the idea! You are a bold
young man, sir.”
But a little smile dimpled her pretty face,
and the rosy blushes of a confusion that
was half gratification made her, in Mr.
Calder’s view, sweetly lovely and altogether
charming. The infatuated boy fell upon his
knees —that sort of thing was done in those
days—and poured forth his soul.
“Teresa, Teresa,” he raved. “Teresa, how
I adore you ! With all my soul, with
all my being, with my whole heart. Say
that you return my affection —oh, give me
some hope, Teresa. Stay—let me clasp you
to this bosom” —: —-
He scrambled to his feet, and she retreated,
a little hand outstretched, as though to fend
off his impetuous advances. Mrs. Moisey
was perhaps a little alarmed —though
enjoying herself immensely—for she was
not altogether sure that she could
trust herself. This handsome and ardent
youth, she felt, might try even the
most decoi’ous of young widows. She
reflected that, although she was propriety
itself, she was also young, and she could feel
the hot blood racing through her veins. It
would never do. So she assumed an iciness
of demeanor that did not nearly so well
become her as her blushes and her dimples.
“Mr. Calder, I must beg of you to recol-
lect yourself and to treat me with respect.
I am too lately bereaved” —there were tears
in her eyes, but the sentiment in her heart
did not concern itself with the late Colonel
Moisey—“to consider such advances. Pray
restrain yourself and assist me into the
saddle. Look, the sun is setting. It will
be dark before we reach the town. I beg
you to remember, sir, that I am an unpro-
tected female, and to desist from such be-
havior!”
This chilling little speech cooled the hot
recklessness of Mr. Calder’s passion. Very
respectfully, and with many sighs, he assisted
her to mount, and they rode homeward in
silence.
Just at dusk they came to the outskirts of
the town, behind the Racecourse. Mrs.
Moisey had gallantly striven to make conver-
sation, but the unhappy subaltern was glum
and morose —heartbroken, he expressed it to
himself —and the latter portion of their ride
was made in a silence that was only broken
by the clatter of the horses’ hooves against
loose stones, and the cries of homing birds.
But as they drew near to the infrequent
twinkling lights of the suburbs of little Syd-
ney, she edged her horse close to his and
half whispered:
“Do you really love me, Patrick? How
can I tell that you are not playing with me?
You have such a sad reputation, you know!
Mr. Macquarie did not half like my riding
with you, alone, to-day. Now, do you really
think you care a lit”
“Oh, Patrick —is it so you treat your
loving Emma ! Oh, darling, baby’s so sick!
Won’t you come to him? Oh, I beg you,
Patrick, if you ever loved me”
“Great God !” exclaimed Mr Calder.
Mrs. Moisey sat up stiffly on her side-
saddle and stared at the woman, who was
tall and heavily veiled. There was a silence,
interrupted only by the woman’s hysterical
sobbing. Presently she turned to Calder.
“Mr. Calder, I demand to know instantly
who this woman is. What is she to you?”
Mr. Calder was smitten with the dumb-
ness of overwhelming astonishment.
“Woman, indeed !” said the figure on the
ground. “I’d have you to know, ma’am, that
I’m Mr. Patrick Calder’s wedded wife, and
I’d like to know what you are doing with
him, a-riding out into the country alone
together ?”
The late Colonel Moisey was a forbearing
creature, but his ghost could have testified
that his relict was a lady with a temper of
a proper spirit. So could Mr. Calder, from
that moment onward.
“Oh, infamous!” she cried. “Oh, you
wretched cad !”
She slashed her escort across his face once
and the next cut of her whip was on her
horse’s flank. She sped off into the darkness
towards Macquarie-street, leaving the petri-
fied Mi\ Calder staring at the sobbing
woman.
“Who are. .. . ” he began angrily, but was
interrupted by a shout of laughter, and
astounded to see the veiled lady gather up
her skirts and run.
“Pansy I’ 1 he roared and galloped in pur-
suit.
The skirts hindered his quarry, and, before
long, any who passed that way might have
been edified by the sight of an infuriated
subaltern of the 46th tearing the clothing
from a woman who yelled in manly tones
for mercy from the blows she was receiving.
A week later, Lieutenant Gregory Wicks.
R.N. —with a black eye—met by appoint-
ment, at Timothy Naylan’s Spotted Dog,
Ensign Patrick Calder, of the 46th —also
with a black eye. They drank and vowed
eternal friendship. But there was no allu-
sion to what Mr. Wicks always referred to.
in conversation with Captain Blake, as
“The Affair.”
J. H. M. ABBOTT.
